0066: 
0067: Letter 1
0068: 
0069: _To Mrs. Saville, England._
0070: 
0071: 
0072: St. Petersburgh, Dec. 11th, 17—.
0073: 
0074: 
0075: You will rejoice to hear that no disaster has accompanied the
0076: commencement of an enterprise which you have regarded with such evil
0077: forebodings. I arrived here yesterday, and my first task is to assure
0078: my dear sister of my welfare and increasing confidence in the success
0079: of my undertaking.
0080: 
0081: I am already far north of London, and as I walk in the streets of
0082: Petersburgh, I feel a cold northern breeze play upon my cheeks, which
0083: braces my nerves and fills me with delight. Do you understand this
0084: feeling? This breeze, which has travelled from the regions towards
0085: which I am advancing, gives me a foretaste of those icy climes.
0086: Inspirited by this wind of promise, my daydreams become more fervent
0087: and vivid. I try in vain to be persuaded that the pole is the seat of
0088: frost and desolation; it ever presents itself to my imagination as the
0089: region of beauty and delight. There, Margaret, the sun is for ever
0090: visible, its broad disk just skirting the horizon and diffusing a
0091: perpetual splendour. There—for with your leave, my sister, I will put
0092: some trust in preceding navigators—there snow and frost are banished;
0093: and, sailing over a calm sea, we may be wafted to a land surpassing in
0094: wonders and in beauty every region hitherto discovered on the habitable
0095: globe. Its productions and features may be without example, as the
0096: phenomena of the heavenly bodies undoubtedly are in those undiscovered
0097: solitudes. What may not be expected in a country of eternal light? I
0098: may there discover the wondrous power which attracts the needle and may
0099: regulate a thousand celestial observations that require only this
0100: voyage to render their seeming eccentricities consistent for ever. I
0101: shall satiate my ardent curiosity with the sight of a part of the world
0102: never before visited, and may tread a land never before imprinted by
0103: the foot of man. These are my enticements, and they are sufficient to
0104: conquer all fear of danger or death and to induce me to commence this
0105: laborious voyage with the joy a child feels when he embarks in a little
0106: boat, with his holiday mates, on an expedition of discovery up his
0107: native river. But supposing all these conjectures to be false, you
0108: cannot contest the inestimable benefit which I shall confer on all
0109: mankind, to the last generation, by discovering a passage near the pole
0110: to those countries, to reach which at present so many months are
0111: requisite; or by ascertaining the secret of the magnet, which, if at
0112: all possible, can only be effected by an undertaking such as mine.
0113: 
0114: These reflections have dispelled the agitation with which I began my
0115: letter, and I feel my heart glow with an enthusiasm which elevates me
0116: to heaven, for nothing contributes so much to tranquillise the mind as
0117: a steady purpose—a point on which the soul may fix its intellectual
0118: eye. This expedition has been the favourite dream of my early years. I
0119: have read with ardour the accounts of the various voyages which have
0120: been made in the prospect of arriving at the North Pacific Ocean
0121: through the seas which surround the pole. You may remember that a
0122: history of all the voyages made for purposes of discovery composed the
0123: whole of our good Uncle Thomas’ library. My education was neglected,
0124: yet I was passionately fond of reading. These volumes were my study
0125: day and night, and my familiarity with them increased that regret which
0126: I had felt, as a child, on learning that my father’s dying injunction
0127: had forbidden my uncle to allow me to embark in a seafaring life.
0128: 
0129: These visions faded when I perused, for the first time, those poets
0130: whose effusions entranced my soul and lifted it to heaven. I also
0131: became a poet and for one year lived in a paradise of my own creation;
0132: I imagined that I also might obtain a niche in the temple where the
0133: names of Homer and Shakespeare are consecrated. You are well
0134: acquainted with my failure and how heavily I bore the disappointment.
0135: But just at that time I inherited the fortune of my cousin, and my
0136: thoughts were turned into the channel of their earlier bent.
0137: 
0138: Six years have passed since I resolved on my present undertaking. I
0139: can, even now, remember the hour from which I dedicated myself to this
0140: great enterprise. I commenced by inuring my body to hardship. I
0141: accompanied the whale-fishers on several expeditions to the North Sea;
0142: I voluntarily endured cold, famine, thirst, and want of sleep; I often
0143: worked harder than the common sailors during the day and devoted my
0144: nights to the study of mathematics, the theory of medicine, and those
0145: branches of physical science from which a naval adventurer might derive
0146: the greatest practical advantage. Twice I actually hired myself as an
0147: under-mate in a Greenland whaler, and acquitted myself to admiration. I
0148: must own I felt a little proud when my captain offered me the second
0149: dignity in the vessel and entreated me to remain with the greatest
0150: earnestness, so valuable did he consider my services.
0151: 
0152: And now, dear Margaret, do I not deserve to accomplish some great purpose?
0153: My life might have been passed in ease and luxury, but I preferred glory to
0154: every enticement that wealth placed in my path. Oh, that some encouraging
0155: voice would answer in the affirmative! My courage and my resolution is
0156: firm; but my hopes fluctuate, and my spirits are often depressed. I am
0157: about to proceed on a long and difficult voyage, the emergencies of which
0158: will demand all my fortitude: I am required not only to raise the spirits
0159: of others, but sometimes to sustain my own, when theirs are failing.
0160: 
0161: This is the most favourable period for travelling in Russia. They fly
0162: quickly over the snow in their sledges; the motion is pleasant, and, in
0163: my opinion, far more agreeable than that of an English stagecoach. The
0164: cold is not excessive, if you are wrapped in furs—a dress which I have
0165: already adopted, for there is a great difference between walking the
0166: deck and remaining seated motionless for hours, when no exercise
0167: prevents the blood from actually freezing in your veins. I have no
0168: ambition to lose my life on the post-road between St. Petersburgh and
0169: Archangel.
0170: 
0171: I shall depart for the latter town in a fortnight or three weeks; and my
0172: intention is to hire a ship there, which can easily be done by paying the
0173: insurance for the owner, and to engage as many sailors as I think necessary
0174: among those who are accustomed to the whale-fishing. I do not intend to
0175: sail until the month of June; and when shall I return? Ah, dear sister, how
0176: can I answer this question? If I succeed, many, many months, perhaps years,
0177: will pass before you and I may meet. If I fail, you will see me again soon,
0178: or never.
0179: 
0180: Farewell, my dear, excellent Margaret. Heaven shower down blessings on you,
0181: and save me, that I may again and again testify my gratitude for all your
0182: love and kindness.
0183: 
0184: Your affectionate brother,
0185: 
0186: R. Walton
0187: 
0188: 
0189: 
0190: 
0191: Letter 2
0192: 
0193: _To Mrs. Saville, England._
0194: 
0195: Archangel, 28th March, 17—.
0196: 
0197: 
0198: How slowly the time passes here, encompassed as I am by frost and snow!
0199: Yet a second step is taken towards my enterprise. I have hired a
0200: vessel and am occupied in collecting my sailors; those whom I have
0201: already engaged appear to be men on whom I can depend and are certainly
0202: possessed of dauntless courage.
0203: 
0204: But I have one want which I have never yet been able to satisfy, and the
0205: absence of the object of which I now feel as a most severe evil, I have no
0206: friend, Margaret: when I am glowing with the enthusiasm of success, there
0207: will be none to participate my joy; if I am assailed by disappointment, no
0208: one will endeavour to sustain me in dejection. I shall commit my thoughts
0209: to paper, it is true; but that is a poor medium for the communication of
0210: feeling. I desire the company of a man who could sympathise with me, whose
0211: eyes would reply to mine. You may deem me romantic, my dear sister, but I
0212: bitterly feel the want of a friend. I have no one near me, gentle yet
0213: courageous, possessed of a cultivated as well as of a capacious mind, whose
0214: tastes are like my own, to approve or amend my plans. How would such a
0215: friend repair the faults of your poor brother! I am too ardent in execution
0216: and too impatient of difficulties. But it is a still greater evil to me
0217: that I am self-educated: for the first fourteen years of my life I ran wild
0218: on a common and read nothing but our Uncle Thomas’ books of voyages.
0219: At that age I became acquainted with the celebrated poets of our own
0220: country; but it was only when it had ceased to be in my power to derive its
0221: most important benefits from such a conviction that I perceived the
0222: necessity of becoming acquainted with more languages than that of my native
0223: country. Now I am twenty-eight and am in reality more illiterate than many
0224: schoolboys of fifteen. It is true that I have thought more and that my
0225: daydreams are more extended and magnificent, but they want (as the painters
0226: call it) _keeping;_ and I greatly need a friend who would have sense
0227: enough not to despise me as romantic, and affection enough for me to
0228: endeavour to regulate my mind.
0229: 
0230: Well, these are useless complaints; I shall certainly find no friend on the
0231: wide ocean, nor even here in Archangel, among merchants and seamen. Yet
0232: some feelings, unallied to the dross of human nature, beat even in these
0233: rugged bosoms. My lieutenant, for instance, is a man of wonderful courage
0234: and enterprise; he is madly desirous of glory, or rather, to word my phrase
0235: more characteristically, of advancement in his profession. He is an
0236: Englishman, and in the midst of national and professional prejudices,
0237: unsoftened by cultivation, retains some of the noblest endowments of
0238: humanity. I first became acquainted with him on board a whale vessel;
0239: finding that he was unemployed in this city, I easily engaged him to assist
0240: in my enterprise.
0241: 
0242: The master is a person of an excellent disposition and is remarkable in the
0243: ship for his gentleness and the mildness of his discipline. This
0244: circumstance, added to his well-known integrity and dauntless courage, made
0245: me very desirous to engage him. A youth passed in solitude, my best years
0246: spent under your gentle and feminine fosterage, has so refined the
0247: groundwork of my character that I cannot overcome an intense distaste to
0248: the usual brutality exercised on board ship: I have never believed it to be
0249: necessary, and when I heard of a mariner equally noted for his kindliness
0250: of heart and the respect and obedience paid to him by his crew, I felt
0251: myself peculiarly fortunate in being able to secure his services. I heard
0252: of him first in rather a romantic manner, from a lady who owes to him the
0253: happiness of her life. This, briefly, is his story. Some years ago he loved
0254: a young Russian lady of moderate fortune, and having amassed a considerable
0255: sum in prize-money, the father of the girl consented to the match. He saw
0256: his mistress once before the destined ceremony; but she was bathed in
0257: tears, and throwing herself at his feet, entreated him to spare her,
0258: confessing at the same time that she loved another, but that he was poor,
0259: and that her father would never consent to the union. My generous friend
0260: reassured the suppliant, and on being informed of the name of her lover,
0261: instantly abandoned his pursuit. He had already bought a farm with his
0262: money, on which he had designed to pass the remainder of his life; but he
0263: bestowed the whole on his rival, together with the remains of his
0264: prize-money to purchase stock, and then himself solicited the young
0265: woman’s father to consent to her marriage with her lover. But the old
0266: man decidedly refused, thinking himself bound in honour to my friend, who,
0267: when he found the father inexorable, quitted his country, nor returned
0268: until he heard that his former mistress was married according to her
0269: inclinations. “What a noble fellow!” you will exclaim. He is
0270: so; but then he is wholly uneducated: he is as silent as a Turk, and a kind
0271: of ignorant carelessness attends him, which, while it renders his conduct
0272: the more astonishing, detracts from the interest and sympathy which
0273: otherwise he would command.
0274: 
0275: Yet do not suppose, because I complain a little or because I can
0276: conceive a consolation for my toils which I may never know, that I am
0277: wavering in my resolutions. Those are as fixed as fate, and my voyage
0278: is only now delayed until the weather shall permit my embarkation. The
0279: winter has been dreadfully severe, but the spring promises well, and it
0280: is considered as a remarkably early season, so that perhaps I may sail
0281: sooner than I expected. I shall do nothing rashly: you know me
0282: sufficiently to confide in my prudence and considerateness whenever the
0283: safety of others is committed to my care.
0284: 
0285: I cannot describe to you my sensations on the near prospect of my
0286: undertaking. It is impossible to communicate to you a conception of
0287: the trembling sensation, half pleasurable and half fearful, with which
0288: I am preparing to depart. I am going to unexplored regions, to “the
0289: land of mist and snow,” but I shall kill no albatross; therefore do not
0290: be alarmed for my safety or if I should come back to you as worn and
0291: woeful as the “Ancient Mariner.” You will smile at my allusion, but I
0292: will disclose a secret. I have often attributed my attachment to, my
0293: passionate enthusiasm for, the dangerous mysteries of ocean to that
0294: production of the most imaginative of modern poets. There is something
0295: at work in my soul which I do not understand. I am practically
0296: industrious—painstaking, a workman to execute with perseverance and
0297: labour—but besides this there is a love for the marvellous, a belief
0298: in the marvellous, intertwined in all my projects, which hurries me out
0299: of the common pathways of men, even to the wild sea and unvisited
0300: regions I am about to explore.
0301: 
0302: But to return to dearer considerations. Shall I meet you again, after
0303: having traversed immense seas, and returned by the most southern cape of
0304: Africa or America? I dare not expect such success, yet I cannot bear to
0305: look on the reverse of the picture. Continue for the present to write to
0306: me by every opportunity: I may receive your letters on some occasions when
0307: I need them most to support my spirits. I love you very tenderly. 
0308: Remember me with affection, should you never hear from me again.
0309: 
0310: Your affectionate brother,
0311:  Robert Walton
0312: 
0313: 
0314: 
0315: 
0316: Letter 3
0317: 
0318: _To Mrs. Saville, England._
0319: 
0320: July 7th, 17—.
0321: 
0322: 
0323: My dear Sister,
0324: 
0325: I write a few lines in haste to say that I am safe—and well advanced
0326: on my voyage. This letter will reach England by a merchantman now on
0327: its homeward voyage from Archangel; more fortunate than I, who may not
0328: see my native land, perhaps, for many years. I am, however, in good
0329: spirits: my men are bold and apparently firm of purpose, nor do the
0330: floating sheets of ice that continually pass us, indicating the dangers
0331: of the region towards which we are advancing, appear to dismay them. We
0332: have already reached a very high latitude; but it is the height of
0333: summer, and although not so warm as in England, the southern gales,
0334: which blow us speedily towards those shores which I so ardently desire
0335: to attain, breathe a degree of renovating warmth which I had not
0336: expected.
0337: 
0338: No incidents have hitherto befallen us that would make a figure in a
0339: letter. One or two stiff gales and the springing of a leak are
0340: accidents which experienced navigators scarcely remember to record, and
0341: I shall be well content if nothing worse happen to us during our voyage.
0342: 
0343: Adieu, my dear Margaret. Be assured that for my own sake, as well as
0344: yours, I will not rashly encounter danger. I will be cool,
0345: persevering, and prudent.
0346: 
0347: But success _shall_ crown my endeavours. Wherefore not? Thus far I
0348: have gone, tracing a secure way over the pathless seas, the very stars
0349: themselves being witnesses and testimonies of my triumph. Why not
0350: still proceed over the untamed yet obedient element? What can stop the
0351: determined heart and resolved will of man?
0352: 
0353: My swelling heart involuntarily pours itself out thus. But I must
0354: finish. Heaven bless my beloved sister!
0355: 
0356: R.W.
0357: 
0358: 
0359: 
0360: 
0361: Letter 4
0362: 
0363: 
0364: _To Mrs. Saville, England._
0365: 
0366: August 5th, 17—.
0367: 
0368: So strange an accident has happened to us that I cannot forbear
0369: recording it, although it is very probable that you will see me before
0370: these papers can come into your possession.
0371: 
0372: Last Monday (July 31st) we were nearly surrounded by ice, which closed
0373: in the ship on all sides, scarcely leaving her the sea-room in which
0374: she floated. Our situation was somewhat dangerous, especially as we
0375: were compassed round by a very thick fog. We accordingly lay to,
0376: hoping that some change would take place in the atmosphere and weather.
0377: 
0378: About two o’clock the mist cleared away, and we beheld, stretched out
0379: in every direction, vast and irregular plains of ice, which seemed to
0380: have no end. Some of my comrades groaned, and my own mind began to
0381: grow watchful with anxious thoughts, when a strange sight suddenly
0382: attracted our attention and diverted our solicitude from our own
0383: situation. We perceived a low carriage, fixed on a sledge and drawn by
0384: dogs, pass on towards the north, at the distance of half a mile; a
0385: being which had the shape of a man, but apparently of gigantic stature,
0386: sat in the sledge and guided the dogs. We watched the rapid progress
0387: of the traveller with our telescopes until he was lost among the
0388: distant inequalities of the ice.
0389: 
0390: This appearance excited our unqualified wonder. We were, as we believed,
0391: many hundred miles from any land; but this apparition seemed to denote that
0392: it was not, in reality, so distant as we had supposed. Shut in, however, by
0393: ice, it was impossible to follow his track, which we had observed with the
0394: greatest attention.
0395: 
0396: About two hours after this occurrence we heard the ground sea, and before
0397: night the ice broke and freed our ship. We, however, lay to until the
0398: morning, fearing to encounter in the dark those large loose masses which
0399: float about after the breaking up of the ice. I profited of this time to
0400: rest for a few hours.
0401: 
0402: In the morning, however, as soon as it was light, I went upon deck and
0403: found all the sailors busy on one side of the vessel, apparently
0404: talking to someone in the sea. It was, in fact, a sledge, like that we
0405: had seen before, which had drifted towards us in the night on a large
0406: fragment of ice. Only one dog remained alive; but there was a human
0407: being within it whom the sailors were persuading to enter the vessel.
0408: He was not, as the other traveller seemed to be, a savage inhabitant of
0409: some undiscovered island, but a European. When I appeared on deck the
0410: master said, “Here is our captain, and he will not allow you to perish
0411: on the open sea.”
0412: 
0413: On perceiving me, the stranger addressed me in English, although with a
0414: foreign accent. “Before I come on board your vessel,” said he,
0415: “will you have the kindness to inform me whither you are bound?”
0416: 
0417: You may conceive my astonishment on hearing such a question addressed
0418: to me from a man on the brink of destruction and to whom I should have
0419: supposed that my vessel would have been a resource which he would not
0420: have exchanged for the most precious wealth the earth can afford. I
0421: replied, however, that we were on a voyage of discovery towards the
0422: northern pole.
0423: 
0424: Upon hearing this he appeared satisfied and consented to come on board.
0425: Good God! Margaret, if you had seen the man who thus capitulated for
0426: his safety, your surprise would have been boundless. His limbs were
0427: nearly frozen, and his body dreadfully emaciated by fatigue and
0428: suffering. I never saw a man in so wretched a condition. We attempted
0429: to carry him into the cabin, but as soon as he had quitted the fresh
0430: air he fainted. We accordingly brought him back to the deck and
0431: restored him to animation by rubbing him with brandy and forcing him to
0432: swallow a small quantity. As soon as he showed signs of life we
0433: wrapped him up in blankets and placed him near the chimney of the
0434: kitchen stove. By slow degrees he recovered and ate a little soup,
0435: which restored him wonderfully.
0436: 
0437: Two days passed in this manner before he was able to speak, and I often
0438: feared that his sufferings had deprived him of understanding. When he
0439: had in some measure recovered, I removed him to my own cabin and
0440: attended on him as much as my duty would permit. I never saw a more
0441: interesting creature: his eyes have generally an expression of
0442: wildness, and even madness, but there are moments when, if anyone
0443: performs an act of kindness towards him or does him any the most
0444: trifling service, his whole countenance is lighted up, as it were, with
0445: a beam of benevolence and sweetness that I never saw equalled. But he
0446: is generally melancholy and despairing, and sometimes he gnashes his
0447: teeth, as if impatient of the weight of woes that oppresses him.
0448: 
0449: When my guest was a little recovered I had great trouble to keep off
0450: the men, who wished to ask him a thousand questions; but I would not
0451: allow him to be tormented by their idle curiosity, in a state of body
0452: and mind whose restoration evidently depended upon entire repose.
0453: Once, however, the lieutenant asked why he had come so far upon the ice
0454: in so strange a vehicle.
0455: 
0456: His countenance instantly assumed an aspect of the deepest gloom, and
0457: he replied, “To seek one who fled from me.”
0458: 
0459: “And did the man whom you pursued travel in the same fashion?”
0460: 
0461: “Yes.”
0462: 
0463: “Then I fancy we have seen him, for the day before we picked you up we
0464: saw some dogs drawing a sledge, with a man in it, across the ice.”
0465: 
0466: This aroused the stranger’s attention, and he asked a multitude of
0467: questions concerning the route which the dæmon, as he called him, had
0468: pursued. Soon after, when he was alone with me, he said, “I have,
0469: doubtless, excited your curiosity, as well as that of these good
0470: people; but you are too considerate to make inquiries.”
0471: 
0472: “Certainly; it would indeed be very impertinent and inhuman in me to
0473: trouble you with any inquisitiveness of mine.”
0474: 
0475: “And yet you rescued me from a strange and perilous situation; you have
0476: benevolently restored me to life.”
0477: 
0478: Soon after this he inquired if I thought that the breaking up of the
0479: ice had destroyed the other sledge. I replied that I could not answer
0480: with any degree of certainty, for the ice had not broken until near
0481: midnight, and the traveller might have arrived at a place of safety
0482: before that time; but of this I could not judge.
0483: 
0484: From this time a new spirit of life animated the decaying frame of the
0485: stranger. He manifested the greatest eagerness to be upon deck to watch for
0486: the sledge which had before appeared; but I have persuaded him to remain in
0487: the cabin, for he is far too weak to sustain the rawness of the atmosphere.
0488: I have promised that someone should watch for him and give him instant
0489: notice if any new object should appear in sight.
0490: 
0491: Such is my journal of what relates to this strange occurrence up to the
0492: present day. The stranger has gradually improved in health but is very
0493: silent and appears uneasy when anyone except myself enters his cabin.
0494: Yet his manners are so conciliating and gentle that the sailors are all
0495: interested in him, although they have had very little communication
0496: with him. For my own part, I begin to love him as a brother, and his
0497: constant and deep grief fills me with sympathy and compassion. He must
0498: have been a noble creature in his better days, being even now in wreck
0499: so attractive and amiable.
0500: 
0501: I said in one of my letters, my dear Margaret, that I should find no friend
0502: on the wide ocean; yet I have found a man who, before his spirit had been
0503: broken by misery, I should have been happy to have possessed as the brother
0504: of my heart.
0505: 
0506: I shall continue my journal concerning the stranger at intervals,
0507: should I have any fresh incidents to record.
0508: 
0509: 
0510: 
0511: 
0512: August 13th, 17—.
0513: 
0514: 
0515: My affection for my guest increases every day. He excites at once my
0516: admiration and my pity to an astonishing degree. How can I see so
0517: noble a creature destroyed by misery without feeling the most poignant
0518: grief? He is so gentle, yet so wise; his mind is so cultivated, and
0519: when he speaks, although his words are culled with the choicest art,
0520: yet they flow with rapidity and unparalleled eloquence.
0521: 
0522: He is now much recovered from his illness and is continually on the deck,
0523: apparently watching for the sledge that preceded his own. Yet, although
0524: unhappy, he is not so utterly occupied by his own misery but that he
0525: interests himself deeply in the projects of others. He has frequently
0526: conversed with me on mine, which I have communicated to him without
0527: disguise. He entered attentively into all my arguments in favour of my
0528: eventual success and into every minute detail of the measures I had taken
0529: to secure it. I was easily led by the sympathy which he evinced to use the
0530: language of my heart, to give utterance to the burning ardour of my soul
0531: and to say, with all the fervour that warmed me, how gladly I would
0532: sacrifice my fortune, my existence, my every hope, to the furtherance of my
0533: enterprise. One man’s life or death were but a small price to pay for
0534: the acquirement of the knowledge which I sought, for the dominion I should
0535: acquire and transmit over the elemental foes of our race. As I spoke, a
0536: dark gloom spread over my listener’s countenance. At first I
0537: perceived that he tried to suppress his emotion; he placed his hands before
0538: his eyes, and my voice quivered and failed me as I beheld tears trickle
0539: fast from between his fingers; a groan burst from his heaving breast. I
0540: paused; at length he spoke, in broken accents: “Unhappy man! Do you
0541: share my madness? Have you drunk also of the intoxicating draught? Hear me;
0542: let me reveal my tale, and you will dash the cup from your lips!”
0543: 
0544: Such words, you may imagine, strongly excited my curiosity; but the
0545: paroxysm of grief that had seized the stranger overcame his weakened
0546: powers, and many hours of repose and tranquil conversation were
0547: necessary to restore his composure.
0548: 
0549: Having conquered the violence of his feelings, he appeared to despise
0550: himself for being the slave of passion; and quelling the dark tyranny of
0551: despair, he led me again to converse concerning myself personally. He asked
0552: me the history of my earlier years. The tale was quickly told, but it
0553: awakened various trains of reflection. I spoke of my desire of finding a
0554: friend, of my thirst for a more intimate sympathy with a fellow mind than
0555: had ever fallen to my lot, and expressed my conviction that a man could
0556: boast of little happiness who did not enjoy this blessing.
0557: 
0558: “I agree with you,” replied the stranger; “we are
0559: unfashioned creatures, but half made up, if one wiser, better, dearer than
0560: ourselves—such a friend ought to be—do not lend his aid to
0561: perfectionate our weak and faulty natures. I once had a friend, the most
0562: noble of human creatures, and am entitled, therefore, to judge respecting
0563: friendship. You have hope, and the world before you, and have no cause for
0564: despair. But I—I have lost everything and cannot begin life
0565: anew.”
0566: 
0567: As he said this his countenance became expressive of a calm, settled
0568: grief that touched me to the heart. But he was silent and presently
0569: retired to his cabin.
0570: 
0571: Even broken in spirit as he is, no one can feel more deeply than he
0572: does the beauties of nature. The starry sky, the sea, and every sight
0573: afforded by these wonderful regions seem still to have the power of
0574: elevating his soul from earth. Such a man has a double existence: he
0575: may suffer misery and be overwhelmed by disappointments, yet when he
0576: has retired into himself, he will be like a celestial spirit that has a
0577: halo around him, within whose circle no grief or folly ventures.
0578: 
0579: Will you smile at the enthusiasm I express concerning this divine
0580: wanderer? You would not if you saw him. You have been tutored and
0581: refined by books and retirement from the world, and you are therefore
0582: somewhat fastidious; but this only renders you the more fit to
0583: appreciate the extraordinary merits of this wonderful man. Sometimes I
0584: have endeavoured to discover what quality it is which he possesses that
0585: elevates him so immeasurably above any other person I ever knew. I
0586: believe it to be an intuitive discernment, a quick but never-failing
0587: power of judgment, a penetration into the causes of things, unequalled
0588: for clearness and precision; add to this a facility of expression and a
0589: voice whose varied intonations are soul-subduing music.
0590: 
0591: 
0592: 
0593: 
0594: August 19th, 17—.
0595: 
0596: 
0597: Yesterday the stranger said to me, “You may easily perceive, Captain
0598: Walton, that I have suffered great and unparalleled misfortunes. I had
0599: determined at one time that the memory of these evils should die with
0600: me, but you have won me to alter my determination. You seek for
0601: knowledge and wisdom, as I once did; and I ardently hope that the
0602: gratification of your wishes may not be a serpent to sting you, as mine
0603: has been. I do not know that the relation of my disasters will be
0604: useful to you; yet, when I reflect that you are pursuing the same
0605: course, exposing yourself to the same dangers which have rendered me
0606: what I am, I imagine that you may deduce an apt moral from my tale, one
0607: that may direct you if you succeed in your undertaking and console you
0608: in case of failure. Prepare to hear of occurrences which are usually
0609: deemed marvellous. Were we among the tamer scenes of nature I might
0610: fear to encounter your unbelief, perhaps your ridicule; but many things
0611: will appear possible in these wild and mysterious regions which would
0612: provoke the laughter of those unacquainted with the ever-varied powers
0613: of nature; nor can I doubt but that my tale conveys in its series
0614: internal evidence of the truth of the events of which it is composed.”
0615: 
0616: You may easily imagine that I was much gratified by the offered
0617: communication, yet I could not endure that he should renew his grief by
0618: a recital of his misfortunes. I felt the greatest eagerness to hear
0619: the promised narrative, partly from curiosity and partly from a strong
0620: desire to ameliorate his fate if it were in my power. I expressed
0621: these feelings in my answer.
0622: 
0623: “I thank you,” he replied, “for your sympathy, but it is
0624: useless; my fate is nearly fulfilled. I wait but for one event, and then I
0625: shall repose in peace. I understand your feeling,” continued he,
0626: perceiving that I wished to interrupt him; “but you are mistaken, my
0627: friend, if thus you will allow me to name you; nothing can alter my
0628: destiny; listen to my history, and you will perceive how irrevocably it is
0629: determined.”
0630: 
0631: He then told me that he would commence his narrative the next day when I
0632: should be at leisure. This promise drew from me the warmest thanks. I have
0633: resolved every night, when I am not imperatively occupied by my duties, to
0634: record, as nearly as possible in his own words, what he has related during
0635: the day. If I should be engaged, I will at least make notes. This
0636: manuscript will doubtless afford you the greatest pleasure; but to me, who
0637: know him, and who hear it from his own lips—with what interest and
0638: sympathy shall I read it in some future day! Even now, as I commence my
0639: task, his full-toned voice swells in my ears; his lustrous eyes dwell on me
0640: with all their melancholy sweetness; I see his thin hand raised in
0641: animation, while the lineaments of his face are irradiated by the soul
0642: within. Strange and harrowing must be his story, frightful the storm which
0643: embraced the gallant vessel on its course and wrecked it—thus!
0644: 
0645: 
0646: 
0647: 
0648: Chapter 1
0649: 
0650: 
0651: I am by birth a Genevese, and my family is one of the most
0652: distinguished of that republic. My ancestors had been for many years
0653: counsellors and syndics, and my father had filled several public
0654: situations with honour and reputation. He was respected by all who
0655: knew him for his integrity and indefatigable attention to public
0656: business. He passed his younger days perpetually occupied by the
0657: affairs of his country; a variety of circumstances had prevented his
0658: marrying early, nor was it until the decline of life that he became a
0659: husband and the father of a family.
0660: 
0661: As the circumstances of his marriage illustrate his character, I cannot
0662: refrain from relating them. One of his most intimate friends was a
0663: merchant who, from a flourishing state, fell, through numerous
0664: mischances, into poverty. This man, whose name was Beaufort, was of a
0665: proud and unbending disposition and could not bear to live in poverty
0666: and oblivion in the same country where he had formerly been
0667: distinguished for his rank and magnificence. Having paid his debts,
0668: therefore, in the most honourable manner, he retreated with his
0669: daughter to the town of Lucerne, where he lived unknown and in
0670: wretchedness. My father loved Beaufort with the truest friendship and
0671: was deeply grieved by his retreat in these unfortunate circumstances.
0672: He bitterly deplored the false pride which led his friend to a conduct
0673: so little worthy of the affection that united them. He lost no time in
0674: endeavouring to seek him out, with the hope of persuading him to begin
0675: the world again through his credit and assistance.
0676: 
0677: Beaufort had taken effectual measures to conceal himself, and it was ten
0678: months before my father discovered his abode. Overjoyed at this discovery,
0679: he hastened to the house, which was situated in a mean street near the
0680: Reuss. But when he entered, misery and despair alone welcomed him. Beaufort
0681: had saved but a very small sum of money from the wreck of his fortunes, but
0682: it was sufficient to provide him with sustenance for some months, and in
0683: the meantime he hoped to procure some respectable employment in a
0684: merchant’s house. The interval was, consequently, spent in inaction;
0685: his grief only became more deep and rankling when he had leisure for
0686: reflection, and at length it took so fast hold of his mind that at the end
0687: of three months he lay on a bed of sickness, incapable of any exertion.
0688: 
0689: His daughter attended him with the greatest tenderness, but she saw
0690: with despair that their little fund was rapidly decreasing and that
0691: there was no other prospect of support. But Caroline Beaufort
0692: possessed a mind of an uncommon mould, and her courage rose to support
0693: her in her adversity. She procured plain work; she plaited straw and
0694: by various means contrived to earn a pittance scarcely sufficient to
0695: support life.
0696: 
0697: Several months passed in this manner. Her father grew worse; her time
0698: was more entirely occupied in attending him; her means of subsistence
0699: decreased; and in the tenth month her father died in her arms, leaving
0700: her an orphan and a beggar. This last blow overcame her, and she knelt
0701: by Beaufort’s coffin weeping bitterly, when my father entered the
0702: chamber. He came like a protecting spirit to the poor girl, who
0703: committed herself to his care; and after the interment of his friend he
0704: conducted her to Geneva and placed her under the protection of a
0705: relation. Two years after this event Caroline became his wife.
0706: 
0707: There was a considerable difference between the ages of my parents, but
0708: this circumstance seemed to unite them only closer in bonds of devoted
0709: affection. There was a sense of justice in my father’s upright mind
0710: which rendered it necessary that he should approve highly to love
0711: strongly. Perhaps during former years he had suffered from the
0712: late-discovered unworthiness of one beloved and so was disposed to set
0713: a greater value on tried worth. There was a show of gratitude and
0714: worship in his attachment to my mother, differing wholly from the
0715: doting fondness of age, for it was inspired by reverence for her
0716: virtues and a desire to be the means of, in some degree, recompensing
0717: her for the sorrows she had endured, but which gave inexpressible grace
0718: to his behaviour to her. Everything was made to yield to her wishes
0719: and her convenience. He strove to shelter her, as a fair exotic is
0720: sheltered by the gardener, from every rougher wind and to surround her
0721: with all that could tend to excite pleasurable emotion in her soft and
0722: benevolent mind. Her health, and even the tranquillity of her hitherto
0723: constant spirit, had been shaken by what she had gone through. During
0724: the two years that had elapsed previous to their marriage my father had
0725: gradually relinquished all his public functions; and immediately after
0726: their union they sought the pleasant climate of Italy, and the change
0727: of scene and interest attendant on a tour through that land of wonders,
0728: as a restorative for her weakened frame.
0729: 
0730: From Italy they visited Germany and France. I, their eldest child, was born
0731: at Naples, and as an infant accompanied them in their rambles. I remained
0732: for several years their only child. Much as they were attached to each
0733: other, they seemed to draw inexhaustible stores of affection from a very
0734: mine of love to bestow them upon me. My mother’s tender caresses and
0735: my father’s smile of benevolent pleasure while regarding me are my
0736: first recollections. I was their plaything and their idol, and something
0737: better—their child, the innocent and helpless creature bestowed on
0738: them by Heaven, whom to bring up to good, and whose future lot it was in
0739: their hands to direct to happiness or misery, according as they fulfilled
0740: their duties towards me. With this deep consciousness of what they owed
0741: towards the being to which they had given life, added to the active spirit
0742: of tenderness that animated both, it may be imagined that while during
0743: every hour of my infant life I received a lesson of patience, of charity,
0744: and of self-control, I was so guided by a silken cord that all seemed but
0745: one train of enjoyment to me.
0746: 
0747: For a long time I was their only care. My mother had much desired to have a
0748: daughter, but I continued their single offspring. When I was about five
0749: years old, while making an excursion beyond the frontiers of Italy, they
0750: passed a week on the shores of the Lake of Como. Their benevolent
0751: disposition often made them enter the cottages of the poor. This, to my
0752: mother, was more than a duty; it was a necessity, a
0753: passion—remembering what she had suffered, and how she had been
0754: relieved—for her to act in her turn the guardian angel to the
0755: afflicted. During one of their walks a poor cot in the foldings of a vale
0756: attracted their notice as being singularly disconsolate, while the number
0757: of half-clothed children gathered about it spoke of penury in its worst
0758: shape. One day, when my father had gone by himself to Milan, my mother,
0759: accompanied by me, visited this abode. She found a peasant and his wife,
0760: hard working, bent down by care and labour, distributing a scanty meal to
0761: five hungry babes. Among these there was one which attracted my mother far
0762: above all the rest. She appeared of a different stock. The four others were
0763: dark-eyed, hardy little vagrants; this child was thin and very fair. Her
0764: hair was the brightest living gold, and despite the poverty of her
0765: clothing, seemed to set a crown of distinction on her head. Her brow was
0766: clear and ample, her blue eyes cloudless, and her lips and the moulding of
0767: her face so expressive of sensibility and sweetness that none could behold
0768: her without looking on her as of a distinct species, a being heaven-sent,
0769: and bearing a celestial stamp in all her features.
0770: 
0771: The peasant woman, perceiving that my mother fixed eyes of wonder and
0772: admiration on this lovely girl, eagerly communicated her history. She was
0773: not her child, but the daughter of a Milanese nobleman. Her mother was a
0774: German and had died on giving her birth. The infant had been placed with
0775: these good people to nurse: they were better off then. They had not been
0776: long married, and their eldest child was but just born. The father of their
0777: charge was one of those Italians nursed in the memory of the antique glory
0778: of Italy—one among the _schiavi ognor frementi,_ who exerted
0779: himself to obtain the liberty of his country. He became the victim of its
0780: weakness. Whether he had died or still lingered in the dungeons of Austria
0781: was not known. His property was confiscated; his child became an orphan and
0782: a beggar. She continued with her foster parents and bloomed in their rude
0783: abode, fairer than a garden rose among dark-leaved brambles.
0784: 
0785: When my father returned from Milan, he found playing with me in the hall of
0786: our villa a child fairer than pictured cherub—a creature who seemed
0787: to shed radiance from her looks and whose form and motions were lighter
0788: than the chamois of the hills. The apparition was soon explained. With his
0789: permission my mother prevailed on her rustic guardians to yield their
0790: charge to her. They were fond of the sweet orphan. Her presence had seemed
0791: a blessing to them, but it would be unfair to her to keep her in poverty
0792: and want when Providence afforded her such powerful protection. They
0793: consulted their village priest, and the result was that Elizabeth Lavenza
0794: became the inmate of my parents’ house—my more than
0795: sister—the beautiful and adored companion of all my occupations and
0796: my pleasures.
0797: 
0798: Everyone loved Elizabeth. The passionate and almost reverential
0799: attachment with which all regarded her became, while I shared it, my
0800: pride and my delight. On the evening previous to her being brought to
0801: my home, my mother had said playfully, “I have a pretty present for my
0802: Victor—tomorrow he shall have it.” And when, on the morrow, she
0803: presented Elizabeth to me as her promised gift, I, with childish
0804: seriousness, interpreted her words literally and looked upon Elizabeth
0805: as mine—mine to protect, love, and cherish. All praises bestowed on
0806: her I received as made to a possession of my own. We called each other
0807: familiarly by the name of cousin. No word, no expression could body
0808: forth the kind of relation in which she stood to me—my more than
0809: sister, since till death she was to be mine only.
0810: 
0811: 
0812: 
0813: 
0814: Chapter 2
0815: 
0816: 
0817: We were brought up together; there was not quite a year difference in
0818: our ages. I need not say that we were strangers to any species of
0819: disunion or dispute. Harmony was the soul of our companionship, and
0820: the diversity and contrast that subsisted in our characters drew us
0821: nearer together. Elizabeth was of a calmer and more concentrated
0822: disposition; but, with all my ardour, I was capable of a more intense
0823: application and was more deeply smitten with the thirst for knowledge.
0824: She busied herself with following the aerial creations of the poets;
0825: and in the majestic and wondrous scenes which surrounded our Swiss
0826: home —the sublime shapes of the mountains, the changes of the seasons,
0827: tempest and calm, the silence of winter, and the life and turbulence of
0828: our Alpine summers—she found ample scope for admiration and delight.
0829: While my companion contemplated with a serious and satisfied spirit the
0830: magnificent appearances of things, I delighted in investigating their
0831: causes. The world was to me a secret which I desired to divine.
0832: Curiosity, earnest research to learn the hidden laws of nature,
0833: gladness akin to rapture, as they were unfolded to me, are among the
0834: earliest sensations I can remember.
0835: 
0836: On the birth of a second son, my junior by seven years, my parents gave
0837: up entirely their wandering life and fixed themselves in their native
0838: country. We possessed a house in Geneva, and a _campagne_ on Belrive,
0839: the eastern shore of the lake, at the distance of rather more than a
0840: league from the city. We resided principally in the latter, and the
0841: lives of my parents were passed in considerable seclusion. It was my
0842: temper to avoid a crowd and to attach myself fervently to a few. I was
0843: indifferent, therefore, to my school-fellows in general; but I united
0844: myself in the bonds of the closest friendship to one among them. Henry
0845: Clerval was the son of a merchant of Geneva. He was a boy of singular
0846: talent and fancy. He loved enterprise, hardship, and even danger for
0847: its own sake. He was deeply read in books of chivalry and romance. He
0848: composed heroic songs and began to write many a tale of enchantment and
0849: knightly adventure. He tried to make us act plays and to enter into
0850: masquerades, in which the characters were drawn from the heroes of
0851: Roncesvalles, of the Round Table of King Arthur, and the chivalrous
0852: train who shed their blood to redeem the holy sepulchre from the hands
0853: of the infidels.
0854: 
0855: No human being could have passed a happier childhood than myself. My
0856: parents were possessed by the very spirit of kindness and indulgence.
0857: We felt that they were not the tyrants to rule our lot according to
0858: their caprice, but the agents and creators of all the many delights
0859: which we enjoyed. When I mingled with other families I distinctly
0860: discerned how peculiarly fortunate my lot was, and gratitude assisted
0861: the development of filial love.
0862: 
0863: My temper was sometimes violent, and my passions vehement; but by some
0864: law in my temperature they were turned not towards childish pursuits
0865: but to an eager desire to learn, and not to learn all things
0866: indiscriminately. I confess that neither the structure of languages,
0867: nor the code of governments, nor the politics of various states
0868: possessed attractions for me. It was the secrets of heaven and earth
0869: that I desired to learn; and whether it was the outward substance of
0870: things or the inner spirit of nature and the mysterious soul of man
0871: that occupied me, still my inquiries were directed to the metaphysical,
0872: or in its highest sense, the physical secrets of the world.
0873: 
0874: Meanwhile Clerval occupied himself, so to speak, with the moral
0875: relations of things. The busy stage of life, the virtues of heroes,
0876: and the actions of men were his theme; and his hope and his dream was
0877: to become one among those whose names are recorded in story as the
0878: gallant and adventurous benefactors of our species. The saintly soul
0879: of Elizabeth shone like a shrine-dedicated lamp in our peaceful home.
0880: Her sympathy was ours; her smile, her soft voice, the sweet glance of
0881: her celestial eyes, were ever there to bless and animate us. She was
0882: the living spirit of love to soften and attract; I might have become
0883: sullen in my study, rough through the ardour of my nature, but that
0884: she was there to subdue me to a semblance of her own gentleness. And
0885: Clerval—could aught ill entrench on the noble spirit of Clerval? Yet
0886: he might not have been so perfectly humane, so thoughtful in his
0887: generosity, so full of kindness and tenderness amidst his passion for
0888: adventurous exploit, had she not unfolded to him the real loveliness of
0889: beneficence and made the doing good the end and aim of his soaring
0890: ambition.
0891: 
0892: I feel exquisite pleasure in dwelling on the recollections of childhood,
0893: before misfortune had tainted my mind and changed its bright visions of
0894: extensive usefulness into gloomy and narrow reflections upon self. Besides,
0895: in drawing the picture of my early days, I also record those events which
0896: led, by insensible steps, to my after tale of misery, for when I would
0897: account to myself for the birth of that passion which afterwards ruled my
0898: destiny I find it arise, like a mountain river, from ignoble and almost
0899: forgotten sources; but, swelling as it proceeded, it became the torrent
0900: which, in its course, has swept away all my hopes and joys.
0901: 
0902: Natural philosophy is the genius that has regulated my fate; I desire,
0903: therefore, in this narration, to state those facts which led to my
0904: predilection for that science. When I was thirteen years of age we all went
0905: on a party of pleasure to the baths near Thonon; the inclemency of the
0906: weather obliged us to remain a day confined to the inn. In this house I
0907: chanced to find a volume of the works of Cornelius Agrippa. I opened it
0908: with apathy; the theory which he attempts to demonstrate and the wonderful
0909: facts which he relates soon changed this feeling into enthusiasm. A new
0910: light seemed to dawn upon my mind, and bounding with joy, I communicated my
0911: discovery to my father. My father looked carelessly at the title page of my
0912: book and said, “Ah! Cornelius Agrippa! My dear Victor, do not waste
0913: your time upon this; it is sad trash.”
0914: 
0915: If, instead of this remark, my father had taken the pains to explain to me
0916: that the principles of Agrippa had been entirely exploded and that a modern
0917: system of science had been introduced which possessed much greater powers
0918: than the ancient, because the powers of the latter were chimerical, while
0919: those of the former were real and practical, under such circumstances I
0920: should certainly have thrown Agrippa aside and have contented my
0921: imagination, warmed as it was, by returning with greater ardour to my
0922: former studies. It is even possible that the train of my ideas would never
0923: have received the fatal impulse that led to my ruin. But the cursory glance
0924: my father had taken of my volume by no means assured me that he was
0925: acquainted with its contents, and I continued to read with the greatest
0926: avidity.
0927: 
0928: When I returned home my first care was to procure the whole works of this
0929: author, and afterwards of Paracelsus and Albertus Magnus. I read and
0930: studied the wild fancies of these writers with delight; they appeared to me
0931: treasures known to few besides myself. I have described myself as always
0932: having been imbued with a fervent longing to penetrate the secrets of
0933: nature. In spite of the intense labour and wonderful discoveries of modern
0934: philosophers, I always came from my studies discontented and unsatisfied.
0935: Sir Isaac Newton is said to have avowed that he felt like a child picking
0936: up shells beside the great and unexplored ocean of truth. Those of his
0937: successors in each branch of natural philosophy with whom I was acquainted
0938: appeared even to my boy’s apprehensions as tyros engaged in the same
0939: pursuit.
0940: 
0941: The untaught peasant beheld the elements around him and was acquainted
0942: with their practical uses. The most learned philosopher knew little
0943: more. He had partially unveiled the face of Nature, but her immortal
0944: lineaments were still a wonder and a mystery. He might dissect,
0945: anatomise, and give names; but, not to speak of a final cause, causes
0946: in their secondary and tertiary grades were utterly unknown to him. I
0947: had gazed upon the fortifications and impediments that seemed to keep
0948: human beings from entering the citadel of nature, and rashly and
0949: ignorantly I had repined.
0950: 
0951: But here were books, and here were men who had penetrated deeper and knew
0952: more. I took their word for all that they averred, and I became their
0953: disciple. It may appear strange that such should arise in the eighteenth
0954: century; but while I followed the routine of education in the schools of
0955: Geneva, I was, to a great degree, self-taught with regard to my favourite
0956: studies. My father was not scientific, and I was left to struggle with a
0957: child’s blindness, added to a student’s thirst for knowledge.
0958: Under the guidance of my new preceptors I entered with the greatest
0959: diligence into the search of the philosopher’s stone and the elixir
0960: of life; but the latter soon obtained my undivided attention. Wealth was an
0961: inferior object, but what glory would attend the discovery if I could
0962: banish disease from the human frame and render man invulnerable to any but
0963: a violent death!
0964: 
0965: Nor were these my only visions. The raising of ghosts or devils was a
0966: promise liberally accorded by my favourite authors, the fulfilment of which
0967: I most eagerly sought; and if my incantations were always unsuccessful, I
0968: attributed the failure rather to my own inexperience and mistake than to a
0969: want of skill or fidelity in my instructors. And thus for a time I was
0970: occupied by exploded systems, mingling, like an unadept, a thousand
0971: contradictory theories and floundering desperately in a very slough of
0972: multifarious knowledge, guided by an ardent imagination and childish
0973: reasoning, till an accident again changed the current of my ideas.
0974: 
0975: When I was about fifteen years old we had retired to our house near
0976: Belrive, when we witnessed a most violent and terrible thunderstorm. It
0977: advanced from behind the mountains of Jura, and the thunder burst at once
0978: with frightful loudness from various quarters of the heavens. I remained,
0979: while the storm lasted, watching its progress with curiosity and delight.
0980: As I stood at the door, on a sudden I beheld a stream of fire issue from an
0981: old and beautiful oak which stood about twenty yards from our house; and so
0982: soon as the dazzling light vanished, the oak had disappeared, and nothing
0983: remained but a blasted stump. When we visited it the next morning, we found
0984: the tree shattered in a singular manner. It was not splintered by the
0985: shock, but entirely reduced to thin ribbons of wood. I never beheld
0986: anything so utterly destroyed.
0987: 
0988: Before this I was not unacquainted with the more obvious laws of
0989: electricity. On this occasion a man of great research in natural
0990: philosophy was with us, and excited by this catastrophe, he entered on
0991: the explanation of a theory which he had formed on the subject of
0992: electricity and galvanism, which was at once new and astonishing to me.
0993: All that he said threw greatly into the shade Cornelius Agrippa,
0994: Albertus Magnus, and Paracelsus, the lords of my imagination; but by
0995: some fatality the overthrow of these men disinclined me to pursue my
0996: accustomed studies. It seemed to me as if nothing would or could ever
0997: be known. All that had so long engaged my attention suddenly grew
0998: despicable. By one of those caprices of the mind which we are perhaps
0999: most subject to in early youth, I at once gave up my former
1000: occupations, set down natural history and all its progeny as a deformed
1001: and abortive creation, and entertained the greatest disdain for a
1002: would-be science which could never even step within the threshold of
1003: real knowledge. In this mood of mind I betook myself to the
1004: mathematics and the branches of study appertaining to that science as
1005: being built upon secure foundations, and so worthy of my consideration.
1006: 
1007: Thus strangely are our souls constructed, and by such slight ligaments
1008: are we bound to prosperity or ruin. When I look back, it seems to me
1009: as if this almost miraculous change of inclination and will was the
1010: immediate suggestion of the guardian angel of my life—the last effort
1011: made by the spirit of preservation to avert the storm that was even
1012: then hanging in the stars and ready to envelop me. Her victory was
1013: announced by an unusual tranquillity and gladness of soul which
1014: followed the relinquishing of my ancient and latterly tormenting
1015: studies. It was thus that I was to be taught to associate evil with
1016: their prosecution, happiness with their disregard.
1017: 
1018: It was a strong effort of the spirit of good, but it was ineffectual.
1019: Destiny was too potent, and her immutable laws had decreed my utter and
1020: terrible destruction.
1021: 
1022: 
1023: 
1024: 
1025: Chapter 3
1026: 
1027: 
1028: When I had attained the age of seventeen my parents resolved that I
1029: should become a student at the university of Ingolstadt. I had
1030: hitherto attended the schools of Geneva, but my father thought it
1031: necessary for the completion of my education that I should be made
1032: acquainted with other customs than those of my native country. My
1033: departure was therefore fixed at an early date, but before the day
1034: resolved upon could arrive, the first misfortune of my life
1035: occurred—an omen, as it were, of my future misery.
1036: 
1037: Elizabeth had caught the scarlet fever; her illness was severe, and she was
1038: in the greatest danger. During her illness many arguments had been urged to
1039: persuade my mother to refrain from attending upon her. She had at first
1040: yielded to our entreaties, but when she heard that the life of her
1041: favourite was menaced, she could no longer control her anxiety. She
1042: attended her sickbed; her watchful attentions triumphed over the malignity
1043: of the distemper—Elizabeth was saved, but the consequences of this
1044: imprudence were fatal to her preserver. On the third day my mother
1045: sickened; her fever was accompanied by the most alarming symptoms, and the
1046: looks of her medical attendants prognosticated the worst event. On her
1047: deathbed the fortitude and benignity of this best of women did not desert
1048: her. She joined the hands of Elizabeth and myself. “My
1049: children,” she said, “my firmest hopes of future happiness were
1050: placed on the prospect of your union. This expectation will now be the
1051: consolation of your father. Elizabeth, my love, you must supply my place to
1052: my younger children. Alas! I regret that I am taken from you; and, happy
1053: and beloved as I have been, is it not hard to quit you all? But these are
1054: not thoughts befitting me; I will endeavour to resign myself cheerfully to
1055: death and will indulge a hope of meeting you in another world.”
1056: 
1057: She died calmly, and her countenance expressed affection even in death.
1058: I need not describe the feelings of those whose dearest ties are rent
1059: by that most irreparable evil, the void that presents itself to the
1060: soul, and the despair that is exhibited on the countenance. It is so
1061: long before the mind can persuade itself that she whom we saw every day
1062: and whose very existence appeared a part of our own can have departed
1063: for ever—that the brightness of a beloved eye can have been
1064: extinguished and the sound of a voice so familiar and dear to the ear
1065: can be hushed, never more to be heard. These are the reflections of
1066: the first days; but when the lapse of time proves the reality of the
1067: evil, then the actual bitterness of grief commences. Yet from whom has
1068: not that rude hand rent away some dear connection? And why should I
1069: describe a sorrow which all have felt, and must feel? The time at
1070: length arrives when grief is rather an indulgence than a necessity; and
1071: the smile that plays upon the lips, although it may be deemed a
1072: sacrilege, is not banished. My mother was dead, but we had still
1073: duties which we ought to perform; we must continue our course with the
1074: rest and learn to think ourselves fortunate whilst one remains whom the
1075: spoiler has not seized.
1076: 
1077: My departure for Ingolstadt, which had been deferred by these events,
1078: was now again determined upon. I obtained from my father a respite of
1079: some weeks. It appeared to me sacrilege so soon to leave the repose,
1080: akin to death, of the house of mourning and to rush into the thick of
1081: life. I was new to sorrow, but it did not the less alarm me. I was
1082: unwilling to quit the sight of those that remained to me, and above
1083: all, I desired to see my sweet Elizabeth in some degree consoled.
1084: 
1085: She indeed veiled her grief and strove to act the comforter to us all.
1086: She looked steadily on life and assumed its duties with courage and
1087: zeal. She devoted herself to those whom she had been taught to call
1088: her uncle and cousins. Never was she so enchanting as at this time,
1089: when she recalled the sunshine of her smiles and spent them upon us.
1090: She forgot even her own regret in her endeavours to make us forget.
1091: 
1092: The day of my departure at length arrived. Clerval spent the last
1093: evening with us. He had endeavoured to persuade his father to permit
1094: him to accompany me and to become my fellow student, but in vain. His
1095: father was a narrow-minded trader and saw idleness and ruin in the
1096: aspirations and ambition of his son. Henry deeply felt the misfortune
1097: of being debarred from a liberal education. He said little, but when
1098: he spoke I read in his kindling eye and in his animated glance a
1099: restrained but firm resolve not to be chained to the miserable details
1100: of commerce.
1101: 
1102: We sat late. We could not tear ourselves away from each other nor
1103: persuade ourselves to say the word “Farewell!” It was said, and we
1104: retired under the pretence of seeking repose, each fancying that the
1105: other was deceived; but when at morning’s dawn I descended to the
1106: carriage which was to convey me away, they were all there—my father
1107: again to bless me, Clerval to press my hand once more, my Elizabeth to
1108: renew her entreaties that I would write often and to bestow the last
1109: feminine attentions on her playmate and friend.
1110: 
1111: I threw myself into the chaise that was to convey me away and indulged in
1112: the most melancholy reflections. I, who had ever been surrounded by
1113: amiable companions, continually engaged in endeavouring to bestow mutual
1114: pleasure—I was now alone. In the university whither I was going I
1115: must form my own friends and be my own protector. My life had hitherto
1116: been remarkably secluded and domestic, and this had given me invincible
1117: repugnance to new countenances. I loved my brothers, Elizabeth, and
1118: Clerval; these were “old familiar faces,” but I believed myself
1119: totally unfitted for the company of strangers. Such were my reflections as
1120: I commenced my journey; but as I proceeded, my spirits and hopes rose. I
1121: ardently desired the acquisition of knowledge. I had often, when at home,
1122: thought it hard to remain during my youth cooped up in one place and had
1123: longed to enter the world and take my station among other human beings. 
1124: Now my desires were complied with, and it would, indeed, have been folly to
1125: repent.
1126: 
1127: I had sufficient leisure for these and many other reflections during my
1128: journey to Ingolstadt, which was long and fatiguing. At length the
1129: high white steeple of the town met my eyes. I alighted and was
1130: conducted to my solitary apartment to spend the evening as I pleased.
1131: 
1132: The next morning I delivered my letters of introduction and paid a visit to
1133: some of the principal professors. Chance—or rather the evil
1134: influence, the Angel of Destruction, which asserted omnipotent sway over me
1135: from the moment I turned my reluctant steps from my father’s
1136: door—led me first to M. Krempe, professor of natural philosophy. He
1137: was an uncouth man, but deeply imbued in the secrets of his science. He
1138: asked me several questions concerning my progress in the different branches
1139: of science appertaining to natural philosophy. I replied carelessly, and
1140: partly in contempt, mentioned the names of my alchemists as the principal
1141: authors I had studied. The professor stared. “Have you,” he
1142: said, “really spent your time in studying such nonsense?”
1143: 
1144: I replied in the affirmative. “Every minute,” continued M. Krempe with
1145: warmth, “every instant that you have wasted on those books is utterly
1146: and entirely lost. You have burdened your memory with exploded systems
1147: and useless names. Good God! In what desert land have you lived,
1148: where no one was kind enough to inform you that these fancies which you
1149: have so greedily imbibed are a thousand years old and as musty as they
1150: are ancient? I little expected, in this enlightened and scientific
1151: age, to find a disciple of Albertus Magnus and Paracelsus. My dear
1152: sir, you must begin your studies entirely anew.”
1153: 
1154: So saying, he stepped aside and wrote down a list of several books
1155: treating of natural philosophy which he desired me to procure, and
1156: dismissed me after mentioning that in the beginning of the following
1157: week he intended to commence a course of lectures upon natural
1158: philosophy in its general relations, and that M. Waldman, a fellow
1159: professor, would lecture upon chemistry the alternate days that he
1160: omitted.
1161: 
1162: I returned home not disappointed, for I have said that I had long
1163: considered those authors useless whom the professor reprobated; but I
1164: returned not at all the more inclined to recur to these studies in any
1165: shape. M. Krempe was a little squat man with a gruff voice and a
1166: repulsive countenance; the teacher, therefore, did not prepossess me in
1167: favour of his pursuits. In rather a too philosophical and connected a
1168: strain, perhaps, I have given an account of the conclusions I had come
1169: to concerning them in my early years. As a child I had not been
1170: content with the results promised by the modern professors of natural
1171: science. With a confusion of ideas only to be accounted for by my
1172: extreme youth and my want of a guide on such matters, I had retrod the
1173: steps of knowledge along the paths of time and exchanged the
1174: discoveries of recent inquirers for the dreams of forgotten alchemists.
1175: Besides, I had a contempt for the uses of modern natural philosophy.
1176: It was very different when the masters of the science sought
1177: immortality and power; such views, although futile, were grand; but now
1178: the scene was changed. The ambition of the inquirer seemed to limit
1179: itself to the annihilation of those visions on which my interest in
1180: science was chiefly founded. I was required to exchange chimeras of
1181: boundless grandeur for realities of little worth.
1182: 
1183: Such were my reflections during the first two or three days of my
1184: residence at Ingolstadt, which were chiefly spent in becoming
1185: acquainted with the localities and the principal residents in my new
1186: abode. But as the ensuing week commenced, I thought of the information
1187: which M. Krempe had given me concerning the lectures. And although I
1188: could not consent to go and hear that little conceited fellow deliver
1189: sentences out of a pulpit, I recollected what he had said of M.
1190: Waldman, whom I had never seen, as he had hitherto been out of town.
1191: 
1192: Partly from curiosity and partly from idleness, I went into the lecturing
1193: room, which M. Waldman entered shortly after. This professor was very
1194: unlike his colleague. He appeared about fifty years of age, but with an
1195: aspect expressive of the greatest benevolence; a few grey hairs covered his
1196: temples, but those at the back of his head were nearly black. His person
1197: was short but remarkably erect and his voice the sweetest I had ever heard.
1198: He began his lecture by a recapitulation of the history of chemistry and
1199: the various improvements made by different men of learning, pronouncing
1200: with fervour the names of the most distinguished discoverers. He then took
1201: a cursory view of the present state of the science and explained many of
1202: its elementary terms. After having made a few preparatory experiments, he
1203: concluded with a panegyric upon modern chemistry, the terms of which I
1204: shall never forget:
1205: 
1206: “The ancient teachers of this science,” said he,
1207: “promised impossibilities and performed nothing. The modern masters
1208: promise very little; they know that metals cannot be transmuted and that
1209: the elixir of life is a chimera but these philosophers, whose hands seem
1210: only made to dabble in dirt, and their eyes to pore over the microscope or
1211: crucible, have indeed performed miracles. They penetrate into the recesses
1212: of nature and show how she works in her hiding-places. They ascend into the
1213: heavens; they have discovered how the blood circulates, and the nature of
1214: the air we breathe. They have acquired new and almost unlimited powers;
1215: they can command the thunders of heaven, mimic the earthquake, and even
1216: mock the invisible world with its own shadows.”
1217: 
1218: Such were the professor’s words—rather let me say such the words of
1219: the fate—enounced to destroy me. As he went on I felt as if my soul
1220: were grappling with a palpable enemy; one by one the various keys were
1221: touched which formed the mechanism of my being; chord after chord was
1222: sounded, and soon my mind was filled with one thought, one conception,
1223: one purpose. So much has been done, exclaimed the soul of
1224: Frankenstein—more, far more, will I achieve; treading in the steps
1225: already marked, I will pioneer a new way, explore unknown powers, and
1226: unfold to the world the deepest mysteries of creation.
1227: 
1228: I closed not my eyes that night. My internal being was in a state of
1229: insurrection and turmoil; I felt that order would thence arise, but I
1230: had no power to produce it. By degrees, after the morning’s dawn,
1231: sleep came. I awoke, and my yesternight’s thoughts were as a dream.
1232: There only remained a resolution to return to my ancient studies and to
1233: devote myself to a science for which I believed myself to possess a
1234: natural talent. On the same day I paid M. Waldman a visit. His
1235: manners in private were even more mild and attractive than in public,
1236: for there was a certain dignity in his mien during his lecture which in
1237: his own house was replaced by the greatest affability and kindness. I
1238: gave him pretty nearly the same account of my former pursuits as I had
1239: given to his fellow professor. He heard with attention the little
1240: narration concerning my studies and smiled at the names of Cornelius
1241: Agrippa and Paracelsus, but without the contempt that M. Krempe had
1242: exhibited. He said that “These were men to whose indefatigable zeal
1243: modern philosophers were indebted for most of the foundations of their
1244: knowledge. They had left to us, as an easier task, to give new names
1245: and arrange in connected classifications the facts which they in a
1246: great degree had been the instruments of bringing to light. The
1247: labours of men of genius, however erroneously directed, scarcely ever
1248: fail in ultimately turning to the solid advantage of mankind.” I
1249: listened to his statement, which was delivered without any presumption
1250: or affectation, and then added that his lecture had removed my
1251: prejudices against modern chemists; I expressed myself in measured
1252: terms, with the modesty and deference due from a youth to his
1253: instructor, without letting escape (inexperience in life would have
1254: made me ashamed) any of the enthusiasm which stimulated my intended
1255: labours. I requested his advice concerning the books I ought to
1256: procure.
1257: 
1258: “I am happy,” said M. Waldman, “to have gained a
1259: disciple; and if your application equals your ability, I have no doubt of
1260: your success. Chemistry is that branch of natural philosophy in which the
1261: greatest improvements have been and may be made; it is on that account that
1262: I have made it my peculiar study; but at the same time, I have not
1263: neglected the other branches of science. A man would make but a very sorry
1264: chemist if he attended to that department of human knowledge alone. If your
1265: wish is to become really a man of science and not merely a petty
1266: experimentalist, I should advise you to apply to every branch of natural
1267: philosophy, including mathematics.”
1268: 
1269: He then took me into his laboratory and explained to me the uses of his
1270: various machines, instructing me as to what I ought to procure and
1271: promising me the use of his own when I should have advanced far enough in
1272: the science not to derange their mechanism. He also gave me the list of
1273: books which I had requested, and I took my leave.
1274: 
1275: Thus ended a day memorable to me; it decided my future destiny.
1276: 
1277: 
1278: 
1279: 
1280: Chapter 4
1281: 
1282: 
1283: From this day natural philosophy, and particularly chemistry, in the
1284: most comprehensive sense of the term, became nearly my sole occupation.
1285: I read with ardour those works, so full of genius and discrimination,
1286: which modern inquirers have written on these subjects. I attended the
1287: lectures and cultivated the acquaintance of the men of science of the
1288: university, and I found even in M. Krempe a great deal of sound sense
1289: and real information, combined, it is true, with a repulsive
1290: physiognomy and manners, but not on that account the less valuable. In
1291: M. Waldman I found a true friend. His gentleness was never tinged by
1292: dogmatism, and his instructions were given with an air of frankness and
1293: good nature that banished every idea of pedantry. In a thousand ways
1294: he smoothed for me the path of knowledge and made the most abstruse
1295: inquiries clear and facile to my apprehension. My application was at
1296: first fluctuating and uncertain; it gained strength as I proceeded and
1297: soon became so ardent and eager that the stars often disappeared in the
1298: light of morning whilst I was yet engaged in my laboratory.
1299: 
1300: As I applied so closely, it may be easily conceived that my progress
1301: was rapid. My ardour was indeed the astonishment of the students, and
1302: my proficiency that of the masters. Professor Krempe often asked me,
1303: with a sly smile, how Cornelius Agrippa went on, whilst M. Waldman
1304: expressed the most heartfelt exultation in my progress. Two years
1305: passed in this manner, during which I paid no visit to Geneva, but was
1306: engaged, heart and soul, in the pursuit of some discoveries which I
1307: hoped to make. None but those who have experienced them can conceive
1308: of the enticements of science. In other studies you go as far as
1309: others have gone before you, and there is nothing more to know; but in
1310: a scientific pursuit there is continual food for discovery and wonder.
1311: A mind of moderate capacity which closely pursues one study must
1312: infallibly arrive at great proficiency in that study; and I, who
