1313: continually sought the attainment of one object of pursuit and was
1314: solely wrapped up in this, improved so rapidly that at the end of two
1315: years I made some discoveries in the improvement of some chemical
1316: instruments, which procured me great esteem and admiration at the
1317: university. When I had arrived at this point and had become as well
1318: acquainted with the theory and practice of natural philosophy as
1319: depended on the lessons of any of the professors at Ingolstadt, my
1320: residence there being no longer conducive to my improvements, I thought
1321: of returning to my friends and my native town, when an incident
1322: happened that protracted my stay.
1323: 
1324: One of the phenomena which had peculiarly attracted my attention was
1325: the structure of the human frame, and, indeed, any animal endued with
1326: life. Whence, I often asked myself, did the principle of life proceed?
1327: It was a bold question, and one which has ever been considered as a
1328: mystery; yet with how many things are we upon the brink of becoming
1329: acquainted, if cowardice or carelessness did not restrain our
1330: inquiries. I revolved these circumstances in my mind and determined
1331: thenceforth to apply myself more particularly to those branches of
1332: natural philosophy which relate to physiology. Unless I had been
1333: animated by an almost supernatural enthusiasm, my application to this
1334: study would have been irksome and almost intolerable. To examine the
1335: causes of life, we must first have recourse to death. I became
1336: acquainted with the science of anatomy, but this was not sufficient; I
1337: must also observe the natural decay and corruption of the human body.
1338: In my education my father had taken the greatest precautions that my
1339: mind should be impressed with no supernatural horrors. I do not ever
1340: remember to have trembled at a tale of superstition or to have feared
1341: the apparition of a spirit. Darkness had no effect upon my fancy, and
1342: a churchyard was to me merely the receptacle of bodies deprived of
1343: life, which, from being the seat of beauty and strength, had become
1344: food for the worm. Now I was led to examine the cause and progress of
1345: this decay and forced to spend days and nights in vaults and
1346: charnel-houses. My attention was fixed upon every object the most
1347: insupportable to the delicacy of the human feelings. I saw how the
1348: fine form of man was degraded and wasted; I beheld the corruption of
1349: death succeed to the blooming cheek of life; I saw how the worm
1350: inherited the wonders of the eye and brain. I paused, examining and
1351: analysing all the minutiae of causation, as exemplified in the change
1352: from life to death, and death to life, until from the midst of this
1353: darkness a sudden light broke in upon me—a light so brilliant and
1354: wondrous, yet so simple, that while I became dizzy with the immensity
1355: of the prospect which it illustrated, I was surprised that among so
1356: many men of genius who had directed their inquiries towards the same
1357: science, that I alone should be reserved to discover so astonishing a
1358: secret.
1359: 
1360: Remember, I am not recording the vision of a madman. The sun does not
1361: more certainly shine in the heavens than that which I now affirm is
1362: true. Some miracle might have produced it, yet the stages of the
1363: discovery were distinct and probable. After days and nights of
1364: incredible labour and fatigue, I succeeded in discovering the cause of
1365: generation and life; nay, more, I became myself capable of bestowing
1366: animation upon lifeless matter.
1367: 
1368: The astonishment which I had at first experienced on this discovery
1369: soon gave place to delight and rapture. After so much time spent in
1370: painful labour, to arrive at once at the summit of my desires was the
1371: most gratifying consummation of my toils. But this discovery was so
1372: great and overwhelming that all the steps by which I had been
1373: progressively led to it were obliterated, and I beheld only the result.
1374: What had been the study and desire of the wisest men since the creation
1375: of the world was now within my grasp. Not that, like a magic scene, it
1376: all opened upon me at once: the information I had obtained was of a
1377: nature rather to direct my endeavours so soon as I should point them
1378: towards the object of my search than to exhibit that object already
1379: accomplished. I was like the Arabian who had been buried with the dead
1380: and found a passage to life, aided only by one glimmering and seemingly
1381: ineffectual light.
1382: 
1383: I see by your eagerness and the wonder and hope which your eyes
1384: express, my friend, that you expect to be informed of the secret with
1385: which I am acquainted; that cannot be; listen patiently until the end
1386: of my story, and you will easily perceive why I am reserved upon that
1387: subject. I will not lead you on, unguarded and ardent as I then was,
1388: to your destruction and infallible misery. Learn from me, if not by my
1389: precepts, at least by my example, how dangerous is the acquirement of
1390: knowledge and how much happier that man is who believes his native town
1391: to be the world, than he who aspires to become greater than his nature
1392: will allow.
1393: 
1394: When I found so astonishing a power placed within my hands, I hesitated
1395: a long time concerning the manner in which I should employ it.
1396: Although I possessed the capacity of bestowing animation, yet to
1397: prepare a frame for the reception of it, with all its intricacies of
1398: fibres, muscles, and veins, still remained a work of inconceivable
1399: difficulty and labour. I doubted at first whether I should attempt the
1400: creation of a being like myself, or one of simpler organization; but my
1401: imagination was too much exalted by my first success to permit me to
1402: doubt of my ability to give life to an animal as complex and wonderful
1403: as man. The materials at present within my command hardly appeared
1404: adequate to so arduous an undertaking, but I doubted not that I should
1405: ultimately succeed. I prepared myself for a multitude of reverses; my
1406: operations might be incessantly baffled, and at last my work be
1407: imperfect, yet when I considered the improvement which every day takes
1408: place in science and mechanics, I was encouraged to hope my present
1409: attempts would at least lay the foundations of future success. Nor
1410: could I consider the magnitude and complexity of my plan as any
1411: argument of its impracticability. It was with these feelings that I
1412: began the creation of a human being. As the minuteness of the parts
1413: formed a great hindrance to my speed, I resolved, contrary to my first
1414: intention, to make the being of a gigantic stature, that is to say,
1415: about eight feet in height, and proportionably large. After having
1416: formed this determination and having spent some months in successfully
1417: collecting and arranging my materials, I began.
1418: 
1419: No one can conceive the variety of feelings which bore me onwards, like
1420: a hurricane, in the first enthusiasm of success. Life and death
1421: appeared to me ideal bounds, which I should first break through, and
1422: pour a torrent of light into our dark world. A new species would bless
1423: me as its creator and source; many happy and excellent natures would
1424: owe their being to me. No father could claim the gratitude of his
1425: child so completely as I should deserve theirs. Pursuing these
1426: reflections, I thought that if I could bestow animation upon lifeless
1427: matter, I might in process of time (although I now found it impossible)
1428: renew life where death had apparently devoted the body to corruption.
1429: 
1430: These thoughts supported my spirits, while I pursued my undertaking
1431: with unremitting ardour. My cheek had grown pale with study, and my
1432: person had become emaciated with confinement. Sometimes, on the very
1433: brink of certainty, I failed; yet still I clung to the hope which the
1434: next day or the next hour might realise. One secret which I alone
1435: possessed was the hope to which I had dedicated myself; and the moon
1436: gazed on my midnight labours, while, with unrelaxed and breathless
1437: eagerness, I pursued nature to her hiding-places. Who shall conceive
1438: the horrors of my secret toil as I dabbled among the unhallowed damps
1439: of the grave or tortured the living animal to animate the lifeless
1440: clay? My limbs now tremble, and my eyes swim with the remembrance; but
1441: then a resistless and almost frantic impulse urged me forward; I seemed
1442: to have lost all soul or sensation but for this one pursuit. It was
1443: indeed but a passing trance, that only made me feel with renewed
1444: acuteness so soon as, the unnatural stimulus ceasing to operate, I had
1445: returned to my old habits. I collected bones from charnel-houses and
1446: disturbed, with profane fingers, the tremendous secrets of the human
1447: frame. In a solitary chamber, or rather cell, at the top of the house,
1448: and separated from all the other apartments by a gallery and staircase,
1449: I kept my workshop of filthy creation; my eyeballs were starting from
1450: their sockets in attending to the details of my employment. The
1451: dissecting room and the slaughter-house furnished many of my materials;
1452: and often did my human nature turn with loathing from my occupation,
1453: whilst, still urged on by an eagerness which perpetually increased, I
1454: brought my work near to a conclusion.
1455: 
1456: The summer months passed while I was thus engaged, heart and soul, in
1457: one pursuit. It was a most beautiful season; never did the fields
1458: bestow a more plentiful harvest or the vines yield a more luxuriant
1459: vintage, but my eyes were insensible to the charms of nature. And the
1460: same feelings which made me neglect the scenes around me caused me also
1461: to forget those friends who were so many miles absent, and whom I had
1462: not seen for so long a time. I knew my silence disquieted them, and I
1463: well remembered the words of my father: “I know that while you are
1464: pleased with yourself you will think of us with affection, and we shall
1465: hear regularly from you. You must pardon me if I regard any
1466: interruption in your correspondence as a proof that your other duties
1467: are equally neglected.”
1468: 
1469: I knew well therefore what would be my father’s feelings, but I could
1470: not tear my thoughts from my employment, loathsome in itself, but which
1471: had taken an irresistible hold of my imagination. I wished, as it
1472: were, to procrastinate all that related to my feelings of affection
1473: until the great object, which swallowed up every habit of my nature,
1474: should be completed.
1475: 
1476: I then thought that my father would be unjust if he ascribed my neglect
1477: to vice or faultiness on my part, but I am now convinced that he was
1478: justified in conceiving that I should not be altogether free from
1479: blame. A human being in perfection ought always to preserve a calm and
1480: peaceful mind and never to allow passion or a transitory desire to
1481: disturb his tranquillity. I do not think that the pursuit of knowledge
1482: is an exception to this rule. If the study to which you apply yourself
1483: has a tendency to weaken your affections and to destroy your taste for
1484: those simple pleasures in which no alloy can possibly mix, then that
1485: study is certainly unlawful, that is to say, not befitting the human
1486: mind. If this rule were always observed; if no man allowed any pursuit
1487: whatsoever to interfere with the tranquillity of his domestic
1488: affections, Greece had not been enslaved, Cæsar would have spared his
1489: country, America would have been discovered more gradually, and the
1490: empires of Mexico and Peru had not been destroyed.
1491: 
1492: But I forget that I am moralizing in the most interesting part of my
1493: tale, and your looks remind me to proceed.
1494: 
1495: My father made no reproach in his letters and only took notice of my
1496: silence by inquiring into my occupations more particularly than before.
1497: Winter, spring, and summer passed away during my labours; but I did not
1498: watch the blossom or the expanding leaves—sights which before always
1499: yielded me supreme delight—so deeply was I engrossed in my
1500: occupation. The leaves of that year had withered before my work drew near
1501: to a close, and now every day showed me more plainly how well I had
1502: succeeded. But my enthusiasm was checked by my anxiety, and I appeared
1503: rather like one doomed by slavery to toil in the mines, or any other
1504: unwholesome trade than an artist occupied by his favourite employment.
1505: Every night I was oppressed by a slow fever, and I became nervous to a most
1506: painful degree; the fall of a leaf startled me, and I shunned my fellow
1507: creatures as if I had been guilty of a crime. Sometimes I grew alarmed at
1508: the wreck I perceived that I had become; the energy of my purpose alone
1509: sustained me: my labours would soon end, and I believed that exercise and
1510: amusement would then drive away incipient disease; and I promised myself
1511: both of these when my creation should be complete.
1512: 
1513: 
1514: 
1515: 
1516: Chapter 5
1517: 
1518: 
1519: It was on a dreary night of November that I beheld the accomplishment
1520: of my toils. With an anxiety that almost amounted to agony, I
1521: collected the instruments of life around me, that I might infuse a
1522: spark of being into the lifeless thing that lay at my feet. It was
1523: already one in the morning; the rain pattered dismally against the
1524: panes, and my candle was nearly burnt out, when, by the glimmer of the
1525: half-extinguished light, I saw the dull yellow eye of the creature
1526: open; it breathed hard, and a convulsive motion agitated its limbs.
1527: 
1528: How can I describe my emotions at this catastrophe, or how delineate
1529: the wretch whom with such infinite pains and care I had endeavoured to
1530: form? His limbs were in proportion, and I had selected his features as
1531: beautiful. Beautiful! Great God! His yellow skin scarcely covered
1532: the work of muscles and arteries beneath; his hair was of a lustrous
1533: black, and flowing; his teeth of a pearly whiteness; but these
1534: luxuriances only formed a more horrid contrast with his watery eyes,
1535: that seemed almost of the same colour as the dun-white sockets in which
1536: they were set, his shrivelled complexion and straight black lips.
1537: 
1538: The different accidents of life are not so changeable as the feelings
1539: of human nature. I had worked hard for nearly two years, for the sole
1540: purpose of infusing life into an inanimate body. For this I had
1541: deprived myself of rest and health. I had desired it with an ardour
1542: that far exceeded moderation; but now that I had finished, the beauty
1543: of the dream vanished, and breathless horror and disgust filled my
1544: heart. Unable to endure the aspect of the being I had created, I
1545: rushed out of the room and continued a long time traversing my
1546: bed-chamber, unable to compose my mind to sleep. At length lassitude
1547: succeeded to the tumult I had before endured, and I threw myself on the
1548: bed in my clothes, endeavouring to seek a few moments of forgetfulness.
1549: But it was in vain; I slept, indeed, but I was disturbed by the wildest
1550: dreams. I thought I saw Elizabeth, in the bloom of health, walking in
1551: the streets of Ingolstadt. Delighted and surprised, I embraced her,
1552: but as I imprinted the first kiss on her lips, they became livid with
1553: the hue of death; her features appeared to change, and I thought that I
1554: held the corpse of my dead mother in my arms; a shroud enveloped her
1555: form, and I saw the grave-worms crawling in the folds of the flannel.
1556: I started from my sleep with horror; a cold dew covered my forehead, my
1557: teeth chattered, and every limb became convulsed; when, by the dim and
1558: yellow light of the moon, as it forced its way through the window
1559: shutters, I beheld the wretch—the miserable monster whom I had
1560: created. He held up the curtain of the bed; and his eyes, if eyes they
1561: may be called, were fixed on me. His jaws opened, and he muttered some
1562: inarticulate sounds, while a grin wrinkled his cheeks. He might have
1563: spoken, but I did not hear; one hand was stretched out, seemingly to
1564: detain me, but I escaped and rushed downstairs. I took refuge in the
1565: courtyard belonging to the house which I inhabited, where I remained
1566: during the rest of the night, walking up and down in the greatest
1567: agitation, listening attentively, catching and fearing each sound as if
1568: it were to announce the approach of the demoniacal corpse to which I
1569: had so miserably given life.
1570: 
1571: Oh! No mortal could support the horror of that countenance. A mummy
1572: again endued with animation could not be so hideous as that wretch. I
1573: had gazed on him while unfinished; he was ugly then, but when those
1574: muscles and joints were rendered capable of motion, it became a thing
1575: such as even Dante could not have conceived.
1576: 
1577: I passed the night wretchedly. Sometimes my pulse beat so quickly and
1578: hardly that I felt the palpitation of every artery; at others, I nearly
1579: sank to the ground through languor and extreme weakness. Mingled with
1580: this horror, I felt the bitterness of disappointment; dreams that had
1581: been my food and pleasant rest for so long a space were now become a
1582: hell to me; and the change was so rapid, the overthrow so complete!
1583: 
1584: Morning, dismal and wet, at length dawned and discovered to my
1585: sleepless and aching eyes the church of Ingolstadt, its white steeple
1586: and clock, which indicated the sixth hour. The porter opened the gates
1587: of the court, which had that night been my asylum, and I issued into
1588: the streets, pacing them with quick steps, as if I sought to avoid the
1589: wretch whom I feared every turning of the street would present to my
1590: view. I did not dare return to the apartment which I inhabited, but
1591: felt impelled to hurry on, although drenched by the rain which poured
1592: from a black and comfortless sky.
1593: 
1594: I continued walking in this manner for some time, endeavouring by
1595: bodily exercise to ease the load that weighed upon my mind. I
1596: traversed the streets without any clear conception of where I was or
1597: what I was doing. My heart palpitated in the sickness of fear, and I
1598: hurried on with irregular steps, not daring to look about me:
1599:  
1600:  Like one who, on a lonely road,
1601:  Doth walk in fear and dread,
1602:  And, having once turned round, walks on,
1603:  And turns no more his head;
1604:  Because he knows a frightful fiend
1605:  Doth close behind him tread.
1606:  
1607:  [Coleridge’s “Ancient Mariner.”]
1608: 
1609: 
1610: 
1611: Continuing thus, I came at length opposite to the inn at which the various
1612: diligences and carriages usually stopped. Here I paused, I knew not why;
1613: but I remained some minutes with my eyes fixed on a coach that was coming
1614: towards me from the other end of the street. As it drew nearer I observed
1615: that it was the Swiss diligence; it stopped just where I was standing, and
1616: on the door being opened, I perceived Henry Clerval, who, on seeing me,
1617: instantly sprung out. “My dear Frankenstein,” exclaimed he,
1618: “how glad I am to see you! How fortunate that you should be here at
1619: the very moment of my alighting!”
1620: 
1621: Nothing could equal my delight on seeing Clerval; his presence brought back
1622: to my thoughts my father, Elizabeth, and all those scenes of home so dear
1623: to my recollection. I grasped his hand, and in a moment forgot my horror
1624: and misfortune; I felt suddenly, and for the first time during many months,
1625: calm and serene joy. I welcomed my friend, therefore, in the most cordial
1626: manner, and we walked towards my college. Clerval continued talking for
1627: some time about our mutual friends and his own good fortune in being
1628: permitted to come to Ingolstadt. “You may easily believe,” said
1629: he, “how great was the difficulty to persuade my father that all
1630: necessary knowledge was not comprised in the noble art of book-keeping;
1631: and, indeed, I believe I left him incredulous to the last, for his constant
1632: answer to my unwearied entreaties was the same as that of the Dutch
1633: schoolmaster in The Vicar of Wakefield: ‘I have ten thousand florins
1634: a year without Greek, I eat heartily without Greek.’ But his
1635: affection for me at length overcame his dislike of learning, and he has
1636: permitted me to undertake a voyage of discovery to the land of
1637: knowledge.”
1638: 
1639: “It gives me the greatest delight to see you; but tell me how you left
1640: my father, brothers, and Elizabeth.”
1641: 
1642: “Very well, and very happy, only a little uneasy that they hear from
1643: you so seldom. By the by, I mean to lecture you a little upon their
1644: account myself. But, my dear Frankenstein,” continued he, stopping
1645: short and gazing full in my face, “I did not before remark how very ill
1646: you appear; so thin and pale; you look as if you had been watching for
1647: several nights.”
1648: 
1649: “You have guessed right; I have lately been so deeply engaged in one
1650: occupation that I have not allowed myself sufficient rest, as you see;
1651: but I hope, I sincerely hope, that all these employments are now at an
1652: end and that I am at length free.”
1653: 
1654: I trembled excessively; I could not endure to think of, and far less to
1655: allude to, the occurrences of the preceding night. I walked with a
1656: quick pace, and we soon arrived at my college. I then reflected, and
1657: the thought made me shiver, that the creature whom I had left in my
1658: apartment might still be there, alive and walking about. I dreaded to
1659: behold this monster, but I feared still more that Henry should see him.
1660: Entreating him, therefore, to remain a few minutes at the bottom of the
1661: stairs, I darted up towards my own room. My hand was already on the
1662: lock of the door before I recollected myself. I then paused, and a
1663: cold shivering came over me. I threw the door forcibly open, as
1664: children are accustomed to do when they expect a spectre to stand in
1665: waiting for them on the other side; but nothing appeared. I stepped
1666: fearfully in: the apartment was empty, and my bedroom was also freed
1667: from its hideous guest. I could hardly believe that so great a good
1668: fortune could have befallen me, but when I became assured that my enemy
1669: had indeed fled, I clapped my hands for joy and ran down to Clerval.
1670: 
1671: We ascended into my room, and the servant presently brought breakfast;
1672: but I was unable to contain myself. It was not joy only that possessed
1673: me; I felt my flesh tingle with excess of sensitiveness, and my pulse
1674: beat rapidly. I was unable to remain for a single instant in the same
1675: place; I jumped over the chairs, clapped my hands, and laughed aloud.
1676: Clerval at first attributed my unusual spirits to joy on his arrival,
1677: but when he observed me more attentively, he saw a wildness in my eyes
1678: for which he could not account, and my loud, unrestrained, heartless
1679: laughter frightened and astonished him.
1680: 
1681: “My dear Victor,” cried he, “what, for God’s sake,
1682: is the matter? Do not laugh in that manner. How ill you are! What is the
1683: cause of all this?”
1684: 
1685: “Do not ask me,” cried I, putting my hands before my eyes, for I
1686: thought I saw the dreaded spectre glide into the room; “_he_ can
1687: tell. Oh, save me! Save me!” I imagined that the monster seized me;
1688: I struggled furiously and fell down in a fit.
1689: 
1690: Poor Clerval! What must have been his feelings? A meeting, which he
1691: anticipated with such joy, so strangely turned to bitterness. But I
1692: was not the witness of his grief, for I was lifeless and did not
1693: recover my senses for a long, long time.
1694: 
1695: This was the commencement of a nervous fever which confined me for
1696: several months. During all that time Henry was my only nurse. I
1697: afterwards learned that, knowing my father’s advanced age and unfitness
1698: for so long a journey, and how wretched my sickness would make
1699: Elizabeth, he spared them this grief by concealing the extent of my
1700: disorder. He knew that I could not have a more kind and attentive
1701: nurse than himself; and, firm in the hope he felt of my recovery, he
1702: did not doubt that, instead of doing harm, he performed the kindest
1703: action that he could towards them.
1704: 
1705: But I was in reality very ill, and surely nothing but the unbounded and
1706: unremitting attentions of my friend could have restored me to life.
1707: The form of the monster on whom I had bestowed existence was for ever
1708: before my eyes, and I raved incessantly concerning him. Doubtless my
1709: words surprised Henry; he at first believed them to be the wanderings
1710: of my disturbed imagination, but the pertinacity with which I
1711: continually recurred to the same subject persuaded him that my disorder
1712: indeed owed its origin to some uncommon and terrible event.
1713: 
1714: By very slow degrees, and with frequent relapses that alarmed and
1715: grieved my friend, I recovered. I remember the first time I became
1716: capable of observing outward objects with any kind of pleasure, I
1717: perceived that the fallen leaves had disappeared and that the young
1718: buds were shooting forth from the trees that shaded my window. It was
1719: a divine spring, and the season contributed greatly to my
1720: convalescence. I felt also sentiments of joy and affection revive in
1721: my bosom; my gloom disappeared, and in a short time I became as
1722: cheerful as before I was attacked by the fatal passion.
1723: 
1724: “Dearest Clerval,” exclaimed I, “how kind, how very good
1725: you are to me. This whole winter, instead of being spent in study, as you
1726: promised yourself, has been consumed in my sick room. How shall I ever
1727: repay you? I feel the greatest remorse for the disappointment of which I
1728: have been the occasion, but you will forgive me.”
1729: 
1730: “You will repay me entirely if you do not discompose yourself, but get
1731: well as fast as you can; and since you appear in such good spirits, I
1732: may speak to you on one subject, may I not?”
1733: 
1734: I trembled. One subject! What could it be? Could he allude to an object on
1735: whom I dared not even think?
1736: 
1737: “Compose yourself,” said Clerval, who observed my change of
1738: colour, “I will not mention it if it agitates you; but your father
1739: and cousin would be very happy if they received a letter from you in your
1740: own handwriting. They hardly know how ill you have been and are uneasy at
1741: your long silence.”
1742: 
1743: “Is that all, my dear Henry? How could you suppose that my first
1744: thought would not fly towards those dear, dear friends whom I love and
1745: who are so deserving of my love?”
1746: 
1747: “If this is your present temper, my friend, you will perhaps be glad
1748: to see a letter that has been lying here some days for you; it is from
1749: your cousin, I believe.”
1750: 
1751: 
1752: 
1753: 
1754: Chapter 6
1755: 
1756: 
1757: Clerval then put the following letter into my hands. It was from my
1758: own Elizabeth:
1759: 
1760: “My dearest Cousin,
1761: 
1762: “You have been ill, very ill, and even the constant letters of dear
1763: kind Henry are not sufficient to reassure me on your account. You are
1764: forbidden to write—to hold a pen; yet one word from you, dear Victor,
1765: is necessary to calm our apprehensions. For a long time I have thought
1766: that each post would bring this line, and my persuasions have
1767: restrained my uncle from undertaking a journey to Ingolstadt. I have
1768: prevented his encountering the inconveniences and perhaps dangers of so
1769: long a journey, yet how often have I regretted not being able to
1770: perform it myself! I figure to myself that the task of attending on
1771: your sickbed has devolved on some mercenary old nurse, who could never
1772: guess your wishes nor minister to them with the care and affection of
1773: your poor cousin. Yet that is over now: Clerval writes that indeed
1774: you are getting better. I eagerly hope that you will confirm this
1775: intelligence soon in your own handwriting.
1776: 
1777: “Get well—and return to us. You will find a happy, cheerful home and
1778: friends who love you dearly. Your father’s health is vigorous, and he
1779: asks but to see you, but to be assured that you are well; and not a
1780: care will ever cloud his benevolent countenance. How pleased you would
1781: be to remark the improvement of our Ernest! He is now sixteen and full
1782: of activity and spirit. He is desirous to be a true Swiss and to enter
1783: into foreign service, but we cannot part with him, at least until his
1784: elder brother returns to us. My uncle is not pleased with the idea of
1785: a military career in a distant country, but Ernest never had your
1786: powers of application. He looks upon study as an odious fetter; his
1787: time is spent in the open air, climbing the hills or rowing on the
1788: lake. I fear that he will become an idler unless we yield the point
1789: and permit him to enter on the profession which he has selected.
1790: 
1791: “Little alteration, except the growth of our dear children, has taken
1792: place since you left us. The blue lake and snow-clad mountains—they
1793: never change; and I think our placid home and our contented hearts are
1794: regulated by the same immutable laws. My trifling occupations take up
1795: my time and amuse me, and I am rewarded for any exertions by seeing
1796: none but happy, kind faces around me. Since you left us, but one
1797: change has taken place in our little household. Do you remember on
1798: what occasion Justine Moritz entered our family? Probably you do not;
1799: I will relate her history, therefore in a few words. Madame Moritz,
1800: her mother, was a widow with four children, of whom Justine was the
1801: third. This girl had always been the favourite of her father, but
1802: through a strange perversity, her mother could not endure her, and
1803: after the death of M. Moritz, treated her very ill. My aunt observed
1804: this, and when Justine was twelve years of age, prevailed on her mother
1805: to allow her to live at our house. The republican institutions of our
1806: country have produced simpler and happier manners than those which
1807: prevail in the great monarchies that surround it. Hence there is less
1808: distinction between the several classes of its inhabitants; and the
1809: lower orders, being neither so poor nor so despised, their manners are
1810: more refined and moral. A servant in Geneva does not mean the same
1811: thing as a servant in France and England. Justine, thus received in
1812: our family, learned the duties of a servant, a condition which, in our
1813: fortunate country, does not include the idea of ignorance and a
1814: sacrifice of the dignity of a human being.
1815: 
1816: “Justine, you may remember, was a great favourite of yours; and I
1817: recollect you once remarked that if you were in an ill humour, one
1818: glance from Justine could dissipate it, for the same reason that
1819: Ariosto gives concerning the beauty of Angelica—she looked so
1820: frank-hearted and happy. My aunt conceived a great attachment for her,
1821: by which she was induced to give her an education superior to that
1822: which she had at first intended. This benefit was fully repaid;
1823: Justine was the most grateful little creature in the world: I do not
1824: mean that she made any professions I never heard one pass her lips, but
1825: you could see by her eyes that she almost adored her protectress.
1826: Although her disposition was gay and in many respects inconsiderate,
1827: yet she paid the greatest attention to every gesture of my aunt. She
1828: thought her the model of all excellence and endeavoured to imitate her
1829: phraseology and manners, so that even now she often reminds me of her.
1830: 
1831: “When my dearest aunt died every one was too much occupied in their own
1832: grief to notice poor Justine, who had attended her during her illness
1833: with the most anxious affection. Poor Justine was very ill; but other
1834: trials were reserved for her.
1835: 
1836: “One by one, her brothers and sister died; and her mother, with the
1837: exception of her neglected daughter, was left childless. The
1838: conscience of the woman was troubled; she began to think that the
1839: deaths of her favourites was a judgement from heaven to chastise her
1840: partiality. She was a Roman Catholic; and I believe her confessor
1841: confirmed the idea which she had conceived. Accordingly, a few months
1842: after your departure for Ingolstadt, Justine was called home by her
1843: repentant mother. Poor girl! She wept when she quitted our house; she
1844: was much altered since the death of my aunt; grief had given softness
1845: and a winning mildness to her manners, which had before been remarkable
1846: for vivacity. Nor was her residence at her mother’s house of a nature
1847: to restore her gaiety. The poor woman was very vacillating in her
1848: repentance. She sometimes begged Justine to forgive her unkindness,
1849: but much oftener accused her of having caused the deaths of her
1850: brothers and sister. Perpetual fretting at length threw Madame Moritz
1851: into a decline, which at first increased her irritability, but she is
1852: now at peace for ever. She died on the first approach of cold weather,
1853: at the beginning of this last winter. Justine has just returned to us;
1854: and I assure you I love her tenderly. She is very clever and gentle,
1855: and extremely pretty; as I mentioned before, her mien and her
1856: expression continually remind me of my dear aunt.
1857: 
1858: “I must say also a few words to you, my dear cousin, of little darling
1859: William. I wish you could see him; he is very tall of his age, with
1860: sweet laughing blue eyes, dark eyelashes, and curling hair. When he
1861: smiles, two little dimples appear on each cheek, which are rosy with
1862: health. He has already had one or two little _wives,_ but Louisa Biron
1863: is his favourite, a pretty little girl of five years of age.
1864: 
1865: “Now, dear Victor, I dare say you wish to be indulged in a little
1866: gossip concerning the good people of Geneva. The pretty Miss Mansfield
1867: has already received the congratulatory visits on her approaching
1868: marriage with a young Englishman, John Melbourne, Esq. Her ugly
1869: sister, Manon, married M. Duvillard, the rich banker, last autumn. Your
1870: favourite schoolfellow, Louis Manoir, has suffered several misfortunes
1871: since the departure of Clerval from Geneva. But he has already
1872: recovered his spirits, and is reported to be on the point of marrying a
1873: lively pretty Frenchwoman, Madame Tavernier. She is a widow, and much
1874: older than Manoir; but she is very much admired, and a favourite with
1875: everybody.
1876: 
1877: “I have written myself into better spirits, dear cousin; but my anxiety
1878: returns upon me as I conclude. Write, dearest Victor,—one line—one
1879: word will be a blessing to us. Ten thousand thanks to Henry for his
1880: kindness, his affection, and his many letters; we are sincerely
1881: grateful. Adieu! my cousin; take care of yourself; and, I entreat
1882: you, write!
1883: 
1884: “Elizabeth Lavenza.
1885: 
1886: 
1887: “Geneva, March 18th, 17—.”
1888: 
1889: 
1890: 
1891: “Dear, dear Elizabeth!” I exclaimed, when I had read her
1892: letter: “I will write instantly and relieve them from the anxiety
1893: they must feel.” I wrote, and this exertion greatly fatigued me; but
1894: my convalescence had commenced, and proceeded regularly. In another
1895: fortnight I was able to leave my chamber.
1896: 
1897: One of my first duties on my recovery was to introduce Clerval to the
1898: several professors of the university. In doing this, I underwent a
1899: kind of rough usage, ill befitting the wounds that my mind had
1900: sustained. Ever since the fatal night, the end of my labours, and the
1901: beginning of my misfortunes, I had conceived a violent antipathy even
1902: to the name of natural philosophy. When I was otherwise quite restored
1903: to health, the sight of a chemical instrument would renew all the agony
1904: of my nervous symptoms. Henry saw this, and had removed all my
1905: apparatus from my view. He had also changed my apartment; for he
1906: perceived that I had acquired a dislike for the room which had
1907: previously been my laboratory. But these cares of Clerval were made of
1908: no avail when I visited the professors. M. Waldman inflicted torture
1909: when he praised, with kindness and warmth, the astonishing progress I
1910: had made in the sciences. He soon perceived that I disliked the
1911: subject; but not guessing the real cause, he attributed my feelings to
1912: modesty, and changed the subject from my improvement, to the science
1913: itself, with a desire, as I evidently saw, of drawing me out. What
1914: could I do? He meant to please, and he tormented me. I felt as if he
1915: had placed carefully, one by one, in my view those instruments which
1916: were to be afterwards used in putting me to a slow and cruel death. I
1917: writhed under his words, yet dared not exhibit the pain I felt.
1918: Clerval, whose eyes and feelings were always quick in discerning the
1919: sensations of others, declined the subject, alleging, in excuse, his
1920: total ignorance; and the conversation took a more general turn. I
1921: thanked my friend from my heart, but I did not speak. I saw plainly
1922: that he was surprised, but he never attempted to draw my secret from
1923: me; and although I loved him with a mixture of affection and reverence
1924: that knew no bounds, yet I could never persuade myself to confide in
1925: him that event which was so often present to my recollection, but which
1926: I feared the detail to another would only impress more deeply.
1927: 
1928: M. Krempe was not equally docile; and in my condition at that time, of
1929: almost insupportable sensitiveness, his harsh blunt encomiums gave me even
1930: more pain than the benevolent approbation of M. Waldman. “D—n
1931: the fellow!” cried he; “why, M. Clerval, I assure you he has
1932: outstript us all. Ay, stare if you please; but it is nevertheless true. A
1933: youngster who, but a few years ago, believed in Cornelius Agrippa as firmly
1934: as in the gospel, has now set himself at the head of the university; and if
1935: he is not soon pulled down, we shall all be out of countenance.—Ay,
1936: ay,” continued he, observing my face expressive of suffering,
1937: “M. Frankenstein is modest; an excellent quality in a young man.
1938: Young men should be diffident of themselves, you know, M. Clerval: I was
1939: myself when young; but that wears out in a very short time.”
1940: 
1941: M. Krempe had now commenced an eulogy on himself, which happily turned
1942: the conversation from a subject that was so annoying to me.
1943: 
1944: Clerval had never sympathised in my tastes for natural science; and his
1945: literary pursuits differed wholly from those which had occupied me. He
1946: came to the university with the design of making himself complete
1947: master of the oriental languages, and thus he should open a field for
1948: the plan of life he had marked out for himself. Resolved to pursue no
1949: inglorious career, he turned his eyes toward the East, as affording
1950: scope for his spirit of enterprise. The Persian, Arabic, and Sanskrit
1951: languages engaged his attention, and I was easily induced to enter on
1952: the same studies. Idleness had ever been irksome to me, and now that I
1953: wished to fly from reflection, and hated my former studies, I felt
1954: great relief in being the fellow-pupil with my friend, and found not
1955: only instruction but consolation in the works of the orientalists. I
1956: did not, like him, attempt a critical knowledge of their dialects, for
1957: I did not contemplate making any other use of them than temporary
1958: amusement. I read merely to understand their meaning, and they well
1959: repaid my labours. Their melancholy is soothing, and their joy
1960: elevating, to a degree I never experienced in studying the authors of
1961: any other country. When you read their writings, life appears to
1962: consist in a warm sun and a garden of roses,—in the smiles and frowns
1963: of a fair enemy, and the fire that consumes your own heart. How
1964: different from the manly and heroical poetry of Greece and Rome!
1965: 
1966: Summer passed away in these occupations, and my return to Geneva was
1967: fixed for the latter end of autumn; but being delayed by several
1968: accidents, winter and snow arrived, the roads were deemed impassable,
1969: and my journey was retarded until the ensuing spring. I felt this
1970: delay very bitterly; for I longed to see my native town and my beloved
1971: friends. My return had only been delayed so long, from an
1972: unwillingness to leave Clerval in a strange place, before he had become
1973: acquainted with any of its inhabitants. The winter, however, was spent
1974: cheerfully; and although the spring was uncommonly late, when it came
1975: its beauty compensated for its dilatoriness.
1976: 
1977: The month of May had already commenced, and I expected the letter daily
1978: which was to fix the date of my departure, when Henry proposed a
1979: pedestrian tour in the environs of Ingolstadt, that I might bid a
1980: personal farewell to the country I had so long inhabited. I acceded
1981: with pleasure to this proposition: I was fond of exercise, and Clerval
1982: had always been my favourite companion in the ramble of this nature
1983: that I had taken among the scenes of my native country.
1984: 
1985: We passed a fortnight in these perambulations: my health and spirits
1986: had long been restored, and they gained additional strength from the
1987: salubrious air I breathed, the natural incidents of our progress, and
1988: the conversation of my friend. Study had before secluded me from the
1989: intercourse of my fellow-creatures, and rendered me unsocial; but
1990: Clerval called forth the better feelings of my heart; he again taught
1991: me to love the aspect of nature, and the cheerful faces of children.
1992: Excellent friend! how sincerely you did love me, and endeavour to
1993: elevate my mind until it was on a level with your own. A selfish
1994: pursuit had cramped and narrowed me, until your gentleness and
1995: affection warmed and opened my senses; I became the same happy creature
1996: who, a few years ago, loved and beloved by all, had no sorrow or care.
1997: When happy, inanimate nature had the power of bestowing on me the most
1998: delightful sensations. A serene sky and verdant fields filled me with
1999: ecstasy. The present season was indeed divine; the flowers of spring
2000: bloomed in the hedges, while those of summer were already in bud. I
2001: was undisturbed by thoughts which during the preceding year had pressed
2002: upon me, notwithstanding my endeavours to throw them off, with an
2003: invincible burden.
2004: 
2005: Henry rejoiced in my gaiety, and sincerely sympathised in my feelings: he
2006: exerted himself to amuse me, while he expressed the sensations that filled
2007: his soul. The resources of his mind on this occasion were truly
2008: astonishing: his conversation was full of imagination; and very often, in
2009: imitation of the Persian and Arabic writers, he invented tales of wonderful
2010: fancy and passion. At other times he repeated my favourite poems, or drew
2011: me out into arguments, which he supported with great ingenuity.
2012: 
2013: We returned to our college on a Sunday afternoon: the peasants were
2014: dancing, and every one we met appeared gay and happy. My own spirits were
2015: high, and I bounded along with feelings of unbridled joy and hilarity.
2016: 
2017: 
2018: 
2019: 
2020: Chapter 7
2021: 
2022: 
2023: On my return, I found the following letter from my father:—
2024: 
2025: “My dear Victor,
2026: 
2027: “You have probably waited impatiently for a letter to fix the date of
2028: your return to us; and I was at first tempted to write only a few
2029: lines, merely mentioning the day on which I should expect you. But
2030: that would be a cruel kindness, and I dare not do it. What would be
2031: your surprise, my son, when you expected a happy and glad welcome, to
2032: behold, on the contrary, tears and wretchedness? And how, Victor, can
2033: I relate our misfortune? Absence cannot have rendered you callous to
2034: our joys and griefs; and how shall I inflict pain on my long absent
2035: son? I wish to prepare you for the woeful news, but I know it is
2036: impossible; even now your eye skims over the page to seek the words
2037: which are to convey to you the horrible tidings.
2038: 
2039: “William is dead!—that sweet child, whose smiles delighted and warmed
2040: my heart, who was so gentle, yet so gay! Victor, he is murdered!
2041: 
2042: “I will not attempt to console you; but will simply relate the
2043: circumstances of the transaction.
2044: 
2045: “Last Thursday (May 7th), I, my niece, and your two brothers, went to
2046: walk in Plainpalais. The evening was warm and serene, and we prolonged
2047: our walk farther than usual. It was already dusk before we thought of
2048: returning; and then we discovered that William and Ernest, who had gone
2049: on before, were not to be found. We accordingly rested on a seat until
2050: they should return. Presently Ernest came, and enquired if we had seen
2051: his brother; he said, that he had been playing with him, that William
2052: had run away to hide himself, and that he vainly sought for him, and
2053: afterwards waited for a long time, but that he did not return.
2054: 
2055: “This account rather alarmed us, and we continued to search for him
2056: until night fell, when Elizabeth conjectured that he might have
2057: returned to the house. He was not there. We returned again, with
2058: torches; for I could not rest, when I thought that my sweet boy had
2059: lost himself, and was exposed to all the damps and dews of night;
2060: Elizabeth also suffered extreme anguish. About five in the morning I
2061: discovered my lovely boy, whom the night before I had seen blooming and
2062: active in health, stretched on the grass livid and motionless; the
2063: print of the murder’s finger was on his neck.
2064: 
2065: “He was conveyed home, and the anguish that was visible in my
2066: countenance betrayed the secret to Elizabeth. She was very earnest to
2067: see the corpse. At first I attempted to prevent her but she persisted,
2068: and entering the room where it lay, hastily examined the neck of the
2069: victim, and clasping her hands exclaimed, ‘O God! I have murdered my
2070: darling child!’
2071: 
2072: “She fainted, and was restored with extreme difficulty. When she again
2073: lived, it was only to weep and sigh. She told me, that that same
2074: evening William had teased her to let him wear a very valuable
2075: miniature that she possessed of your mother. This picture is gone, and
2076: was doubtless the temptation which urged the murderer to the deed. We
2077: have no trace of him at present, although our exertions to discover him
2078: are unremitted; but they will not restore my beloved William!
2079: 
2080: “Come, dearest Victor; you alone can console Elizabeth. She weeps
2081: continually, and accuses herself unjustly as the cause of his death;
2082: her words pierce my heart. We are all unhappy; but will not that be an
2083: additional motive for you, my son, to return and be our comforter?
2084: Your dear mother! Alas, Victor! I now say, Thank God she did not live
2085: to witness the cruel, miserable death of her youngest darling!
2086: 
2087: “Come, Victor; not brooding thoughts of vengeance against the assassin,
2088: but with feelings of peace and gentleness, that will heal, instead of
2089: festering, the wounds of our minds. Enter the house of mourning, my
2090: friend, but with kindness and affection for those who love you, and not
2091: with hatred for your enemies.
2092: 
2093: “Your affectionate and afflicted father,
2094: 
2095: “Alphonse Frankenstein.
2096: 
2097: 
2098: 
2099: “Geneva, May 12th, 17—.”
2100: 
2101: 
2102: 
2103: Clerval, who had watched my countenance as I read this letter, was
2104: surprised to observe the despair that succeeded the joy I at first
2105: expressed on receiving news from my friends. I threw the letter on the
2106: table, and covered my face with my hands.
2107: 
2108: “My dear Frankenstein,” exclaimed Henry, when he perceived me
2109: weep with bitterness, “are you always to be unhappy? My dear friend,
2110: what has happened?”
2111: 
2112: I motioned him to take up the letter, while I walked up and down the
2113: room in the extremest agitation. Tears also gushed from the eyes of
2114: Clerval, as he read the account of my misfortune.
2115: 
2116: “I can offer you no consolation, my friend,” said he;
2117: “your disaster is irreparable. What do you intend to do?”
2118: 
2119: “To go instantly to Geneva: come with me, Henry, to order the horses.”
2120: 
2121: During our walk, Clerval endeavoured to say a few words of consolation;
2122: he could only express his heartfelt sympathy. “Poor William!” said he,
2123: “dear lovely child, he now sleeps with his angel mother! Who that had
2124: seen him bright and joyous in his young beauty, but must weep over his
2125: untimely loss! To die so miserably; to feel the murderer’s grasp! How
2126: much more a murdered that could destroy radiant innocence! Poor little
2127: fellow! one only consolation have we; his friends mourn and weep, but
2128: he is at rest. The pang is over, his sufferings are at an end for ever.
2129: A sod covers his gentle form, and he knows no pain. He can no longer
2130: be a subject for pity; we must reserve that for his miserable
2131: survivors.”
2132: 
2133: Clerval spoke thus as we hurried through the streets; the words
2134: impressed themselves on my mind and I remembered them afterwards in
2135: solitude. But now, as soon as the horses arrived, I hurried into a
2136: cabriolet, and bade farewell to my friend.
2137: 
2138: My journey was very melancholy. At first I wished to hurry on, for I longed
2139: to console and sympathise with my loved and sorrowing friends; but when I
2140: drew near my native town, I slackened my progress. I could hardly sustain
2141: the multitude of feelings that crowded into my mind. I passed through
2142: scenes familiar to my youth, but which I had not seen for nearly six years.
2143: How altered every thing might be during that time! One sudden and
2144: desolating change had taken place; but a thousand little circumstances
2145: might have by degrees worked other alterations, which, although they were
2146: done more tranquilly, might not be the less decisive. Fear overcame me; I
2147: dared no advance, dreading a thousand nameless evils that made me tremble,
2148: although I was unable to define them.
2149: 
2150: I remained two days at Lausanne, in this painful state of mind. I
2151: contemplated the lake: the waters were placid; all around was calm; and the
2152: snowy mountains, “the palaces of nature,” were not changed. By
2153: degrees the calm and heavenly scene restored me, and I continued my journey
2154: towards Geneva.
2155: 
2156: The road ran by the side of the lake, which became narrower as I
2157: approached my native town. I discovered more distinctly the black
2158: sides of Jura, and the bright summit of Mont Blanc. I wept like a
2159: child. “Dear mountains! my own beautiful lake! how do you welcome your
2160: wanderer? Your summits are clear; the sky and lake are blue and
2161: placid. Is this to prognosticate peace, or to mock at my unhappiness?”
2162: 
2163: I fear, my friend, that I shall render myself tedious by dwelling on
2164: these preliminary circumstances; but they were days of comparative
2165: happiness, and I think of them with pleasure. My country, my beloved
2166: country! who but a native can tell the delight I took in again
2167: beholding thy streams, thy mountains, and, more than all, thy lovely
2168: lake!
2169: 
2170: Yet, as I drew nearer home, grief and fear again overcame me. Night also
2171: closed around; and when I could hardly see the dark mountains, I felt still
2172: more gloomily. The picture appeared a vast and dim scene of evil, and I
2173: foresaw obscurely that I was destined to become the most wretched of human
2174: beings. Alas! I prophesied truly, and failed only in one single
2175: circumstance, that in all the misery I imagined and dreaded, I did not
2176: conceive the hundredth part of the anguish I was destined to endure.
2177: 
2178: It was completely dark when I arrived in the environs of Geneva; the gates
2179: of the town were already shut; and I was obliged to pass the night at
2180: Secheron, a village at the distance of half a league from the city. The sky
2181: was serene; and, as I was unable to rest, I resolved to visit the spot
2182: where my poor William had been murdered. As I could not pass through the
2183: town, I was obliged to cross the lake in a boat to arrive at Plainpalais.
2184: During this short voyage I saw the lightning playing on the summit of Mont
2185: Blanc in the most beautiful figures. The storm appeared to approach
2186: rapidly, and, on landing, I ascended a low hill, that I might observe its
2187: progress. It advanced; the heavens were clouded, and I soon felt the rain
2188: coming slowly in large drops, but its violence quickly increased.
2189: 
2190: I quitted my seat, and walked on, although the darkness and storm
2191: increased every minute, and the thunder burst with a terrific crash
2192: over my head. It was echoed from Salêve, the Juras, and the Alps of
2193: Savoy; vivid flashes of lightning dazzled my eyes, illuminating the
2194: lake, making it appear like a vast sheet of fire; then for an instant
2195: every thing seemed of a pitchy darkness, until the eye recovered itself
2196: from the preceding flash. The storm, as is often the case in
2197: Switzerland, appeared at once in various parts of the heavens. The
2198: most violent storm hung exactly north of the town, over the part of the
2199: lake which lies between the promontory of Belrive and the village of
2200: Copêt. Another storm enlightened Jura with faint flashes; and another
2201: darkened and sometimes disclosed the Môle, a peaked mountain to the
2202: east of the lake.
2203: 
2204: While I watched the tempest, so beautiful yet terrific, I wandered on with
2205: a hasty step. This noble war in the sky elevated my spirits; I clasped my
2206: hands, and exclaimed aloud, “William, dear angel! this is thy
2207: funeral, this thy dirge!” As I said these words, I perceived in the
2208: gloom a figure which stole from behind a clump of trees near me; I stood
2209: fixed, gazing intently: I could not be mistaken. A flash of lightning
2210: illuminated the object, and discovered its shape plainly to me; its
2211: gigantic stature, and the deformity of its aspect more hideous than belongs
2212: to humanity, instantly informed me that it was the wretch, the filthy
2213: dæmon, to whom I had given life. What did he there? Could he be (I
2214: shuddered at the conception) the murderer of my brother? No sooner did that
2215: idea cross my imagination, than I became convinced of its truth; my teeth
2216: chattered, and I was forced to lean against a tree for support. The figure
2217: passed me quickly, and I lost it in the gloom. Nothing in human shape could
2218: have destroyed the fair child. _He_ was the murderer! I could not
2219: doubt it. The mere presence of the idea was an irresistible proof of the
2220: fact. I thought of pursuing the devil; but it would have been in vain, for
2221: another flash discovered him to me hanging among the rocks of the nearly
2222: perpendicular ascent of Mont Salêve, a hill that bounds Plainpalais on the
2223: south. He soon reached the summit, and disappeared.
2224: 
2225: I remained motionless. The thunder ceased; but the rain still
2226: continued, and the scene was enveloped in an impenetrable darkness. I
2227: revolved in my mind the events which I had until now sought to forget:
2228: the whole train of my progress toward the creation; the appearance of
2229: the works of my own hands at my bedside; its departure. Two years had
2230: now nearly elapsed since the night on which he first received life; and
2231: was this his first crime? Alas! I had turned loose into the world a
2232: depraved wretch, whose delight was in carnage and misery; had he not
2233: murdered my brother?
2234: 
2235: No one can conceive the anguish I suffered during the remainder of the
2236: night, which I spent, cold and wet, in the open air. But I did not
2237: feel the inconvenience of the weather; my imagination was busy in
2238: scenes of evil and despair. I considered the being whom I had cast
2239: among mankind, and endowed with the will and power to effect purposes
2240: of horror, such as the deed which he had now done, nearly in the light
2241: of my own vampire, my own spirit let loose from the grave, and forced
2242: to destroy all that was dear to me.
2243: 
2244: Day dawned; and I directed my steps towards the town. The gates were
2245: open, and I hastened to my father’s house. My first thought was to
2246: discover what I knew of the murderer, and cause instant pursuit to be
2247: made. But I paused when I reflected on the story that I had to tell. A
2248: being whom I myself had formed, and endued with life, had met me at
2249: midnight among the precipices of an inaccessible mountain. I
2250: remembered also the nervous fever with which I had been seized just at
2251: the time that I dated my creation, and which would give an air of
2252: delirium to a tale otherwise so utterly improbable. I well knew that
2253: if any other had communicated such a relation to me, I should have
2254: looked upon it as the ravings of insanity. Besides, the strange nature
2255: of the animal would elude all pursuit, even if I were so far credited
2256: as to persuade my relatives to commence it. And then of what use would
2257: be pursuit? Who could arrest a creature capable of scaling the
2258: overhanging sides of Mont Salêve? These reflections determined me, and
2259: I resolved to remain silent.
2260: 
2261: It was about five in the morning when I entered my father’s house. I
2262: told the servants not to disturb the family, and went into the library
2263: to attend their usual hour of rising.
2264: 
2265: Six years had elapsed, passed in a dream but for one indelible trace, and I
2266: stood in the same place where I had last embraced my father before my
2267: departure for Ingolstadt. Beloved and venerable parent! He still remained
2268: to me. I gazed on the picture of my mother, which stood over the
2269: mantel-piece. It was an historical subject, painted at my father’s
2270: desire, and represented Caroline Beaufort in an agony of despair, kneeling
2271: by the coffin of her dead father. Her garb was rustic, and her cheek pale;
2272: but there was an air of dignity and beauty, that hardly permitted the
2273: sentiment of pity. Below this picture was a miniature of William; and my
2274: tears flowed when I looked upon it. While I was thus engaged, Ernest
2275: entered: he had heard me arrive, and hastened to welcome me:
2276: “Welcome, my dearest Victor,” said he. “Ah! I wish you
2277: had come three months ago, and then you would have found us all joyous and
2278: delighted. You come to us now to share a misery which nothing can
2279: alleviate; yet your presence will, I hope, revive our father, who seems
2280: sinking under his misfortune; and your persuasions will induce poor
2281: Elizabeth to cease her vain and tormenting self-accusations.—Poor
2282: William! he was our darling and our pride!”
2283: 
2284: Tears, unrestrained, fell from my brother’s eyes; a sense of mortal
2285: agony crept over my frame. Before, I had only imagined the
2286: wretchedness of my desolated home; the reality came on me as a new, and
2287: a not less terrible, disaster. I tried to calm Ernest; I enquired more
2288: minutely concerning my father, and here I named my cousin.
2289: 
2290: “She most of all,” said Ernest, “requires consolation; she accused
2291: herself of having caused the death of my brother, and that made her
2292: very wretched. But since the murderer has been discovered—”
2293: 
2294: “The murderer discovered! Good God! how can that be? who could attempt
2295: to pursue him? It is impossible; one might as well try to overtake the
2296: winds, or confine a mountain-stream with a straw. I saw him too; he
2297: was free last night!”
2298: 
2299: “I do not know what you mean,” replied my brother, in accents of
2300: wonder, “but to us the discovery we have made completes our misery. No
2301: one would believe it at first; and even now Elizabeth will not be
2302: convinced, notwithstanding all the evidence. Indeed, who would credit
2303: that Justine Moritz, who was so amiable, and fond of all the family,
2304: could suddenly become so capable of so frightful, so appalling a crime?”
2305: 
2306: “Justine Moritz! Poor, poor girl, is she the accused? But it is
2307: wrongfully; every one knows that; no one believes it, surely, Ernest?”
2308: 
2309: “No one did at first; but several circumstances came out, that have
2310: almost forced conviction upon us; and her own behaviour has been so
2311: confused, as to add to the evidence of facts a weight that, I fear,
2312: leaves no hope for doubt. But she will be tried today, and you will
2313: then hear all.”
2314: 
2315: He then related that, the morning on which the murder of poor William
2316: had been discovered, Justine had been taken ill, and confined to her
2317: bed for several days. During this interval, one of the servants,
2318: happening to examine the apparel she had worn on the night of the
2319: murder, had discovered in her pocket the picture of my mother, which
2320: had been judged to be the temptation of the murderer. The servant
2321: instantly showed it to one of the others, who, without saying a word to
2322: any of the family, went to a magistrate; and, upon their deposition,
2323: Justine was apprehended. On being charged with the fact, the poor girl
2324: confirmed the suspicion in a great measure by her extreme confusion of
2325: manner.
2326: 
2327: This was a strange tale, but it did not shake my faith; and I replied
2328: earnestly, “You are all mistaken; I know the murderer. Justine, poor,
2329: good Justine, is innocent.”
2330: 
2331: At that instant my father entered. I saw unhappiness deeply impressed
2332: on his countenance, but he endeavoured to welcome me cheerfully; and,
2333: after we had exchanged our mournful greeting, would have introduced
2334: some other topic than that of our disaster, had not Ernest exclaimed,
2335: “Good God, papa! Victor says that he knows who was the murderer of
2336: poor William.”
2337: 
2338: “We do also, unfortunately,” replied my father, “for indeed I had
2339: rather have been for ever ignorant than have discovered so much
2340: depravity and ungratitude in one I valued so highly.”
2341: 
2342: “My dear father, you are mistaken; Justine is innocent.”
2343: 
2344: “If she is, God forbid that she should suffer as guilty. She is to be
2345: tried today, and I hope, I sincerely hope, that she will be acquitted.”
2346: 
2347: This speech calmed me. I was firmly convinced in my own mind that
2348: Justine, and indeed every human being, was guiltless of this murder. I
2349: had no fear, therefore, that any circumstantial evidence could be
2350: brought forward strong enough to convict her. My tale was not one to
2351: announce publicly; its astounding horror would be looked upon as
2352: madness by the vulgar. Did any one indeed exist, except I, the
2353: creator, who would believe, unless his senses convinced him, in the
2354: existence of the living monument of presumption and rash ignorance
2355: which I had let loose upon the world?
2356: 
2357: We were soon joined by Elizabeth. Time had altered her since I last
2358: beheld her; it had endowed her with loveliness surpassing the beauty of
2359: her childish years. There was the same candour, the same vivacity, but
2360: it was allied to an expression more full of sensibility and intellect.
2361: She welcomed me with the greatest affection. “Your arrival, my dear
2362: cousin,” said she, “fills me with hope. You perhaps will find some
2363: means to justify my poor guiltless Justine. Alas! who is safe, if she
2364: be convicted of crime? I rely on her innocence as certainly as I do
2365: upon my own. Our misfortune is doubly hard to us; we have not only
2366: lost that lovely darling boy, but this poor girl, whom I sincerely
2367: love, is to be torn away by even a worse fate. If she is condemned, I
2368: never shall know joy more. But she will not, I am sure she will not;
2369: and then I shall be happy again, even after the sad death of my little
2370: William.”
2371: 
2372: “She is innocent, my Elizabeth,” said I, “and that shall
2373: be proved; fear nothing, but let your spirits be cheered by the assurance
2374: of her acquittal.”
2375: 
2376: “How kind and generous you are! every one else believes in her guilt,
2377: and that made me wretched, for I knew that it was impossible: and to
2378: see every one else prejudiced in so deadly a manner rendered me
2379: hopeless and despairing.” She wept.
2380: 
2381: “Dearest niece,” said my father, “dry your tears. If she
2382: is, as you believe, innocent, rely on the justice of our laws, and the
2383: activity with which I shall prevent the slightest shadow of
2384: partiality.”
2385: 
2386: 
2387: 
2388: 
2389: Chapter 8
2390: 
2391: 
2392: We passed a few sad hours until eleven o’clock, when the trial was to
2393: commence. My father and the rest of the family being obliged to attend
2394: as witnesses, I accompanied them to the court. During the whole of
2395: this wretched mockery of justice I suffered living torture. It was to
2396: be decided whether the result of my curiosity and lawless devices would
2397: cause the death of two of my fellow beings: one a smiling babe full of
2398: innocence and joy, the other far more dreadfully murdered, with every
2399: aggravation of infamy that could make the murder memorable in horror.
2400: Justine also was a girl of merit and possessed qualities which promised
2401: to render her life happy; now all was to be obliterated in an
2402: ignominious grave, and I the cause! A thousand times rather would I
2403: have confessed myself guilty of the crime ascribed to Justine, but I
2404: was absent when it was committed, and such a declaration would have
2405: been considered as the ravings of a madman and would not have
2406: exculpated her who suffered through me.
2407: 
2408: The appearance of Justine was calm. She was dressed in mourning, and
2409: her countenance, always engaging, was rendered, by the solemnity of her
2410: feelings, exquisitely beautiful. Yet she appeared confident in
2411: innocence and did not tremble, although gazed on and execrated by
2412: thousands, for all the kindness which her beauty might otherwise have
2413: excited was obliterated in the minds of the spectators by the
2414: imagination of the enormity she was supposed to have committed. She
2415: was tranquil, yet her tranquillity was evidently constrained; and as
2416: her confusion had before been adduced as a proof of her guilt, she
2417: worked up her mind to an appearance of courage. When she entered the
2418: court she threw her eyes round it and quickly discovered where we were
2419: seated. A tear seemed to dim her eye when she saw us, but she quickly
2420: recovered herself, and a look of sorrowful affection seemed to attest
2421: her utter guiltlessness.
2422: 
2423: The trial began, and after the advocate against her had stated the
2424: charge, several witnesses were called. Several strange facts combined
2425: against her, which might have staggered anyone who had not such proof
2426: of her innocence as I had. She had been out the whole of the night on
2427: which the murder had been committed and towards morning had been
2428: perceived by a market-woman not far from the spot where the body of the
2429: murdered child had been afterwards found. The woman asked her what she
2430: did there, but she looked very strangely and only returned a confused
2431: and unintelligible answer. She returned to the house about eight
2432: o’clock, and when one inquired where she had passed the night, she
2433: replied that she had been looking for the child and demanded earnestly
2434: if anything had been heard concerning him. When shown the body, she
2435: fell into violent hysterics and kept her bed for several days. The
2436: picture was then produced which the servant had found in her pocket;
2437: and when Elizabeth, in a faltering voice, proved that it was the same
2438: which, an hour before the child had been missed, she had placed round
2439: his neck, a murmur of horror and indignation filled the court.
2440: 
2441: Justine was called on for her defence. As the trial had proceeded, her
2442: countenance had altered. Surprise, horror, and misery were strongly
2443: expressed. Sometimes she struggled with her tears, but when she was
2444: desired to plead, she collected her powers and spoke in an audible
2445: although variable voice.
2446: 
2447: “God knows,” she said, “how entirely I am innocent. But I
2448: do not pretend that my protestations should acquit me; I rest my innocence
2449: on a plain and simple explanation of the facts which have been adduced
2450: against me, and I hope the character I have always borne will incline my
2451: judges to a favourable interpretation where any circumstance appears
2452: doubtful or suspicious.”
2453: 
2454: She then related that, by the permission of Elizabeth, she had passed
2455: the evening of the night on which the murder had been committed at the
2456: house of an aunt at Chêne, a village situated at about a league from
2457: Geneva. On her return, at about nine o’clock, she met a man who asked
2458: her if she had seen anything of the child who was lost. She was
2459: alarmed by this account and passed several hours in looking for him,
2460: when the gates of Geneva were shut, and she was forced to remain
2461: several hours of the night in a barn belonging to a cottage, being
2462: unwilling to call up the inhabitants, to whom she was well known. Most
2463: of the night she spent here watching; towards morning she believed that
2464: she slept for a few minutes; some steps disturbed her, and she awoke.
2465: It was dawn, and she quitted her asylum, that she might again endeavour
2466: to find my brother. If she had gone near the spot where his body lay,
2467: it was without her knowledge. That she had been bewildered when
2468: questioned by the market-woman was not surprising, since she had passed
2469: a sleepless night and the fate of poor William was yet uncertain.
2470: Concerning the picture she could give no account.
2471: 
2472: “I know,” continued the unhappy victim, “how heavily and
2473: fatally this one circumstance weighs against me, but I have no power of
2474: explaining it; and when I have expressed my utter ignorance, I am only left
2475: to conjecture concerning the probabilities by which it might have been
2476: placed in my pocket. But here also I am checked. I believe that I have no
2477: enemy on earth, and none surely would have been so wicked as to destroy me
2478: wantonly. Did the murderer place it there? I know of no opportunity
2479: afforded him for so doing; or, if I had, why should he have stolen the
2480: jewel, to part with it again so soon?
2481: 
2482: “I commit my cause to the justice of my judges, yet I see no room for
2483: hope. I beg permission to have a few witnesses examined concerning my
2484: character, and if their testimony shall not overweigh my supposed
2485: guilt, I must be condemned, although I would pledge my salvation on my
2486: innocence.”
2487: 
2488: Several witnesses were called who had known her for many years, and
2489: they spoke well of her; but fear and hatred of the crime of which they
2490: supposed her guilty rendered them timorous and unwilling to come
2491: forward. Elizabeth saw even this last resource, her excellent
2492: dispositions and irreproachable conduct, about to fail the accused,
2493: when, although violently agitated, she desired permission to address
2494: the court.
2495: 
2496: “I am,” said she, “the cousin of the unhappy child who
2497: was murdered, or rather his sister, for I was educated by and have lived
2498: with his parents ever since and even long before his birth. It may
2499: therefore be judged indecent in me to come forward on this occasion, but
2500: when I see a fellow creature about to perish through the cowardice of her
2501: pretended friends, I wish to be allowed to speak, that I may say what I
2502: know of her character. I am well acquainted with the accused. I have lived
2503: in the same house with her, at one time for five and at another for nearly
2504: two years. During all that period she appeared to me the most amiable and
2505: benevolent of human creatures. She nursed Madame Frankenstein, my aunt, in
2506: her last illness, with the greatest affection and care and afterwards
2507: attended her own mother during a tedious illness, in a manner that excited
2508: the admiration of all who knew her, after which she again lived in my
2509: uncle’s house, where she was beloved by all the family. She was
2510: warmly attached to the child who is now dead and acted towards him like a
2511: most affectionate mother. For my own part, I do not hesitate to say that,
2512: notwithstanding all the evidence produced against her, I believe and rely
2513: on her perfect innocence. She had no temptation for such an action; as to
2514: the bauble on which the chief proof rests, if she had earnestly desired it,
2515: I should have willingly given it to her, so much do I esteem and value
2516: her.”
2517: 
2518: A murmur of approbation followed Elizabeth’s simple and powerful
2519: appeal, but it was excited by her generous interference, and not in
2520: favour of poor Justine, on whom the public indignation was turned with
2521: renewed violence, charging her with the blackest ingratitude. She
2522: herself wept as Elizabeth spoke, but she did not answer. My own
2523: agitation and anguish was extreme during the whole trial. I believed
2524: in her innocence; I knew it. Could the dæmon who had (I did not for a
2525: minute doubt) murdered my brother also in his hellish sport have
2526: betrayed the innocent to death and ignominy? I could not sustain the
2527: horror of my situation, and when I perceived that the popular voice and
2528: the countenances of the judges had already condemned my unhappy victim,
2529: I rushed out of the court in agony. The tortures of the accused did
2530: not equal mine; she was sustained by innocence, but the fangs of
2531: remorse tore my bosom and would not forgo their hold.
2532: 
2533: I passed a night of unmingled wretchedness. In the morning I went to
2534: the court; my lips and throat were parched. I dared not ask the fatal
2535: question, but I was known, and the officer guessed the cause of my
2536: visit. The ballots had been thrown; they were all black, and Justine
2537: was condemned.
2538: 
2539: I cannot pretend to describe what I then felt. I had before
2540: experienced sensations of horror, and I have endeavoured to bestow upon
2541: them adequate expressions, but words cannot convey an idea of the
2542: heart-sickening despair that I then endured. The person to whom I
2543: addressed myself added that Justine had already confessed her guilt.
2544: “That evidence,” he observed, “was hardly required in so glaring a
2545: case, but I am glad of it, and, indeed, none of our judges like to
2546: condemn a criminal upon circumstantial evidence, be it ever so
2547: decisive.”
2548: 
2549: This was strange and unexpected intelligence; what could it mean? Had
2550: my eyes deceived me? And was I really as mad as the whole world would
2551: believe me to be if I disclosed the object of my suspicions? I
2552: hastened to return home, and Elizabeth eagerly demanded the result.
2553: 
2554: “My cousin,” replied I, “it is decided as you may have expected; all
2555: judges had rather that ten innocent should suffer than that one guilty
2556: should escape. But she has confessed.”
2557: 
2558: This was a dire blow to poor Elizabeth, who had relied with firmness upon
2559: Justine’s innocence. “Alas!” said she. “How shall I
2560: ever again believe in human goodness? Justine, whom I loved and esteemed as
2561: my sister, how could she put on those smiles of innocence only to betray?
2562: Her mild eyes seemed incapable of any severity or guile, and yet she has
2563: committed a murder.”
2564: 
2565: Soon after we heard that the poor victim had expressed a desire to see my
2566: cousin. My father wished her not to go but said that he left it to her own
