2567: judgment and feelings to decide. “Yes,” said Elizabeth,
2568: “I will go, although she is guilty; and you, Victor, shall accompany
2569: me; I cannot go alone.” The idea of this visit was torture to me, yet
2570: I could not refuse.
2571: 
2572: We entered the gloomy prison chamber and beheld Justine sitting on some
2573: straw at the farther end; her hands were manacled, and her head rested on
2574: her knees. She rose on seeing us enter, and when we were left alone with
2575: her, she threw herself at the feet of Elizabeth, weeping bitterly. My
2576: cousin wept also.
2577: 
2578: “Oh, Justine!” said she. “Why did you rob me of my last consolation?
2579: I relied on your innocence, and although I was then very wretched, I
2580: was not so miserable as I am now.”
2581: 
2582: “And do you also believe that I am so very, very wicked? Do you also
2583: join with my enemies to crush me, to condemn me as a murderer?” Her
2584: voice was suffocated with sobs.
2585: 
2586: “Rise, my poor girl,” said Elizabeth; “why do you kneel,
2587: if you are innocent? I am not one of your enemies, I believed you
2588: guiltless, notwithstanding every evidence, until I heard that you had
2589: yourself declared your guilt. That report, you say, is false; and be
2590: assured, dear Justine, that nothing can shake my confidence in you for a
2591: moment, but your own confession.”
2592: 
2593: “I did confess, but I confessed a lie. I confessed, that I might
2594: obtain absolution; but now that falsehood lies heavier at my heart than
2595: all my other sins. The God of heaven forgive me! Ever since I was
2596: condemned, my confessor has besieged me; he threatened and menaced,
2597: until I almost began to think that I was the monster that he said I
2598: was. He threatened excommunication and hell fire in my last moments if
2599: I continued obdurate. Dear lady, I had none to support me; all looked
2600: on me as a wretch doomed to ignominy and perdition. What could I do?
2601: In an evil hour I subscribed to a lie; and now only am I truly
2602: miserable.”
2603: 
2604: She paused, weeping, and then continued, “I thought with horror, my
2605: sweet lady, that you should believe your Justine, whom your blessed
2606: aunt had so highly honoured, and whom you loved, was a creature capable
2607: of a crime which none but the devil himself could have perpetrated.
2608: Dear William! dearest blessed child! I soon shall see you again in
2609: heaven, where we shall all be happy; and that consoles me, going as I
2610: am to suffer ignominy and death.”
2611: 
2612: “Oh, Justine! Forgive me for having for one moment distrusted you.
2613: Why did you confess? But do not mourn, dear girl. Do not fear. I
2614: will proclaim, I will prove your innocence. I will melt the stony
2615: hearts of your enemies by my tears and prayers. You shall not die!
2616: You, my playfellow, my companion, my sister, perish on the scaffold!
2617: No! No! I never could survive so horrible a misfortune.”
2618: 
2619: Justine shook her head mournfully. “I do not fear to die,” she said;
2620: “that pang is past. God raises my weakness and gives me courage to
2621: endure the worst. I leave a sad and bitter world; and if you remember
2622: me and think of me as of one unjustly condemned, I am resigned to the
2623: fate awaiting me. Learn from me, dear lady, to submit in patience to
2624: the will of heaven!”
2625: 
2626: During this conversation I had retired to a corner of the prison room,
2627: where I could conceal the horrid anguish that possessed me. Despair!
2628: Who dared talk of that? The poor victim, who on the morrow was to pass
2629: the awful boundary between life and death, felt not, as I did, such
2630: deep and bitter agony. I gnashed my teeth and ground them together,
2631: uttering a groan that came from my inmost soul. Justine started. When
2632: she saw who it was, she approached me and said, “Dear sir, you are very
2633: kind to visit me; you, I hope, do not believe that I am guilty?”
2634: 
2635: I could not answer. “No, Justine,” said Elizabeth; “he is more
2636: convinced of your innocence than I was, for even when he heard that you
2637: had confessed, he did not credit it.”
2638: 
2639: “I truly thank him. In these last moments I feel the sincerest
2640: gratitude towards those who think of me with kindness. How sweet is
2641: the affection of others to such a wretch as I am! It removes more than
2642: half my misfortune, and I feel as if I could die in peace now that my
2643: innocence is acknowledged by you, dear lady, and your cousin.”
2644: 
2645: Thus the poor sufferer tried to comfort others and herself. She indeed
2646: gained the resignation she desired. But I, the true murderer, felt the
2647: never-dying worm alive in my bosom, which allowed of no hope or
2648: consolation. Elizabeth also wept and was unhappy, but hers also was
2649: the misery of innocence, which, like a cloud that passes over the fair
2650: moon, for a while hides but cannot tarnish its brightness. Anguish and
2651: despair had penetrated into the core of my heart; I bore a hell within
2652: me which nothing could extinguish. We stayed several hours with
2653: Justine, and it was with great difficulty that Elizabeth could tear
2654: herself away. “I wish,” cried she, “that I were to die with you; I
2655: cannot live in this world of misery.”
2656: 
2657: Justine assumed an air of cheerfulness, while she with difficulty
2658: repressed her bitter tears. She embraced Elizabeth and said in a voice
2659: of half-suppressed emotion, “Farewell, sweet lady, dearest Elizabeth,
2660: my beloved and only friend; may heaven, in its bounty, bless and
2661: preserve you; may this be the last misfortune that you will ever
2662: suffer! Live, and be happy, and make others so.”
2663: 
2664: And on the morrow Justine died. Elizabeth’s heart-rending eloquence
2665: failed to move the judges from their settled conviction in the
2666: criminality of the saintly sufferer. My passionate and indignant
2667: appeals were lost upon them. And when I received their cold answers
2668: and heard the harsh, unfeeling reasoning of these men, my purposed
2669: avowal died away on my lips. Thus I might proclaim myself a madman,
2670: but not revoke the sentence passed upon my wretched victim. She
2671: perished on the scaffold as a murderess!
2672: 
2673: From the tortures of my own heart, I turned to contemplate the deep and
2674: voiceless grief of my Elizabeth. This also was my doing! And my
2675: father’s woe, and the desolation of that late so smiling home all was
2676: the work of my thrice-accursed hands! Ye weep, unhappy ones, but these
2677: are not your last tears! Again shall you raise the funeral wail, and
2678: the sound of your lamentations shall again and again be heard!
2679: Frankenstein, your son, your kinsman, your early, much-loved friend; he
2680: who would spend each vital drop of blood for your sakes, who has no
2681: thought nor sense of joy except as it is mirrored also in your dear
2682: countenances, who would fill the air with blessings and spend his life
2683: in serving you—he bids you weep, to shed countless tears; happy beyond
2684: his hopes, if thus inexorable fate be satisfied, and if the destruction
2685: pause before the peace of the grave have succeeded to your sad torments!
2686: 
2687: Thus spoke my prophetic soul, as, torn by remorse, horror, and despair,
2688: I beheld those I loved spend vain sorrow upon the graves of William and
2689: Justine, the first hapless victims to my unhallowed arts.
2690: 
2691: 
2692: 
2693: 
2694: Chapter 9
2695: 
2696: 
2697: Nothing is more painful to the human mind than, after the feelings have
2698: been worked up by a quick succession of events, the dead calmness of
2699: inaction and certainty which follows and deprives the soul both of hope
2700: and fear. Justine died, she rested, and I was alive. The blood flowed
2701: freely in my veins, but a weight of despair and remorse pressed on my
2702: heart which nothing could remove. Sleep fled from my eyes; I wandered
2703: like an evil spirit, for I had committed deeds of mischief beyond
2704: description horrible, and more, much more (I persuaded myself) was yet
2705: behind. Yet my heart overflowed with kindness and the love of virtue.
2706: I had begun life with benevolent intentions and thirsted for the moment
2707: when I should put them in practice and make myself useful to my fellow
2708: beings. Now all was blasted; instead of that serenity of conscience
2709: which allowed me to look back upon the past with self-satisfaction, and
2710: from thence to gather promise of new hopes, I was seized by remorse and
2711: the sense of guilt, which hurried me away to a hell of intense tortures
2712: such as no language can describe.
2713: 
2714: This state of mind preyed upon my health, which had perhaps never
2715: entirely recovered from the first shock it had sustained. I shunned
2716: the face of man; all sound of joy or complacency was torture to me;
2717: solitude was my only consolation—deep, dark, deathlike solitude.
2718: 
2719: My father observed with pain the alteration perceptible in my disposition
2720: and habits and endeavoured by arguments deduced from the feelings of his
2721: serene conscience and guiltless life to inspire me with fortitude and
2722: awaken in me the courage to dispel the dark cloud which brooded over me.
2723: “Do you think, Victor,” said he, “that I do not suffer
2724: also? No one could love a child more than I loved your
2725: brother”—tears came into his eyes as he spoke—“but
2726: is it not a duty to the survivors that we should refrain from augmenting
2727: their unhappiness by an appearance of immoderate grief? It is also a duty
2728: owed to yourself, for excessive sorrow prevents improvement or enjoyment,
2729: or even the discharge of daily usefulness, without which no man is fit for
2730: society.”
2731: 
2732: This advice, although good, was totally inapplicable to my case; I
2733: should have been the first to hide my grief and console my friends if
2734: remorse had not mingled its bitterness, and terror its alarm, with my
2735: other sensations. Now I could only answer my father with a look of
2736: despair and endeavour to hide myself from his view.
2737: 
2738: About this time we retired to our house at Belrive. This change was
2739: particularly agreeable to me. The shutting of the gates regularly at
2740: ten o’clock and the impossibility of remaining on the lake after that
2741: hour had rendered our residence within the walls of Geneva very irksome
2742: to me. I was now free. Often, after the rest of the family had
2743: retired for the night, I took the boat and passed many hours upon the
2744: water. Sometimes, with my sails set, I was carried by the wind; and
2745: sometimes, after rowing into the middle of the lake, I left the boat to
2746: pursue its own course and gave way to my own miserable reflections. I
2747: was often tempted, when all was at peace around me, and I the only
2748: unquiet thing that wandered restless in a scene so beautiful and
2749: heavenly—if I except some bat, or the frogs, whose harsh and
2750: interrupted croaking was heard only when I approached the shore—often,
2751: I say, I was tempted to plunge into the silent lake, that the waters
2752: might close over me and my calamities for ever. But I was restrained,
2753: when I thought of the heroic and suffering Elizabeth, whom I tenderly
2754: loved, and whose existence was bound up in mine. I thought also of my
2755: father and surviving brother; should I by my base desertion leave them
2756: exposed and unprotected to the malice of the fiend whom I had let loose
2757: among them?
2758: 
2759: At these moments I wept bitterly and wished that peace would revisit my
2760: mind only that I might afford them consolation and happiness. But that
2761: could not be. Remorse extinguished every hope. I had been the author of
2762: unalterable evils, and I lived in daily fear lest the monster whom I had
2763: created should perpetrate some new wickedness. I had an obscure feeling
2764: that all was not over and that he would still commit some signal crime,
2765: which by its enormity should almost efface the recollection of the past.
2766: There was always scope for fear so long as anything I loved remained
2767: behind. My abhorrence of this fiend cannot be conceived. When I thought of
2768: him I gnashed my teeth, my eyes became inflamed, and I ardently wished to
2769: extinguish that life which I had so thoughtlessly bestowed. When I
2770: reflected on his crimes and malice, my hatred and revenge burst all bounds
2771: of moderation. I would have made a pilgrimage to the highest peak of the
2772: Andes, could I, when there, have precipitated him to their base. I wished
2773: to see him again, that I might wreak the utmost extent of abhorrence on his
2774: head and avenge the deaths of William and Justine.
2775: 
2776: Our house was the house of mourning. My father’s health was deeply
2777: shaken by the horror of the recent events. Elizabeth was sad and
2778: desponding; she no longer took delight in her ordinary occupations; all
2779: pleasure seemed to her sacrilege toward the dead; eternal woe and tears she
2780: then thought was the just tribute she should pay to innocence so blasted
2781: and destroyed. She was no longer that happy creature who in earlier youth
2782: wandered with me on the banks of the lake and talked with ecstasy of our
2783: future prospects. The first of those sorrows which are sent to wean us from
2784: the earth had visited her, and its dimming influence quenched her dearest
2785: smiles.
2786: 
2787: “When I reflect, my dear cousin,” said she, “on the miserable death of
2788: Justine Moritz, I no longer see the world and its works as they before
2789: appeared to me. Before, I looked upon the accounts of vice and
2790: injustice that I read in books or heard from others as tales of ancient
2791: days or imaginary evils; at least they were remote and more familiar to
2792: reason than to the imagination; but now misery has come home, and men
2793: appear to me as monsters thirsting for each other’s blood. Yet I am
2794: certainly unjust. Everybody believed that poor girl to be guilty; and
2795: if she could have committed the crime for which she suffered, assuredly
2796: she would have been the most depraved of human creatures. For the sake
2797: of a few jewels, to have murdered the son of her benefactor and friend,
2798: a child whom she had nursed from its birth, and appeared to love as if
2799: it had been her own! I could not consent to the death of any human
2800: being, but certainly I should have thought such a creature unfit to
2801: remain in the society of men. But she was innocent. I know, I feel
2802: she was innocent; you are of the same opinion, and that confirms me.
2803: Alas! Victor, when falsehood can look so like the truth, who can
2804: assure themselves of certain happiness? I feel as if I were walking on
2805: the edge of a precipice, towards which thousands are crowding and
2806: endeavouring to plunge me into the abyss. William and Justine were
2807: assassinated, and the murderer escapes; he walks about the world free,
2808: and perhaps respected. But even if I were condemned to suffer on the
2809: scaffold for the same crimes, I would not change places with such a
2810: wretch.”
2811: 
2812: I listened to this discourse with the extremest agony. I, not in deed,
2813: but in effect, was the true murderer. Elizabeth read my anguish in my
2814: countenance, and kindly taking my hand, said, “My dearest friend, you
2815: must calm yourself. These events have affected me, God knows how
2816: deeply; but I am not so wretched as you are. There is an expression of
2817: despair, and sometimes of revenge, in your countenance that makes me
2818: tremble. Dear Victor, banish these dark passions. Remember the
2819: friends around you, who centre all their hopes in you. Have we lost
2820: the power of rendering you happy? Ah! While we love, while we are
2821: true to each other, here in this land of peace and beauty, your native
2822: country, we may reap every tranquil blessing—what can disturb our
2823: peace?”
2824: 
2825: And could not such words from her whom I fondly prized before every
2826: other gift of fortune suffice to chase away the fiend that lurked in my
2827: heart? Even as she spoke I drew near to her, as if in terror, lest at
2828: that very moment the destroyer had been near to rob me of her.
2829: 
2830: Thus not the tenderness of friendship, nor the beauty of earth, nor of
2831: heaven, could redeem my soul from woe; the very accents of love were
2832: ineffectual. I was encompassed by a cloud which no beneficial
2833: influence could penetrate. The wounded deer dragging its fainting
2834: limbs to some untrodden brake, there to gaze upon the arrow which had
2835: pierced it, and to die, was but a type of me.
2836: 
2837: Sometimes I could cope with the sullen despair that overwhelmed me, but
2838: sometimes the whirlwind passions of my soul drove me to seek, by bodily
2839: exercise and by change of place, some relief from my intolerable
2840: sensations. It was during an access of this kind that I suddenly left
2841: my home, and bending my steps towards the near Alpine valleys, sought
2842: in the magnificence, the eternity of such scenes, to forget myself and
2843: my ephemeral, because human, sorrows. My wanderings were directed
2844: towards the valley of Chamounix. I had visited it frequently during my
2845: boyhood. Six years had passed since then: _I_ was a wreck, but nought
2846: had changed in those savage and enduring scenes.
2847: 
2848: I performed the first part of my journey on horseback. I afterwards
2849: hired a mule, as the more sure-footed and least liable to receive
2850: injury on these rugged roads. The weather was fine; it was about the
2851: middle of the month of August, nearly two months after the death of
2852: Justine, that miserable epoch from which I dated all my woe. The
2853: weight upon my spirit was sensibly lightened as I plunged yet deeper in
2854: the ravine of Arve. The immense mountains and precipices that overhung
2855: me on every side, the sound of the river raging among the rocks, and
2856: the dashing of the waterfalls around spoke of a power mighty as
2857: Omnipotence—and I ceased to fear or to bend before any being less
2858: almighty than that which had created and ruled the elements, here
2859: displayed in their most terrific guise. Still, as I ascended higher,
2860: the valley assumed a more magnificent and astonishing character.
2861: Ruined castles hanging on the precipices of piny mountains, the
2862: impetuous Arve, and cottages every here and there peeping forth from
2863: among the trees formed a scene of singular beauty. But it was
2864: augmented and rendered sublime by the mighty Alps, whose white and
2865: shining pyramids and domes towered above all, as belonging to another
2866: earth, the habitations of another race of beings.
2867: 
2868: I passed the bridge of Pélissier, where the ravine, which the river
2869: forms, opened before me, and I began to ascend the mountain that
2870: overhangs it. Soon after, I entered the valley of Chamounix. This
2871: valley is more wonderful and sublime, but not so beautiful and
2872: picturesque as that of Servox, through which I had just passed. The
2873: high and snowy mountains were its immediate boundaries, but I saw no
2874: more ruined castles and fertile fields. Immense glaciers approached
2875: the road; I heard the rumbling thunder of the falling avalanche and
2876: marked the smoke of its passage. Mont Blanc, the supreme and
2877: magnificent Mont Blanc, raised itself from the surrounding _aiguilles_,
2878: and its tremendous _dôme_ overlooked the valley.
2879: 
2880: A tingling long-lost sense of pleasure often came across me during this
2881: journey. Some turn in the road, some new object suddenly perceived and
2882: recognised, reminded me of days gone by, and were associated with the
2883: lighthearted gaiety of boyhood. The very winds whispered in soothing
2884: accents, and maternal Nature bade me weep no more. Then again the
2885: kindly influence ceased to act—I found myself fettered again to grief
2886: and indulging in all the misery of reflection. Then I spurred on my
2887: animal, striving so to forget the world, my fears, and more than all,
2888: myself—or, in a more desperate fashion, I alighted and threw myself on
2889: the grass, weighed down by horror and despair.
2890: 
2891: At length I arrived at the village of Chamounix. Exhaustion succeeded
2892: to the extreme fatigue both of body and of mind which I had endured.
2893: For a short space of time I remained at the window watching the pallid
2894: lightnings that played above Mont Blanc and listening to the rushing of
2895: the Arve, which pursued its noisy way beneath. The same lulling sounds
2896: acted as a lullaby to my too keen sensations; when I placed my head
2897: upon my pillow, sleep crept over me; I felt it as it came and blessed
2898: the giver of oblivion.
2899: 
2900: 
2901: 
2902: 
2903: Chapter 10
2904: 
2905: 
2906: I spent the following day roaming through the valley. I stood beside
2907: the sources of the Arveiron, which take their rise in a glacier, that
2908: with slow pace is advancing down from the summit of the hills to
2909: barricade the valley. The abrupt sides of vast mountains were before
2910: me; the icy wall of the glacier overhung me; a few shattered pines were
2911: scattered around; and the solemn silence of this glorious
2912: presence-chamber of imperial Nature was broken only by the brawling
2913: waves or the fall of some vast fragment, the thunder sound of the
2914: avalanche or the cracking, reverberated along the mountains, of the
2915: accumulated ice, which, through the silent working of immutable laws,
2916: was ever and anon rent and torn, as if it had been but a plaything in
2917: their hands. These sublime and magnificent scenes afforded me the
2918: greatest consolation that I was capable of receiving. They elevated me
2919: from all littleness of feeling, and although they did not remove my
2920: grief, they subdued and tranquillised it. In some degree, also, they
2921: diverted my mind from the thoughts over which it had brooded for the
2922: last month. I retired to rest at night; my slumbers, as it were,
2923: waited on and ministered to by the assemblance of grand shapes which I
2924: had contemplated during the day. They congregated round me; the
2925: unstained snowy mountain-top, the glittering pinnacle, the pine woods,
2926: and ragged bare ravine, the eagle, soaring amidst the clouds—they all
2927: gathered round me and bade me be at peace.
2928: 
2929: Where had they fled when the next morning I awoke? All of
2930: soul-inspiriting fled with sleep, and dark melancholy clouded every
2931: thought. The rain was pouring in torrents, and thick mists hid the
2932: summits of the mountains, so that I even saw not the faces of those
2933: mighty friends. Still I would penetrate their misty veil and seek them
2934: in their cloudy retreats. What were rain and storm to me? My mule was
2935: brought to the door, and I resolved to ascend to the summit of
2936: Montanvert. I remembered the effect that the view of the tremendous
2937: and ever-moving glacier had produced upon my mind when I first saw it.
2938: It had then filled me with a sublime ecstasy that gave wings to the
2939: soul and allowed it to soar from the obscure world to light and joy.
2940: The sight of the awful and majestic in nature had indeed always the
2941: effect of solemnising my mind and causing me to forget the passing
2942: cares of life. I determined to go without a guide, for I was well
2943: acquainted with the path, and the presence of another would destroy the
2944: solitary grandeur of the scene.
2945: 
2946: The ascent is precipitous, but the path is cut into continual and short
2947: windings, which enable you to surmount the perpendicularity of the
2948: mountain. It is a scene terrifically desolate. In a thousand spots
2949: the traces of the winter avalanche may be perceived, where trees lie
2950: broken and strewed on the ground, some entirely destroyed, others bent,
2951: leaning upon the jutting rocks of the mountain or transversely upon
2952: other trees. The path, as you ascend higher, is intersected by ravines
2953: of snow, down which stones continually roll from above; one of them is
2954: particularly dangerous, as the slightest sound, such as even speaking
2955: in a loud voice, produces a concussion of air sufficient to draw
2956: destruction upon the head of the speaker. The pines are not tall or
2957: luxuriant, but they are sombre and add an air of severity to the scene.
2958: I looked on the valley beneath; vast mists were rising from the rivers
2959: which ran through it and curling in thick wreaths around the opposite
2960: mountains, whose summits were hid in the uniform clouds, while rain
2961: poured from the dark sky and added to the melancholy impression I
2962: received from the objects around me. Alas! Why does man boast of
2963: sensibilities superior to those apparent in the brute; it only renders
2964: them more necessary beings. If our impulses were confined to hunger,
2965: thirst, and desire, we might be nearly free; but now we are moved by
2966: every wind that blows and a chance word or scene that that word may
2967: convey to us.
2968: 
2969:   We rest; a dream has power to poison sleep.
2970:      We rise; one wand’ring thought pollutes the day.
2971:   We feel, conceive, or reason; laugh or weep,
2972:      Embrace fond woe, or cast our cares away;
2973:   It is the same: for, be it joy or sorrow,
2974:      The path of its departure still is free.
2975:   Man’s yesterday may ne’er be like his morrow;
2976:      Nought may endure but mutability!
2977: 
2978: 
2979: It was nearly noon when I arrived at the top of the ascent. For some
2980: time I sat upon the rock that overlooks the sea of ice. A mist covered
2981: both that and the surrounding mountains. Presently a breeze dissipated
2982: the cloud, and I descended upon the glacier. The surface is very
2983: uneven, rising like the waves of a troubled sea, descending low, and
2984: interspersed by rifts that sink deep. The field of ice is almost a
2985: league in width, but I spent nearly two hours in crossing it. The
2986: opposite mountain is a bare perpendicular rock. From the side where I
2987: now stood Montanvert was exactly opposite, at the distance of a league;
2988: and above it rose Mont Blanc, in awful majesty. I remained in a recess
2989: of the rock, gazing on this wonderful and stupendous scene. The sea,
2990: or rather the vast river of ice, wound among its dependent mountains,
2991: whose aerial summits hung over its recesses. Their icy and glittering
2992: peaks shone in the sunlight over the clouds. My heart, which was
2993: before sorrowful, now swelled with something like joy; I exclaimed,
2994: “Wandering spirits, if indeed ye wander, and do not rest in your narrow
2995: beds, allow me this faint happiness, or take me, as your companion,
2996: away from the joys of life.”
2997: 
2998: As I said this I suddenly beheld the figure of a man, at some distance,
2999: advancing towards me with superhuman speed. He bounded over the
3000: crevices in the ice, among which I had walked with caution; his
3001: stature, also, as he approached, seemed to exceed that of man. I was
3002: troubled; a mist came over my eyes, and I felt a faintness seize me,
3003: but I was quickly restored by the cold gale of the mountains. I
3004: perceived, as the shape came nearer (sight tremendous and abhorred!)
3005: that it was the wretch whom I had created. I trembled with rage and
3006: horror, resolving to wait his approach and then close with him in
3007: mortal combat. He approached; his countenance bespoke bitter anguish,
3008: combined with disdain and malignity, while its unearthly ugliness
3009: rendered it almost too horrible for human eyes. But I scarcely
3010: observed this; rage and hatred had at first deprived me of utterance,
3011: and I recovered only to overwhelm him with words expressive of furious
3012: detestation and contempt.
3013: 
3014: “Devil,” I exclaimed, “do you dare approach me? And do
3015: not you fear the fierce vengeance of my arm wreaked on your miserable head?
3016: Begone, vile insect! Or rather, stay, that I may trample you to dust! And,
3017: oh! That I could, with the extinction of your miserable existence, restore
3018: those victims whom you have so diabolically murdered!”
3019: 
3020: “I expected this reception,” said the dæmon. “All men hate the
3021: wretched; how, then, must I be hated, who am miserable beyond all
3022: living things! Yet you, my creator, detest and spurn me, thy creature,
3023: to whom thou art bound by ties only dissoluble by the annihilation of
3024: one of us. You purpose to kill me. How dare you sport thus with life?
3025: Do your duty towards me, and I will do mine towards you and the rest of
3026: mankind. If you will comply with my conditions, I will leave them and
3027: you at peace; but if you refuse, I will glut the maw of death, until it
3028: be satiated with the blood of your remaining friends.”
3029: 
3030: “Abhorred monster! Fiend that thou art! The tortures of hell are too
3031: mild a vengeance for thy crimes. Wretched devil! You reproach me with
3032: your creation, come on, then, that I may extinguish the spark which I
3033: so negligently bestowed.”
3034: 
3035: My rage was without bounds; I sprang on him, impelled by all the
3036: feelings which can arm one being against the existence of another.
3037: 
3038: He easily eluded me and said,
3039: 
3040: “Be calm! I entreat you to hear me before you give vent to your hatred
3041: on my devoted head. Have I not suffered enough, that you seek to
3042: increase my misery? Life, although it may only be an accumulation of
3043: anguish, is dear to me, and I will defend it. Remember, thou hast made
3044: me more powerful than thyself; my height is superior to thine, my
3045: joints more supple. But I will not be tempted to set myself in
3046: opposition to thee. I am thy creature, and I will be even mild and
3047: docile to my natural lord and king if thou wilt also perform thy part,
3048: the which thou owest me. Oh, Frankenstein, be not equitable to every
3049: other and trample upon me alone, to whom thy justice, and even thy
3050: clemency and affection, is most due. Remember that I am thy creature;
3051: I ought to be thy Adam, but I am rather the fallen angel, whom thou
3052: drivest from joy for no misdeed. Everywhere I see bliss, from which I
3053: alone am irrevocably excluded. I was benevolent and good; misery made
3054: me a fiend. Make me happy, and I shall again be virtuous.”
3055: 
3056: “Begone! I will not hear you. There can be no community between you
3057: and me; we are enemies. Begone, or let us try our strength in a fight,
3058: in which one must fall.”
3059: 
3060: “How can I move thee? Will no entreaties cause thee to turn a
3061: favourable eye upon thy creature, who implores thy goodness and
3062: compassion? Believe me, Frankenstein, I was benevolent; my soul glowed
3063: with love and humanity; but am I not alone, miserably alone? You, my
3064: creator, abhor me; what hope can I gather from your fellow creatures,
3065: who owe me nothing? They spurn and hate me. The desert mountains and
3066: dreary glaciers are my refuge. I have wandered here many days; the
3067: caves of ice, which I only do not fear, are a dwelling to me, and the
3068: only one which man does not grudge. These bleak skies I hail, for they
3069: are kinder to me than your fellow beings. If the multitude of mankind
3070: knew of my existence, they would do as you do, and arm themselves for
3071: my destruction. Shall I not then hate them who abhor me? I will keep
3072: no terms with my enemies. I am miserable, and they shall share my
3073: wretchedness. Yet it is in your power to recompense me, and deliver
3074: them from an evil which it only remains for you to make so great, that
3075: not only you and your family, but thousands of others, shall be
3076: swallowed up in the whirlwinds of its rage. Let your compassion be
3077: moved, and do not disdain me. Listen to my tale; when you have heard
3078: that, abandon or commiserate me, as you shall judge that I deserve.
3079: But hear me. The guilty are allowed, by human laws, bloody as they
3080: are, to speak in their own defence before they are condemned. Listen
3081: to me, Frankenstein. You accuse me of murder, and yet you would, with
3082: a satisfied conscience, destroy your own creature. Oh, praise the
3083: eternal justice of man! Yet I ask you not to spare me; listen to me,
3084: and then, if you can, and if you will, destroy the work of your hands.”
3085: 
3086: “Why do you call to my remembrance,” I rejoined, “circumstances of
3087: which I shudder to reflect, that I have been the miserable origin and
3088: author? Cursed be the day, abhorred devil, in which you first saw
3089: light! Cursed (although I curse myself) be the hands that formed you!
3090: You have made me wretched beyond expression. You have left me no power
3091: to consider whether I am just to you or not. Begone! Relieve me from
3092: the sight of your detested form.”
3093: 
3094: “Thus I relieve thee, my creator,” he said, and placed his hated hands
3095: before my eyes, which I flung from me with violence; “thus I take from
3096: thee a sight which you abhor. Still thou canst listen to me and grant
3097: me thy compassion. By the virtues that I once possessed, I demand this
3098: from you. Hear my tale; it is long and strange, and the temperature of
3099: this place is not fitting to your fine sensations; come to the hut upon
3100: the mountain. The sun is yet high in the heavens; before it descends
3101: to hide itself behind your snowy precipices and illuminate another
3102: world, you will have heard my story and can decide. On you it rests,
3103: whether I quit for ever the neighbourhood of man and lead a harmless
3104: life, or become the scourge of your fellow creatures and the author of
3105: your own speedy ruin.”
3106: 
3107: As he said this he led the way across the ice; I followed. My heart
3108: was full, and I did not answer him, but as I proceeded, I weighed the
3109: various arguments that he had used and determined at least to listen to
3110: his tale. I was partly urged by curiosity, and compassion confirmed my
3111: resolution. I had hitherto supposed him to be the murderer of my
3112: brother, and I eagerly sought a confirmation or denial of this opinion.
3113: For the first time, also, I felt what the duties of a creator towards
3114: his creature were, and that I ought to render him happy before I
3115: complained of his wickedness. These motives urged me to comply with
3116: his demand. We crossed the ice, therefore, and ascended the opposite
3117: rock. The air was cold, and the rain again began to descend; we
3118: entered the hut, the fiend with an air of exultation, I with a heavy
3119: heart and depressed spirits. But I consented to listen, and seating
3120: myself by the fire which my odious companion had lighted, he thus began
3121: his tale.
3122: 
3123: 
3124: 
3125: 
3126: Chapter 11
3127: 
3128: 
3129: “It is with considerable difficulty that I remember the original era of
3130: my being; all the events of that period appear confused and indistinct.
3131: A strange multiplicity of sensations seized me, and I saw, felt, heard,
3132: and smelt at the same time; and it was, indeed, a long time before I
3133: learned to distinguish between the operations of my various senses. By
3134: degrees, I remember, a stronger light pressed upon my nerves, so that I
3135: was obliged to shut my eyes. Darkness then came over me and troubled
3136: me, but hardly had I felt this when, by opening my eyes, as I now
3137: suppose, the light poured in upon me again. I walked and, I believe,
3138: descended, but I presently found a great alteration in my sensations.
3139: Before, dark and opaque bodies had surrounded me, impervious to my
3140: touch or sight; but I now found that I could wander on at liberty, with
3141: no obstacles which I could not either surmount or avoid. The light
3142: became more and more oppressive to me, and the heat wearying me as I
3143: walked, I sought a place where I could receive shade. This was the
3144: forest near Ingolstadt; and here I lay by the side of a brook resting
3145: from my fatigue, until I felt tormented by hunger and thirst. This
3146: roused me from my nearly dormant state, and I ate some berries which I
3147: found hanging on the trees or lying on the ground. I slaked my thirst
3148: at the brook, and then lying down, was overcome by sleep.
3149: 
3150: “It was dark when I awoke; I felt cold also, and half frightened, as it
3151: were, instinctively, finding myself so desolate. Before I had quitted
3152: your apartment, on a sensation of cold, I had covered myself with some
3153: clothes, but these were insufficient to secure me from the dews of
3154: night. I was a poor, helpless, miserable wretch; I knew, and could
3155: distinguish, nothing; but feeling pain invade me on all sides, I sat
3156: down and wept.
3157: 
3158: “Soon a gentle light stole over the heavens and gave me a sensation of
3159: pleasure. I started up and beheld a radiant form rise from among the
3160: trees. [The moon] I gazed with a kind of wonder. It moved slowly,
3161: but it enlightened my path, and I again went out in search of berries.
3162: I was still cold when under one of the trees I found a huge cloak, with
3163: which I covered myself, and sat down upon the ground. No distinct
3164: ideas occupied my mind; all was confused. I felt light, and hunger,
3165: and thirst, and darkness; innumerable sounds rang in my ears, and on
3166: all sides various scents saluted me; the only object that I could
3167: distinguish was the bright moon, and I fixed my eyes on that with
3168: pleasure.
3169: 
3170: “Several changes of day and night passed, and the orb of night had
3171: greatly lessened, when I began to distinguish my sensations from each
3172: other. I gradually saw plainly the clear stream that supplied me with
3173: drink and the trees that shaded me with their foliage. I was delighted
3174: when I first discovered that a pleasant sound, which often saluted my
3175: ears, proceeded from the throats of the little winged animals who had
3176: often intercepted the light from my eyes. I began also to observe,
3177: with greater accuracy, the forms that surrounded me and to perceive the
3178: boundaries of the radiant roof of light which canopied me. Sometimes I
3179: tried to imitate the pleasant songs of the birds but was unable.
3180: Sometimes I wished to express my sensations in my own mode, but the
3181: uncouth and inarticulate sounds which broke from me frightened me into
3182: silence again.
3183: 
3184: “The moon had disappeared from the night, and again, with a lessened
3185: form, showed itself, while I still remained in the forest. My
3186: sensations had by this time become distinct, and my mind received every
3187: day additional ideas. My eyes became accustomed to the light and to
3188: perceive objects in their right forms; I distinguished the insect from
3189: the herb, and by degrees, one herb from another. I found that the
3190: sparrow uttered none but harsh notes, whilst those of the blackbird and
3191: thrush were sweet and enticing.
3192: 
3193: “One day, when I was oppressed by cold, I found a fire which had been
3194: left by some wandering beggars, and was overcome with delight at the
3195: warmth I experienced from it. In my joy I thrust my hand into the live
3196: embers, but quickly drew it out again with a cry of pain. How strange,
3197: I thought, that the same cause should produce such opposite effects! I
3198: examined the materials of the fire, and to my joy found it to be
3199: composed of wood. I quickly collected some branches, but they were wet
3200: and would not burn. I was pained at this and sat still watching the
3201: operation of the fire. The wet wood which I had placed near the heat
3202: dried and itself became inflamed. I reflected on this, and by touching
3203: the various branches, I discovered the cause and busied myself in
3204: collecting a great quantity of wood, that I might dry it and have a
3205: plentiful supply of fire. When night came on and brought sleep with
3206: it, I was in the greatest fear lest my fire should be extinguished. I
3207: covered it carefully with dry wood and leaves and placed wet branches
3208: upon it; and then, spreading my cloak, I lay on the ground and sank
3209: into sleep.
3210: 
3211: “It was morning when I awoke, and my first care was to visit the fire.
3212: I uncovered it, and a gentle breeze quickly fanned it into a flame. I
3213: observed this also and contrived a fan of branches, which roused the
3214: embers when they were nearly extinguished. When night came again I
3215: found, with pleasure, that the fire gave light as well as heat and that
3216: the discovery of this element was useful to me in my food, for I found
3217: some of the offals that the travellers had left had been roasted, and
3218: tasted much more savoury than the berries I gathered from the trees. I
3219: tried, therefore, to dress my food in the same manner, placing it on
3220: the live embers. I found that the berries were spoiled by this
3221: operation, and the nuts and roots much improved.
3222: 
3223: “Food, however, became scarce, and I often spent the whole day
3224: searching in vain for a few acorns to assuage the pangs of hunger. When
3225: I found this, I resolved to quit the place that I had hitherto
3226: inhabited, to seek for one where the few wants I experienced would be
3227: more easily satisfied. In this emigration I exceedingly lamented the
3228: loss of the fire which I had obtained through accident and knew not how
3229: to reproduce it. I gave several hours to the serious consideration of
3230: this difficulty, but I was obliged to relinquish all attempt to supply
3231: it, and wrapping myself up in my cloak, I struck across the wood
3232: towards the setting sun. I passed three days in these rambles and at
3233: length discovered the open country. A great fall of snow had taken
3234: place the night before, and the fields were of one uniform white; the
3235: appearance was disconsolate, and I found my feet chilled by the cold
3236: damp substance that covered the ground.
3237: 
3238: “It was about seven in the morning, and I longed to obtain food and
3239: shelter; at length I perceived a small hut, on a rising ground, which
3240: had doubtless been built for the convenience of some shepherd. This
3241: was a new sight to me, and I examined the structure with great
3242: curiosity. Finding the door open, I entered. An old man sat in it,
3243: near a fire, over which he was preparing his breakfast. He turned on
3244: hearing a noise, and perceiving me, shrieked loudly, and quitting the
3245: hut, ran across the fields with a speed of which his debilitated form
3246: hardly appeared capable. His appearance, different from any I had ever
3247: before seen, and his flight somewhat surprised me. But I was enchanted
3248: by the appearance of the hut; here the snow and rain could not
3249: penetrate; the ground was dry; and it presented to me then as exquisite
3250: and divine a retreat as Pandæmonium appeared to the dæmons of hell
3251: after their sufferings in the lake of fire. I greedily devoured the
3252: remnants of the shepherd’s breakfast, which consisted of bread, cheese,
3253: milk, and wine; the latter, however, I did not like. Then, overcome by
3254: fatigue, I lay down among some straw and fell asleep.
3255: 
3256: “It was noon when I awoke, and allured by the warmth of the sun, which
3257: shone brightly on the white ground, I determined to recommence my
3258: travels; and, depositing the remains of the peasant’s breakfast in a
3259: wallet I found, I proceeded across the fields for several hours, until
3260: at sunset I arrived at a village. How miraculous did this appear! The
3261: huts, the neater cottages, and stately houses engaged my admiration by
3262: turns. The vegetables in the gardens, the milk and cheese that I saw
3263: placed at the windows of some of the cottages, allured my appetite. One
3264: of the best of these I entered, but I had hardly placed my foot within
3265: the door before the children shrieked, and one of the women fainted.
3266: The whole village was roused; some fled, some attacked me, until,
3267: grievously bruised by stones and many other kinds of missile weapons, I
3268: escaped to the open country and fearfully took refuge in a low hovel,
3269: quite bare, and making a wretched appearance after the palaces I had
3270: beheld in the village. This hovel however, joined a cottage of a neat
3271: and pleasant appearance, but after my late dearly bought experience, I
3272: dared not enter it. My place of refuge was constructed of wood, but so
3273: low that I could with difficulty sit upright in it. No wood, however,
3274: was placed on the earth, which formed the floor, but it was dry; and
3275: although the wind entered it by innumerable chinks, I found it an
3276: agreeable asylum from the snow and rain.
3277: 
3278: “Here, then, I retreated and lay down happy to have found a shelter,
3279: however miserable, from the inclemency of the season, and still more
3280: from the barbarity of man. As soon as morning dawned I crept from my
3281: kennel, that I might view the adjacent cottage and discover if I could
3282: remain in the habitation I had found. It was situated against the back
3283: of the cottage and surrounded on the sides which were exposed by a pig
3284: sty and a clear pool of water. One part was open, and by that I had
3285: crept in; but now I covered every crevice by which I might be perceived
3286: with stones and wood, yet in such a manner that I might move them on
3287: occasion to pass out; all the light I enjoyed came through the sty, and
3288: that was sufficient for me.
3289: 
3290: “Having thus arranged my dwelling and carpeted it with clean straw, I
3291: retired, for I saw the figure of a man at a distance, and I remembered
3292: too well my treatment the night before to trust myself in his power. I
3293: had first, however, provided for my sustenance for that day by a loaf
3294: of coarse bread, which I purloined, and a cup with which I could drink
3295: more conveniently than from my hand of the pure water which flowed by
3296: my retreat. The floor was a little raised, so that it was kept
3297: perfectly dry, and by its vicinity to the chimney of the cottage it was
3298: tolerably warm.
3299: 
3300: “Being thus provided, I resolved to reside in this hovel until
3301: something should occur which might alter my determination. It was
3302: indeed a paradise compared to the bleak forest, my former residence,
3303: the rain-dropping branches, and dank earth. I ate my breakfast with
3304: pleasure and was about to remove a plank to procure myself a little
3305: water when I heard a step, and looking through a small chink, I beheld
3306: a young creature, with a pail on her head, passing before my hovel. The
3307: girl was young and of gentle demeanour, unlike what I have since found
3308: cottagers and farmhouse servants to be. Yet she was meanly dressed, a
3309: coarse blue petticoat and a linen jacket being her only garb; her fair
3310: hair was plaited but not adorned: she looked patient yet sad. I lost
3311: sight of her, and in about a quarter of an hour she returned bearing
3312: the pail, which was now partly filled with milk. As she walked along,
3313: seemingly incommoded by the burden, a young man met her, whose
3314: countenance expressed a deeper despondence. Uttering a few sounds with
3315: an air of melancholy, he took the pail from her head and bore it to the
3316: cottage himself. She followed, and they disappeared. Presently I saw
3317: the young man again, with some tools in his hand, cross the field
3318: behind the cottage; and the girl was also busied, sometimes in the
3319: house and sometimes in the yard.
3320: 
3321: “On examining my dwelling, I found that one of the windows of the
3322: cottage had formerly occupied a part of it, but the panes had been
3323: filled up with wood. In one of these was a small and almost
3324: imperceptible chink through which the eye could just penetrate.
3325: Through this crevice a small room was visible, whitewashed and clean
3326: but very bare of furniture. In one corner, near a small fire, sat an
3327: old man, leaning his head on his hands in a disconsolate attitude. The
3328: young girl was occupied in arranging the cottage; but presently she
3329: took something out of a drawer, which employed her hands, and she sat
3330: down beside the old man, who, taking up an instrument, began to play
3331: and to produce sounds sweeter than the voice of the thrush or the
3332: nightingale. It was a lovely sight, even to me, poor wretch who had
3333: never beheld aught beautiful before. The silver hair and benevolent
3334: countenance of the aged cottager won my reverence, while the gentle
3335: manners of the girl enticed my love. He played a sweet mournful air
3336: which I perceived drew tears from the eyes of his amiable companion, of
3337: which the old man took no notice, until she sobbed audibly; he then
3338: pronounced a few sounds, and the fair creature, leaving her work, knelt
3339: at his feet. He raised her and smiled with such kindness and affection
3340: that I felt sensations of a peculiar and overpowering nature; they were
3341: a mixture of pain and pleasure, such as I had never before experienced,
3342: either from hunger or cold, warmth or food; and I withdrew from the
3343: window, unable to bear these emotions.
3344: 
3345: “Soon after this the young man returned, bearing on his shoulders a
3346: load of wood. The girl met him at the door, helped to relieve him of
3347: his burden, and taking some of the fuel into the cottage, placed it on
3348: the fire; then she and the youth went apart into a nook of the cottage,
3349: and he showed her a large loaf and a piece of cheese. She seemed
3350: pleased and went into the garden for some roots and plants, which she
3351: placed in water, and then upon the fire. She afterwards continued her
3352: work, whilst the young man went into the garden and appeared busily
3353: employed in digging and pulling up roots. After he had been employed
3354: thus about an hour, the young woman joined him and they entered the
3355: cottage together.
3356: 
3357: “The old man had, in the meantime, been pensive, but on the appearance
3358: of his companions he assumed a more cheerful air, and they sat down to
3359: eat. The meal was quickly dispatched. The young woman was again
3360: occupied in arranging the cottage, the old man walked before the
3361: cottage in the sun for a few minutes, leaning on the arm of the youth.
3362: Nothing could exceed in beauty the contrast between these two excellent
3363: creatures. One was old, with silver hairs and a countenance beaming
3364: with benevolence and love; the younger was slight and graceful in his
3365: figure, and his features were moulded with the finest symmetry, yet his
3366: eyes and attitude expressed the utmost sadness and despondency. The
3367: old man returned to the cottage, and the youth, with tools different
3368: from those he had used in the morning, directed his steps across the
3369: fields.
3370: 
3371: “Night quickly shut in, but to my extreme wonder, I found that the
3372: cottagers had a means of prolonging light by the use of tapers, and was
3373: delighted to find that the setting of the sun did not put an end to the
3374: pleasure I experienced in watching my human neighbours. In the evening
3375: the young girl and her companion were employed in various occupations
3376: which I did not understand; and the old man again took up the
3377: instrument which produced the divine sounds that had enchanted me in
3378: the morning. So soon as he had finished, the youth began, not to play,
3379: but to utter sounds that were monotonous, and neither resembling the
3380: harmony of the old man’s instrument nor the songs of the birds; I since
3381: found that he read aloud, but at that time I knew nothing of the
3382: science of words or letters.
3383: 
3384: “The family, after having been thus occupied for a short time,
3385: extinguished their lights and retired, as I conjectured, to rest.”
3386: 
3387: 
3388: 
3389: 
3390: Chapter 12
3391: 
3392: 
3393: “I lay on my straw, but I could not sleep. I thought of the
3394: occurrences of the day. What chiefly struck me was the gentle manners
3395: of these people, and I longed to join them, but dared not. I
3396: remembered too well the treatment I had suffered the night before from
3397: the barbarous villagers, and resolved, whatever course of conduct I
3398: might hereafter think it right to pursue, that for the present I would
3399: remain quietly in my hovel, watching and endeavouring to discover the
3400: motives which influenced their actions.
3401: 
3402: “The cottagers arose the next morning before the sun. The young woman
3403: arranged the cottage and prepared the food, and the youth departed
3404: after the first meal.
3405: 
3406: “This day was passed in the same routine as that which preceded it.
3407: The young man was constantly employed out of doors, and the girl in
3408: various laborious occupations within. The old man, whom I soon
3409: perceived to be blind, employed his leisure hours on his instrument or
3410: in contemplation. Nothing could exceed the love and respect which the
3411: younger cottagers exhibited towards their venerable companion. They
3412: performed towards him every little office of affection and duty with
3413: gentleness, and he rewarded them by his benevolent smiles.
3414: 
3415: “They were not entirely happy. The young man and his companion often
3416: went apart and appeared to weep. I saw no cause for their unhappiness,
3417: but I was deeply affected by it. If such lovely creatures were
3418: miserable, it was less strange that I, an imperfect and solitary being,
3419: should be wretched. Yet why were these gentle beings unhappy? They
3420: possessed a delightful house (for such it was in my eyes) and every
3421: luxury; they had a fire to warm them when chill and delicious viands
3422: when hungry; they were dressed in excellent clothes; and, still more,
3423: they enjoyed one another’s company and speech, interchanging each day
3424: looks of affection and kindness. What did their tears imply? Did they
3425: really express pain? I was at first unable to solve these questions,
3426: but perpetual attention and time explained to me many appearances which
3427: were at first enigmatic.
3428: 
3429: “A considerable period elapsed before I discovered one of the causes of
3430: the uneasiness of this amiable family: it was poverty, and they
3431: suffered that evil in a very distressing degree. Their nourishment
3432: consisted entirely of the vegetables of their garden and the milk of
3433: one cow, which gave very little during the winter, when its masters
3434: could scarcely procure food to support it. They often, I believe,
3435: suffered the pangs of hunger very poignantly, especially the two
3436: younger cottagers, for several times they placed food before the old
3437: man when they reserved none for themselves.
3438: 
3439: “This trait of kindness moved me sensibly. I had been accustomed,
3440: during the night, to steal a part of their store for my own
3441: consumption, but when I found that in doing this I inflicted pain on
3442: the cottagers, I abstained and satisfied myself with berries, nuts, and
3443: roots which I gathered from a neighbouring wood.
3444: 
3445: “I discovered also another means through which I was enabled to assist
3446: their labours. I found that the youth spent a great part of each day
3447: in collecting wood for the family fire, and during the night I often
3448: took his tools, the use of which I quickly discovered, and brought home
3449: firing sufficient for the consumption of several days.
3450: 
3451: “I remember, the first time that I did this, the young woman, when she
3452: opened the door in the morning, appeared greatly astonished on seeing a great
3453: pile of wood on the outside. She uttered some words in a loud voice, and the
3454: youth joined her, who also expressed surprise. I observed, with pleasure,
3455: that he did not go to the forest that day, but spent it in repairing the
3456: cottage and cultivating the garden.
3457: 
3458: “By degrees I made a discovery of still greater moment. I found that
3459: these people possessed a method of communicating their experience and
3460: feelings to one another by articulate sounds. I perceived that the words
3461: they spoke sometimes produced pleasure or pain, smiles or sadness, in the
3462: minds and countenances of the hearers. This was indeed a godlike science,
3463: and I ardently desired to become acquainted with it. But I was baffled in
3464: every attempt I made for this purpose. Their pronunciation was quick, and
3465: the words they uttered, not having any apparent connection with visible
3466: objects, I was unable to discover any clue by which I could unravel the
3467: mystery of their reference. By great application, however, and after having
3468: remained during the space of several revolutions of the moon in my hovel, I
3469: discovered the names that were given to some of the most familiar objects of
3470: discourse; I learned and applied the words, _fire, milk, bread,_ and
3471: _wood._ I learned also the names of the cottagers themselves. The youth
3472: and his companion had each of them several names, but the old man had only
3473: one, which was _father._ The girl was called _sister_ or
3474: _Agatha,_ and the youth _Felix, brother,_ or _son_. I cannot
3475: describe the delight I felt when I learned the ideas appropriated to each of
3476: these sounds and was able to pronounce them. I distinguished several other
3477: words without being able as yet to understand or apply them, such as _good,
3478: dearest, unhappy._
3479: 
3480: “I spent the winter in this manner. The gentle manners and beauty of
3481: the cottagers greatly endeared them to me; when they were unhappy, I
3482: felt depressed; when they rejoiced, I sympathised in their joys. I saw
3483: few human beings besides them, and if any other happened to enter the
3484: cottage, their harsh manners and rude gait only enhanced to me the
3485: superior accomplishments of my friends. The old man, I could perceive,
3486: often endeavoured to encourage his children, as sometimes I found that
3487: he called them, to cast off their melancholy. He would talk in a
3488: cheerful accent, with an expression of goodness that bestowed pleasure
3489: even upon me. Agatha listened with respect, her eyes sometimes filled
3490: with tears, which she endeavoured to wipe away unperceived; but I
3491: generally found that her countenance and tone were more cheerful after
3492: having listened to the exhortations of her father. It was not thus
3493: with Felix. He was always the saddest of the group, and even to my
3494: unpractised senses, he appeared to have suffered more deeply than his
3495: friends. But if his countenance was more sorrowful, his voice was more
3496: cheerful than that of his sister, especially when he addressed the old
3497: man.
3498: 
3499: “I could mention innumerable instances which, although slight, marked
3500: the dispositions of these amiable cottagers. In the midst of poverty
3501: and want, Felix carried with pleasure to his sister the first little
3502: white flower that peeped out from beneath the snowy ground. Early in
3503: the morning, before she had risen, he cleared away the snow that
3504: obstructed her path to the milk-house, drew water from the well, and
3505: brought the wood from the outhouse, where, to his perpetual
3506: astonishment, he found his store always replenished by an invisible
3507: hand. In the day, I believe, he worked sometimes for a neighbouring
3508: farmer, because he often went forth and did not return until dinner,
3509: yet brought no wood with him. At other times he worked in the garden,
3510: but as there was little to do in the frosty season, he read to the old
3511: man and Agatha.
3512: 
3513: “This reading had puzzled me extremely at first, but by degrees I
3514: discovered that he uttered many of the same sounds when he read as when
3515: he talked. I conjectured, therefore, that he found on the paper signs
3516: for speech which he understood, and I ardently longed to comprehend
3517: these also; but how was that possible when I did not even understand
3518: the sounds for which they stood as signs? I improved, however,
3519: sensibly in this science, but not sufficiently to follow up any kind of
3520: conversation, although I applied my whole mind to the endeavour, for I
3521: easily perceived that, although I eagerly longed to discover myself to
3522: the cottagers, I ought not to make the attempt until I had first become
3523: master of their language, which knowledge might enable me to make them
3524: overlook the deformity of my figure, for with this also the contrast
3525: perpetually presented to my eyes had made me acquainted.
3526: 
3527: “I had admired the perfect forms of my cottagers—their grace, beauty,
3528: and delicate complexions; but how was I terrified when I viewed myself
3529: in a transparent pool! At first I started back, unable to believe that
3530: it was indeed I who was reflected in the mirror; and when I became
3531: fully convinced that I was in reality the monster that I am, I was
3532: filled with the bitterest sensations of despondence and mortification.
3533: Alas! I did not yet entirely know the fatal effects of this miserable
3534: deformity.
3535: 
3536: “As the sun became warmer and the light of day longer, the snow
3537: vanished, and I beheld the bare trees and the black earth. From this
3538: time Felix was more employed, and the heart-moving indications of
3539: impending famine disappeared. Their food, as I afterwards found, was
3540: coarse, but it was wholesome; and they procured a sufficiency of it.
3541: Several new kinds of plants sprang up in the garden, which they
3542: dressed; and these signs of comfort increased daily as the season
3543: advanced.
3544: 
3545: “The old man, leaning on his son, walked each day at noon, when it did
3546: not rain, as I found it was called when the heavens poured forth its
3547: waters. This frequently took place, but a high wind quickly dried the
3548: earth, and the season became far more pleasant than it had been.
3549: 
3550: “My mode of life in my hovel was uniform. During the morning I
3551: attended the motions of the cottagers, and when they were dispersed in
3552: various occupations, I slept; the remainder of the day was spent in
3553: observing my friends. When they had retired to rest, if there was any
3554: moon or the night was star-light, I went into the woods and collected
3555: my own food and fuel for the cottage. When I returned, as often as it
3556: was necessary, I cleared their path from the snow and performed those
3557: offices that I had seen done by Felix. I afterwards found that these
3558: labours, performed by an invisible hand, greatly astonished them; and
3559: once or twice I heard them, on these occasions, utter the words _good
3560: spirit, wonderful_; but I did not then understand the signification
3561: of these terms.
3562: 
3563: “My thoughts now became more active, and I longed to discover the
3564: motives and feelings of these lovely creatures; I was inquisitive to
3565: know why Felix appeared so miserable and Agatha so sad. I thought
3566: (foolish wretch!) that it might be in my power to restore happiness to
3567: these deserving people. When I slept or was absent, the forms of the
3568: venerable blind father, the gentle Agatha, and the excellent Felix
3569: flitted before me. I looked upon them as superior beings who would be
3570: the arbiters of my future destiny. I formed in my imagination a
3571: thousand pictures of presenting myself to them, and their reception of
3572: me. I imagined that they would be disgusted, until, by my gentle
3573: demeanour and conciliating words, I should first win their favour and
3574: afterwards their love.
3575: 
3576: “These thoughts exhilarated me and led me to apply with fresh ardour to
3577: the acquiring the art of language. My organs were indeed harsh, but
3578: supple; and although my voice was very unlike the soft music of their
3579: tones, yet I pronounced such words as I understood with tolerable ease.
3580: It was as the ass and the lap-dog; yet surely the gentle ass whose
3581: intentions were affectionate, although his manners were rude, deserved
3582: better treatment than blows and execration.
3583: 
3584: “The pleasant showers and genial warmth of spring greatly altered the
3585: aspect of the earth. Men who before this change seemed to have been
3586: hid in caves dispersed themselves and were employed in various arts of
3587: cultivation. The birds sang in more cheerful notes, and the leaves
3588: began to bud forth on the trees. Happy, happy earth! Fit habitation
3589: for gods, which, so short a time before, was bleak, damp, and
3590: unwholesome. My spirits were elevated by the enchanting appearance of
3591: nature; the past was blotted from my memory, the present was tranquil,
3592: and the future gilded by bright rays of hope and anticipations of joy.”
3593: 
3594: 
3595: 
3596: 
3597: Chapter 13
3598: 
3599: 
3600: “I now hasten to the more moving part of my story. I shall relate
3601: events that impressed me with feelings which, from what I had been,
3602: have made me what I am.
3603: 
3604: “Spring advanced rapidly; the weather became fine and the skies
3605: cloudless. It surprised me that what before was desert and gloomy
3606: should now bloom with the most beautiful flowers and verdure. My
3607: senses were gratified and refreshed by a thousand scents of delight and
3608: a thousand sights of beauty.
3609: 
3610: “It was on one of these days, when my cottagers periodically rested
3611: from labour—the old man played on his guitar, and the children
3612: listened to him—that I observed the countenance of Felix was
3613: melancholy beyond expression; he sighed frequently, and once his father
3614: paused in his music, and I conjectured by his manner that he inquired
3615: the cause of his son’s sorrow. Felix replied in a cheerful accent, and
3616: the old man was recommencing his music when someone tapped at the door.
3617: 
3618: “It was a lady on horseback, accompanied by a country-man as a guide.
3619: The lady was dressed in a dark suit and covered with a thick black
3620: veil. Agatha asked a question, to which the stranger only replied by
3621: pronouncing, in a sweet accent, the name of Felix. Her voice was
3622: musical but unlike that of either of my friends. On hearing this word,
3623: Felix came up hastily to the lady, who, when she saw him, threw up her
3624: veil, and I beheld a countenance of angelic beauty and expression. Her
3625: hair of a shining raven black, and curiously braided; her eyes were
3626: dark, but gentle, although animated; her features of a regular
3627: proportion, and her complexion wondrously fair, each cheek tinged with
3628: a lovely pink.
3629: 
3630: “Felix seemed ravished with delight when he saw her, every trait of
3631: sorrow vanished from his face, and it instantly expressed a degree of
3632: ecstatic joy, of which I could hardly have believed it capable; his
3633: eyes sparkled, as his cheek flushed with pleasure; and at that moment I
3634: thought him as beautiful as the stranger. She appeared affected by
3635: different feelings; wiping a few tears from her lovely eyes, she held
3636: out her hand to Felix, who kissed it rapturously and called her, as
3637: well as I could distinguish, his sweet Arabian. She did not appear to
3638: understand him, but smiled. He assisted her to dismount, and
3639: dismissing her guide, conducted her into the cottage. Some
3640: conversation took place between him and his father, and the young
3641: stranger knelt at the old man’s feet and would have kissed his hand,
3642: but he raised her and embraced her affectionately.
3643: 
3644: “I soon perceived that although the stranger uttered articulate sounds
3645: and appeared to have a language of her own, she was neither understood
3646: by nor herself understood the cottagers. They made many signs which I
3647: did not comprehend, but I saw that her presence diffused gladness
3648: through the cottage, dispelling their sorrow as the sun dissipates the
3649: morning mists. Felix seemed peculiarly happy and with smiles of
3650: delight welcomed his Arabian. Agatha, the ever-gentle Agatha, kissed
3651: the hands of the lovely stranger, and pointing to her brother, made
3652: signs which appeared to me to mean that he had been sorrowful until she
3653: came. Some hours passed thus, while they, by their countenances,
3654: expressed joy, the cause of which I did not comprehend. Presently I
3655: found, by the frequent recurrence of some sound which the stranger
3656: repeated after them, that she was endeavouring to learn their language;
3657: and the idea instantly occurred to me that I should make use of the
3658: same instructions to the same end. The stranger learned about twenty
3659: words at the first lesson; most of them, indeed, were those which I had
3660: before understood, but I profited by the others.
3661: 
3662: “As night came on, Agatha and the Arabian retired early. When they
3663: separated Felix kissed the hand of the stranger and said, ‘Good night
3664: sweet Safie.’ He sat up much longer, conversing with his father, and
3665: by the frequent repetition of her name I conjectured that their lovely
3666: guest was the subject of their conversation. I ardently desired to
3667: understand them, and bent every faculty towards that purpose, but found
3668: it utterly impossible.
3669: 
3670: “The next morning Felix went out to his work, and after the usual
3671: occupations of Agatha were finished, the Arabian sat at the feet of the
3672: old man, and taking his guitar, played some airs so entrancingly
3673: beautiful that they at once drew tears of sorrow and delight from my
3674: eyes. She sang, and her voice flowed in a rich cadence, swelling or
3675: dying away like a nightingale of the woods.
3676: 
3677: “When she had finished, she gave the guitar to Agatha, who at first
3678: declined it. She played a simple air, and her voice accompanied it in
3679: sweet accents, but unlike the wondrous strain of the stranger. The old
3680: man appeared enraptured and said some words which Agatha endeavoured to
3681: explain to Safie, and by which he appeared to wish to express that she
3682: bestowed on him the greatest delight by her music.
3683: 
3684: “The days now passed as peaceably as before, with the sole alteration
3685: that joy had taken place of sadness in the countenances of my friends.
3686: Safie was always gay and happy; she and I improved rapidly in the
3687: knowledge of language, so that in two months I began to comprehend most
3688: of the words uttered by my protectors.
3689: 
3690: “In the meanwhile also the black ground was covered with herbage, and
3691: the green banks interspersed with innumerable flowers, sweet to the
3692: scent and the eyes, stars of pale radiance among the moonlight woods;
3693: the sun became warmer, the nights clear and balmy; and my nocturnal
3694: rambles were an extreme pleasure to me, although they were considerably
3695: shortened by the late setting and early rising of the sun, for I never
3696: ventured abroad during daylight, fearful of meeting with the same
3697: treatment I had formerly endured in the first village which I entered.
3698: 
3699: “My days were spent in close attention, that I might more speedily
3700: master the language; and I may boast that I improved more rapidly than
3701: the Arabian, who understood very little and conversed in broken
3702: accents, whilst I comprehended and could imitate almost every word that
3703: was spoken.
3704: 
3705: “While I improved in speech, I also learned the science of letters as
3706: it was taught to the stranger, and this opened before me a wide field
3707: for wonder and delight.
3708: 
3709: “The book from which Felix instructed Safie was Volney’s _Ruins
3710: of Empires_. I should not have understood the purport of this book had not
3711: Felix, in reading it, given very minute explanations. He had chosen this
3712: work, he said, because the declamatory style was framed in imitation of the
3713: Eastern authors. Through this work I obtained a cursory knowledge of history
3714: and a view of the several empires at present existing in the world; it gave
3715: me an insight into the manners, governments, and religions of the different
3716: nations of the earth. I heard of the slothful Asiatics, of the stupendous
3717: genius and mental activity of the Grecians, of the wars and wonderful virtue
3718: of the early Romans—of their subsequent degenerating—of the
3719: decline of that mighty empire, of chivalry, Christianity, and kings. I heard
3720: of the discovery of the American hemisphere and wept with Safie over the
3721: hapless fate of its original inhabitants.
3722: 
3723: “These wonderful narrations inspired me with strange feelings. Was
3724: man, indeed, at once so powerful, so virtuous and magnificent, yet so
3725: vicious and base? He appeared at one time a mere scion of the evil
3726: principle and at another as all that can be conceived of noble and
3727: godlike. To be a great and virtuous man appeared the highest honour
3728: that can befall a sensitive being; to be base and vicious, as many on
3729: record have been, appeared the lowest degradation, a condition more
3730: abject than that of the blind mole or harmless worm. For a long time I
3731: could not conceive how one man could go forth to murder his fellow, or
3732: even why there were laws and governments; but when I heard details of
3733: vice and bloodshed, my wonder ceased and I turned away with disgust and
3734: loathing.
3735: 
3736: “Every conversation of the cottagers now opened new wonders to me.
3737: While I listened to the instructions which Felix bestowed upon the
3738: Arabian, the strange system of human society was explained to me. I
3739: heard of the division of property, of immense wealth and squalid
3740: poverty, of rank, descent, and noble blood.
3741: 
3742: “The words induced me to turn towards myself. I learned that the
3743: possessions most esteemed by your fellow creatures were high and
3744: unsullied descent united with riches. A man might be respected with
3745: only one of these advantages, but without either he was considered,
3746: except in very rare instances, as a vagabond and a slave, doomed to
3747: waste his powers for the profits of the chosen few! And what was I? Of
3748: my creation and creator I was absolutely ignorant, but I knew that I
3749: possessed no money, no friends, no kind of property. I was, besides,
3750: endued with a figure hideously deformed and loathsome; I was not even
3751: of the same nature as man. I was more agile than they and could
3752: subsist upon coarser diet; I bore the extremes of heat and cold with
3753: less injury to my frame; my stature far exceeded theirs. When I looked
3754: around I saw and heard of none like me. Was I, then, a monster, a blot
3755: upon the earth, from which all men fled and whom all men disowned?
3756: 
3757: “I cannot describe to you the agony that these reflections inflicted
3758: upon me; I tried to dispel them, but sorrow only increased with
3759: knowledge. Oh, that I had for ever remained in my native wood, nor
3760: known nor felt beyond the sensations of hunger, thirst, and heat!
3761: 
3762: “Of what a strange nature is knowledge! It clings to the mind when it
3763: has once seized on it like a lichen on the rock. I wished sometimes to
3764: shake off all thought and feeling, but I learned that there was but one
3765: means to overcome the sensation of pain, and that was death—a state
3766: which I feared yet did not understand. I admired virtue and good
3767: feelings and loved the gentle manners and amiable qualities of my
3768: cottagers, but I was shut out from intercourse with them, except
3769: through means which I obtained by stealth, when I was unseen and
3770: unknown, and which rather increased than satisfied the desire I had of
3771: becoming one among my fellows. The gentle words of Agatha and the
3772: animated smiles of the charming Arabian were not for me. The mild
3773: exhortations of the old man and the lively conversation of the loved
3774: Felix were not for me. Miserable, unhappy wretch!
3775: 
3776: “Other lessons were impressed upon me even more deeply. I heard of the
3777: difference of sexes, and the birth and growth of children, how the
3778: father doted on the smiles of the infant, and the lively sallies of the
3779: older child, how all the life and cares of the mother were wrapped up
3780: in the precious charge, how the mind of youth expanded and gained
3781: knowledge, of brother, sister, and all the various relationships which
3782: bind one human being to another in mutual bonds.
3783: 
3784: “But where were my friends and relations? No father had watched my
3785: infant days, no mother had blessed me with smiles and caresses; or if
3786: they had, all my past life was now a blot, a blind vacancy in which I
3787: distinguished nothing. From my earliest remembrance I had been as I
3788: then was in height and proportion. I had never yet seen a being
3789: resembling me or who claimed any intercourse with me. What was I? The
