3790: question again recurred, to be answered only with groans.
3791: 
3792: “I will soon explain to what these feelings tended, but allow me now to
3793: return to the cottagers, whose story excited in me such various
3794: feelings of indignation, delight, and wonder, but which all terminated
3795: in additional love and reverence for my protectors (for so I loved, in
3796: an innocent, half-painful self-deceit, to call them).”
3797: 
3798: 
3799: 
3800: 
3801: Chapter 14
3802: 
3803: 
3804: “Some time elapsed before I learned the history of my friends. It was
3805: one which could not fail to impress itself deeply on my mind, unfolding
3806: as it did a number of circumstances, each interesting and wonderful to
3807: one so utterly inexperienced as I was.
3808: 
3809: “The name of the old man was De Lacey. He was descended from a good
3810: family in France, where he had lived for many years in affluence,
3811: respected by his superiors and beloved by his equals. His son was bred
3812: in the service of his country, and Agatha had ranked with ladies of the
3813: highest distinction. A few months before my arrival they had lived in
3814: a large and luxurious city called Paris, surrounded by friends and
3815: possessed of every enjoyment which virtue, refinement of intellect, or
3816: taste, accompanied by a moderate fortune, could afford.
3817: 
3818: “The father of Safie had been the cause of their ruin. He was a
3819: Turkish merchant and had inhabited Paris for many years, when, for some
3820: reason which I could not learn, he became obnoxious to the government.
3821: He was seized and cast into prison the very day that Safie arrived from
3822: Constantinople to join him. He was tried and condemned to death. The
3823: injustice of his sentence was very flagrant; all Paris was indignant;
3824: and it was judged that his religion and wealth rather than the crime
3825: alleged against him had been the cause of his condemnation.
3826: 
3827: “Felix had accidentally been present at the trial; his horror and
3828: indignation were uncontrollable when he heard the decision of the
3829: court. He made, at that moment, a solemn vow to deliver him and then
3830: looked around for the means. After many fruitless attempts to gain
3831: admittance to the prison, he found a strongly grated window in an
3832: unguarded part of the building, which lighted the dungeon of the
3833: unfortunate Muhammadan, who, loaded with chains, waited in despair the
3834: execution of the barbarous sentence. Felix visited the grate at night
3835: and made known to the prisoner his intentions in his favour. The Turk,
3836: amazed and delighted, endeavoured to kindle the zeal of his deliverer
3837: by promises of reward and wealth. Felix rejected his offers with
3838: contempt, yet when he saw the lovely Safie, who was allowed to visit
3839: her father and who by her gestures expressed her lively gratitude, the
3840: youth could not help owning to his own mind that the captive possessed
3841: a treasure which would fully reward his toil and hazard.
3842: 
3843: “The Turk quickly perceived the impression that his daughter had made
3844: on the heart of Felix and endeavoured to secure him more entirely in
3845: his interests by the promise of her hand in marriage so soon as he
3846: should be conveyed to a place of safety. Felix was too delicate to
3847: accept this offer, yet he looked forward to the probability of the
3848: event as to the consummation of his happiness.
3849: 
3850: “During the ensuing days, while the preparations were going forward for
3851: the escape of the merchant, the zeal of Felix was warmed by several
3852: letters that he received from this lovely girl, who found means to
3853: express her thoughts in the language of her lover by the aid of an old
3854: man, a servant of her father who understood French. She thanked him in
3855: the most ardent terms for his intended services towards her parent, and
3856: at the same time she gently deplored her own fate.
3857: 
3858: “I have copies of these letters, for I found means, during my residence
3859: in the hovel, to procure the implements of writing; and the letters
3860: were often in the hands of Felix or Agatha. Before I depart I will
3861: give them to you; they will prove the truth of my tale; but at present,
3862: as the sun is already far declined, I shall only have time to repeat
3863: the substance of them to you.
3864: 
3865: “Safie related that her mother was a Christian Arab, seized and made a
3866: slave by the Turks; recommended by her beauty, she had won the heart of
3867: the father of Safie, who married her. The young girl spoke in high and
3868: enthusiastic terms of her mother, who, born in freedom, spurned the
3869: bondage to which she was now reduced. She instructed her daughter in
3870: the tenets of her religion and taught her to aspire to higher powers of
3871: intellect and an independence of spirit forbidden to the female
3872: followers of Muhammad. This lady died, but her lessons were indelibly
3873: impressed on the mind of Safie, who sickened at the prospect of again
3874: returning to Asia and being immured within the walls of a harem,
3875: allowed only to occupy herself with infantile amusements, ill-suited to
3876: the temper of her soul, now accustomed to grand ideas and a noble
3877: emulation for virtue. The prospect of marrying a Christian and
3878: remaining in a country where women were allowed to take a rank in
3879: society was enchanting to her.
3880: 
3881: “The day for the execution of the Turk was fixed, but on the night
3882: previous to it he quitted his prison and before morning was distant
3883: many leagues from Paris. Felix had procured passports in the name of
3884: his father, sister, and himself. He had previously communicated his
3885: plan to the former, who aided the deceit by quitting his house, under
3886: the pretence of a journey and concealed himself, with his daughter, in
3887: an obscure part of Paris.
3888: 
3889: “Felix conducted the fugitives through France to Lyons and across Mont
3890: Cenis to Leghorn, where the merchant had decided to wait a favourable
3891: opportunity of passing into some part of the Turkish dominions.
3892: 
3893: “Safie resolved to remain with her father until the moment of his
3894: departure, before which time the Turk renewed his promise that she
3895: should be united to his deliverer; and Felix remained with them in
3896: expectation of that event; and in the meantime he enjoyed the society
3897: of the Arabian, who exhibited towards him the simplest and tenderest
3898: affection. They conversed with one another through the means of an
3899: interpreter, and sometimes with the interpretation of looks; and Safie
3900: sang to him the divine airs of her native country.
3901: 
3902: “The Turk allowed this intimacy to take place and encouraged the hopes
3903: of the youthful lovers, while in his heart he had formed far other
3904: plans. He loathed the idea that his daughter should be united to a
3905: Christian, but he feared the resentment of Felix if he should appear
3906: lukewarm, for he knew that he was still in the power of his deliverer
3907: if he should choose to betray him to the Italian state which they
3908: inhabited. He revolved a thousand plans by which he should be enabled
3909: to prolong the deceit until it might be no longer necessary, and
3910: secretly to take his daughter with him when he departed. His plans
3911: were facilitated by the news which arrived from Paris.
3912: 
3913: “The government of France were greatly enraged at the escape of their
3914: victim and spared no pains to detect and punish his deliverer. The
3915: plot of Felix was quickly discovered, and De Lacey and Agatha were
3916: thrown into prison. The news reached Felix and roused him from his
3917: dream of pleasure. His blind and aged father and his gentle sister lay
3918: in a noisome dungeon while he enjoyed the free air and the society of
3919: her whom he loved. This idea was torture to him. He quickly arranged
3920: with the Turk that if the latter should find a favourable opportunity
3921: for escape before Felix could return to Italy, Safie should remain as a
3922: boarder at a convent at Leghorn; and then, quitting the lovely Arabian,
3923: he hastened to Paris and delivered himself up to the vengeance of the
3924: law, hoping to free De Lacey and Agatha by this proceeding.
3925: 
3926: “He did not succeed. They remained confined for five months before the
3927: trial took place, the result of which deprived them of their fortune
3928: and condemned them to a perpetual exile from their native country.
3929: 
3930: “They found a miserable asylum in the cottage in Germany, where I
3931: discovered them. Felix soon learned that the treacherous Turk, for
3932: whom he and his family endured such unheard-of oppression, on
3933: discovering that his deliverer was thus reduced to poverty and ruin,
3934: became a traitor to good feeling and honour and had quitted Italy with
3935: his daughter, insultingly sending Felix a pittance of money to aid him,
3936: as he said, in some plan of future maintenance.
3937: 
3938: “Such were the events that preyed on the heart of Felix and rendered
3939: him, when I first saw him, the most miserable of his family. He could
3940: have endured poverty, and while this distress had been the meed of his
3941: virtue, he gloried in it; but the ingratitude of the Turk and the loss
3942: of his beloved Safie were misfortunes more bitter and irreparable. The
3943: arrival of the Arabian now infused new life into his soul.
3944: 
3945: “When the news reached Leghorn that Felix was deprived of his wealth
3946: and rank, the merchant commanded his daughter to think no more of her
3947: lover, but to prepare to return to her native country. The generous
3948: nature of Safie was outraged by this command; she attempted to
3949: expostulate with her father, but he left her angrily, reiterating his
3950: tyrannical mandate.
3951: 
3952: “A few days after, the Turk entered his daughter’s apartment and told
3953: her hastily that he had reason to believe that his residence at Leghorn
3954: had been divulged and that he should speedily be delivered up to the
3955: French government; he had consequently hired a vessel to convey him to
3956: Constantinople, for which city he should sail in a few hours. He
3957: intended to leave his daughter under the care of a confidential
3958: servant, to follow at her leisure with the greater part of his
3959: property, which had not yet arrived at Leghorn.
3960: 
3961: “When alone, Safie resolved in her own mind the plan of conduct that it
3962: would become her to pursue in this emergency. A residence in Turkey
3963: was abhorrent to her; her religion and her feelings were alike averse
3964: to it. By some papers of her father which fell into her hands she
3965: heard of the exile of her lover and learnt the name of the spot where
3966: he then resided. She hesitated some time, but at length she formed her
3967: determination. Taking with her some jewels that belonged to her and a
3968: sum of money, she quitted Italy with an attendant, a native of Leghorn,
3969: but who understood the common language of Turkey, and departed for
3970: Germany.
3971: 
3972: “She arrived in safety at a town about twenty leagues from the cottage
3973: of De Lacey, when her attendant fell dangerously ill. Safie nursed her
3974: with the most devoted affection, but the poor girl died, and the
3975: Arabian was left alone, unacquainted with the language of the country
3976: and utterly ignorant of the customs of the world. She fell, however,
3977: into good hands. The Italian had mentioned the name of the spot for
3978: which they were bound, and after her death the woman of the house in
3979: which they had lived took care that Safie should arrive in safety at
3980: the cottage of her lover.”
3981: 
3982: 
3983: 
3984: 
3985: Chapter 15
3986: 
3987: 
3988: “Such was the history of my beloved cottagers. It impressed me deeply.
3989: I learned, from the views of social life which it developed, to admire
3990: their virtues and to deprecate the vices of mankind.
3991: 
3992: “As yet I looked upon crime as a distant evil, benevolence and
3993: generosity were ever present before me, inciting within me a desire to
3994: become an actor in the busy scene where so many admirable qualities
3995: were called forth and displayed. But in giving an account of the
3996: progress of my intellect, I must not omit a circumstance which occurred
3997: in the beginning of the month of August of the same year.
3998: 
3999: “One night during my accustomed visit to the neighbouring wood where I
4000: collected my own food and brought home firing for my protectors, I found on
4001: the ground a leathern portmanteau containing several articles of dress and
4002: some books. I eagerly seized the prize and returned with it to my hovel. 
4003: Fortunately the books were written in the language, the elements of which I
4004: had acquired at the cottage; they consisted of _Paradise Lost_, a volume
4005: of _Plutarch’s Lives_, and the _Sorrows of Werter_. The
4006: possession of these treasures gave me extreme delight; I now continually
4007: studied and exercised my mind upon these histories, whilst my friends were
4008: employed in their ordinary occupations.
4009: 
4010: “I can hardly describe to you the effect of these books. They produced
4011: in me an infinity of new images and feelings, that sometimes raised me
4012: to ecstasy, but more frequently sunk me into the lowest dejection. In
4013: the _Sorrows of Werter_, besides the interest of its simple and affecting
4014: story, so many opinions are canvassed and so many lights thrown upon
4015: what had hitherto been to me obscure subjects that I found in it a
4016: never-ending source of speculation and astonishment. The gentle and
4017: domestic manners it described, combined with lofty sentiments and
4018: feelings, which had for their object something out of self, accorded
4019: well with my experience among my protectors and with the wants which
4020: were for ever alive in my own bosom. But I thought Werter himself a
4021: more divine being than I had ever beheld or imagined; his character
4022: contained no pretension, but it sank deep. The disquisitions upon
4023: death and suicide were calculated to fill me with wonder. I did not
4024: pretend to enter into the merits of the case, yet I inclined towards
4025: the opinions of the hero, whose extinction I wept, without precisely
4026: understanding it.
4027: 
4028: “As I read, however, I applied much personally to my own feelings and
4029: condition. I found myself similar yet at the same time strangely
4030: unlike to the beings concerning whom I read and to whose conversation I
4031: was a listener. I sympathised with and partly understood them, but I
4032: was unformed in mind; I was dependent on none and related to none.
4033: ‘The path of my departure was free,’ and there was none to lament my
4034: annihilation. My person was hideous and my stature gigantic. What did
4035: this mean? Who was I? What was I? Whence did I come? What was my
4036: destination? These questions continually recurred, but I was unable to
4037: solve them.
4038: 
4039: “The volume of _Plutarch’s Lives_ which I possessed contained the
4040: histories of the first founders of the ancient republics. This book
4041: had a far different effect upon me from the _Sorrows of Werter_. I
4042: learned from Werter’s imaginations despondency and gloom, but Plutarch
4043: taught me high thoughts; he elevated me above the wretched sphere of my
4044: own reflections, to admire and love the heroes of past ages. Many
4045: things I read surpassed my understanding and experience. I had a very
4046: confused knowledge of kingdoms, wide extents of country, mighty rivers,
4047: and boundless seas. But I was perfectly unacquainted with towns and
4048: large assemblages of men. The cottage of my protectors had been the
4049: only school in which I had studied human nature, but this book
4050: developed new and mightier scenes of action. I read of men concerned
4051: in public affairs, governing or massacring their species. I felt the
4052: greatest ardour for virtue rise within me, and abhorrence for vice, as
4053: far as I understood the signification of those terms, relative as they
4054: were, as I applied them, to pleasure and pain alone. Induced by these
4055: feelings, I was of course led to admire peaceable lawgivers, Numa,
4056: Solon, and Lycurgus, in preference to Romulus and Theseus. The
4057: patriarchal lives of my protectors caused these impressions to take a
4058: firm hold on my mind; perhaps, if my first introduction to humanity had
4059: been made by a young soldier, burning for glory and slaughter, I should
4060: have been imbued with different sensations.
4061: 
4062: “But _Paradise Lost_ excited different and far deeper emotions. I read
4063: it, as I had read the other volumes which had fallen into my hands, as
4064: a true history. It moved every feeling of wonder and awe that the
4065: picture of an omnipotent God warring with his creatures was capable of
4066: exciting. I often referred the several situations, as their similarity
4067: struck me, to my own. Like Adam, I was apparently united by no link to
4068: any other being in existence; but his state was far different from mine
4069: in every other respect. He had come forth from the hands of God a
4070: perfect creature, happy and prosperous, guarded by the especial care of
4071: his Creator; he was allowed to converse with and acquire knowledge from
4072: beings of a superior nature, but I was wretched, helpless, and alone.
4073: Many times I considered Satan as the fitter emblem of my condition, for
4074: often, like him, when I viewed the bliss of my protectors, the bitter
4075: gall of envy rose within me.
4076: 
4077: “Another circumstance strengthened and confirmed these feelings. Soon
4078: after my arrival in the hovel I discovered some papers in the pocket of
4079: the dress which I had taken from your laboratory. At first I had
4080: neglected them, but now that I was able to decipher the characters in
4081: which they were written, I began to study them with diligence. It was
4082: your journal of the four months that preceded my creation. You
4083: minutely described in these papers every step you took in the progress
4084: of your work; this history was mingled with accounts of domestic
4085: occurrences. You doubtless recollect these papers. Here they are.
4086: Everything is related in them which bears reference to my accursed
4087: origin; the whole detail of that series of disgusting circumstances
4088: which produced it is set in view; the minutest description of my odious
4089: and loathsome person is given, in language which painted your own
4090: horrors and rendered mine indelible. I sickened as I read. ‘Hateful
4091: day when I received life!’ I exclaimed in agony. ‘Accursed creator!
4092: Why did you form a monster so hideous that even _you_ turned from me in
4093: disgust? God, in pity, made man beautiful and alluring, after his own
4094: image; but my form is a filthy type of yours, more horrid even from the
4095: very resemblance. Satan had his companions, fellow devils, to admire
4096: and encourage him, but I am solitary and abhorred.’
4097: 
4098: “These were the reflections of my hours of despondency and solitude;
4099: but when I contemplated the virtues of the cottagers, their amiable and
4100: benevolent dispositions, I persuaded myself that when they should
4101: become acquainted with my admiration of their virtues they would
4102: compassionate me and overlook my personal deformity. Could they turn
4103: from their door one, however monstrous, who solicited their compassion
4104: and friendship? I resolved, at least, not to despair, but in every way
4105: to fit myself for an interview with them which would decide my fate. I
4106: postponed this attempt for some months longer, for the importance
4107: attached to its success inspired me with a dread lest I should fail.
4108: Besides, I found that my understanding improved so much with every
4109: day’s experience that I was unwilling to commence this undertaking
4110: until a few more months should have added to my sagacity.
4111: 
4112: “Several changes, in the meantime, took place in the cottage. The
4113: presence of Safie diffused happiness among its inhabitants, and I also
4114: found that a greater degree of plenty reigned there. Felix and Agatha
4115: spent more time in amusement and conversation, and were assisted in
4116: their labours by servants. They did not appear rich, but they were
4117: contented and happy; their feelings were serene and peaceful, while
4118: mine became every day more tumultuous. Increase of knowledge only
4119: discovered to me more clearly what a wretched outcast I was. I
4120: cherished hope, it is true, but it vanished when I beheld my person
4121: reflected in water or my shadow in the moonshine, even as that frail
4122: image and that inconstant shade.
4123: 
4124: “I endeavoured to crush these fears and to fortify myself for the trial
4125: which in a few months I resolved to undergo; and sometimes I allowed my
4126: thoughts, unchecked by reason, to ramble in the fields of Paradise, and
4127: dared to fancy amiable and lovely creatures sympathising with my
4128: feelings and cheering my gloom; their angelic countenances breathed
4129: smiles of consolation. But it was all a dream; no Eve soothed my
4130: sorrows nor shared my thoughts; I was alone. I remembered Adam’s
4131: supplication to his Creator. But where was mine? He had abandoned me,
4132: and in the bitterness of my heart I cursed him.
4133: 
4134: “Autumn passed thus. I saw, with surprise and grief, the leaves decay
4135: and fall, and nature again assume the barren and bleak appearance it
4136: had worn when I first beheld the woods and the lovely moon. Yet I did
4137: not heed the bleakness of the weather; I was better fitted by my
4138: conformation for the endurance of cold than heat. But my chief
4139: delights were the sight of the flowers, the birds, and all the gay
4140: apparel of summer; when those deserted me, I turned with more attention
4141: towards the cottagers. Their happiness was not decreased by the
4142: absence of summer. They loved and sympathised with one another; and
4143: their joys, depending on each other, were not interrupted by the
4144: casualties that took place around them. The more I saw of them, the
4145: greater became my desire to claim their protection and kindness; my
4146: heart yearned to be known and loved by these amiable creatures; to see
4147: their sweet looks directed towards me with affection was the utmost
4148: limit of my ambition. I dared not think that they would turn them from
4149: me with disdain and horror. The poor that stopped at their door were
4150: never driven away. I asked, it is true, for greater treasures than a
4151: little food or rest: I required kindness and sympathy; but I did not
4152: believe myself utterly unworthy of it.
4153: 
4154: “The winter advanced, and an entire revolution of the seasons had taken
4155: place since I awoke into life. My attention at this time was solely
4156: directed towards my plan of introducing myself into the cottage of my
4157: protectors. I revolved many projects, but that on which I finally
4158: fixed was to enter the dwelling when the blind old man should be alone.
4159: I had sagacity enough to discover that the unnatural hideousness of my
4160: person was the chief object of horror with those who had formerly
4161: beheld me. My voice, although harsh, had nothing terrible in it; I
4162: thought, therefore, that if in the absence of his children I could gain
4163: the good will and mediation of the old De Lacey, I might by his means
4164: be tolerated by my younger protectors.
4165: 
4166: “One day, when the sun shone on the red leaves that strewed the ground
4167: and diffused cheerfulness, although it denied warmth, Safie, Agatha,
4168: and Felix departed on a long country walk, and the old man, at his own
4169: desire, was left alone in the cottage. When his children had departed,
4170: he took up his guitar and played several mournful but sweet airs, more
4171: sweet and mournful than I had ever heard him play before. At first his
4172: countenance was illuminated with pleasure, but as he continued,
4173: thoughtfulness and sadness succeeded; at length, laying aside the
4174: instrument, he sat absorbed in reflection.
4175: 
4176: “My heart beat quick; this was the hour and moment of trial, which
4177: would decide my hopes or realise my fears. The servants were gone to a
4178: neighbouring fair. All was silent in and around the cottage; it was an
4179: excellent opportunity; yet, when I proceeded to execute my plan, my
4180: limbs failed me and I sank to the ground. Again I rose, and exerting
4181: all the firmness of which I was master, removed the planks which I had
4182: placed before my hovel to conceal my retreat. The fresh air revived
4183: me, and with renewed determination I approached the door of their
4184: cottage.
4185: 
4186: “I knocked. ‘Who is there?’ said the old man. ‘Come in.’
4187: 
4188: “I entered. ‘Pardon this intrusion,’ said I; ‘I am
4189: a traveller in want of a little rest; you would greatly oblige me if you
4190: would allow me to remain a few minutes before the fire.’
4191: 
4192: “‘Enter,’ said De Lacey, ‘and I will try in what
4193: manner I can to relieve your wants; but, unfortunately, my children are
4194: from home, and as I am blind, I am afraid I shall find it difficult to

4664: happy; let me feel gratitude towards you for one benefit! Let me see that I
4665: excite the sympathy of some existing thing; do not deny me my
4666: request!”
4667: 
4668: I was moved. I shuddered when I thought of the possible consequences
4669: of my consent, but I felt that there was some justice in his argument.
4670: His tale and the feelings he now expressed proved him to be a creature
4671: of fine sensations, and did I not as his maker owe him all the portion
4672: of happiness that it was in my power to bestow? He saw my change of
4673: feeling and continued,
4674: 
4675: “If you consent, neither you nor any other human being shall ever see
4676: us again; I will go to the vast wilds of South America. My food is not
4677: that of man; I do not destroy the lamb and the kid to glut my appetite;
4678: acorns and berries afford me sufficient nourishment. My companion will
4679: be of the same nature as myself and will be content with the same fare.
4680: We shall make our bed of dried leaves; the sun will shine on us as on
4681: man and will ripen our food. The picture I present to you is peaceful
4682: and human, and you must feel that you could deny it only in the
4683: wantonness of power and cruelty. Pitiless as you have been towards me,
4684: I now see compassion in your eyes; let me seize the favourable moment
4685: and persuade you to promise what I so ardently desire.”
4686: 
4687: “You propose,” replied I, “to fly from the habitations of
4688: man, to dwell in those wilds where the beasts of the field will be your
4689: only companions. How can you, who long for the love and sympathy of man,
4690: persevere in this exile? You will return and again seek their kindness, and
4691: you will meet with their detestation; your evil passions will be renewed,
4692: and you will then have a companion to aid you in the task of destruction.
4693: This may not be; cease to argue the point, for I cannot consent.”
4694: 
4695: “How inconstant are your feelings! But a moment ago you were moved by
4696: my representations, and why do you again harden yourself to my complaints?
4697: I swear to you, by the earth which I inhabit, and by you that made me, that
4698: with the companion you bestow, I will quit the neighbourhood of man and
4699: dwell, as it may chance, in the most savage of places. My evil passions
4700: will have fled, for I shall meet with sympathy! My life will flow quietly
4701: away, and in my dying moments I shall not curse my maker.”
4702: 
4703: His words had a strange effect upon me. I compassionated him and
4704: sometimes felt a wish to console him, but when I looked upon him, when
4705: I saw the filthy mass that moved and talked, my heart sickened and my
4706: feelings were altered to those of horror and hatred. I tried to stifle
4707: these sensations; I thought that as I could not sympathise with him, I
4708: had no right to withhold from him the small portion of happiness which
4709: was yet in my power to bestow.
4710: 
4711: “You swear,” I said, “to be harmless; but have you not
4712: already shown a degree of malice that should reasonably make me distrust
4713: you? May not even this be a feint that will increase your triumph by
4714: affording a wider scope for your revenge?”
4715: 
4716: “How is this? I must not be trifled with, and I demand an answer. If
4717: I have no ties and no affections, hatred and vice must be my portion;
4718: the love of another will destroy the cause of my crimes, and I shall
4719: become a thing of whose existence everyone will be ignorant. My vices
4720: are the children of a forced solitude that I abhor, and my virtues will
4721: necessarily arise when I live in communion with an equal. I shall feel
4722: the affections of a sensitive being and become linked to the chain of
4723: existence and events from which I am now excluded.”
4724: 
4725: I paused some time to reflect on all he had related and the various
4726: arguments which he had employed. I thought of the promise of virtues which
4727: he had displayed on the opening of his existence and the subsequent blight
4728: of all kindly feeling by the loathing and scorn which his protectors had
4729: manifested towards him. His power and threats were not omitted in my
4730: calculations; a creature who could exist in the ice-caves of the glaciers
4731: and hide himself from pursuit among the ridges of inaccessible precipices
4732: was a being possessing faculties it would be vain to cope with. After a
4733: long pause of reflection I concluded that the justice due both to him and
4734: my fellow creatures demanded of me that I should comply with his request.
4735: Turning to him, therefore, I said,
4736: 
4737: “I consent to your demand, on your solemn oath to quit Europe for ever,
4738: and every other place in the neighbourhood of man, as soon as I shall
4739: deliver into your hands a female who will accompany you in your exile.”
4740: 
4741: “I swear,” he cried, “by the sun, and by the blue sky of
4742: heaven, and by the fire of love that burns my heart, that if you grant my
4743: prayer, while they exist you shall never behold me again. Depart to your
4744: home and commence your labours; I shall watch their progress with
4745: unutterable anxiety; and fear not but that when you are ready I shall
4746: appear.”
4747: 
4748: Saying this, he suddenly quitted me, fearful, perhaps, of any change in
4749: my sentiments. I saw him descend the mountain with greater speed than
4750: the flight of an eagle, and quickly lost among the undulations of the
4751: sea of ice.
4752: 
4753: His tale had occupied the whole day, and the sun was upon the verge of
4754: the horizon when he departed. I knew that I ought to hasten my descent
4755: towards the valley, as I should soon be encompassed in darkness; but my
4756: heart was heavy, and my steps slow. The labour of winding among the
4757: little paths of the mountain and fixing my feet firmly as I advanced
4758: perplexed me, occupied as I was by the emotions which the occurrences
4759: of the day had produced. Night was far advanced when I came to the
4760: halfway resting-place and seated myself beside the fountain. The stars
4761: shone at intervals as the clouds passed from over them; the dark pines
4762: rose before me, and every here and there a broken tree lay on the
4763: ground; it was a scene of wonderful solemnity and stirred strange
4764: thoughts within me. I wept bitterly, and clasping my hands in agony, I
4765: exclaimed, “Oh! stars and clouds and winds, ye are all about to mock
4766: me; if ye really pity me, crush sensation and memory; let me become as
4767: nought; but if not, depart, depart, and leave me in darkness.”
4768: 
4769: These were wild and miserable thoughts, but I cannot describe to you
4770: how the eternal twinkling of the stars weighed upon me and how I
4771: listened to every blast of wind as if it were a dull ugly siroc on its
4772: way to consume me.
4773: 
4774: Morning dawned before I arrived at the village of Chamounix; I took no
4775: rest, but returned immediately to Geneva. Even in my own heart I could
4776: give no expression to my sensations—they weighed on me with a
4777: mountain’s weight and their excess destroyed my agony beneath them.
4778: Thus I returned home, and entering the house, presented myself to the
4779: family. My haggard and wild appearance awoke intense alarm, but I
4780: answered no question, scarcely did I speak. I felt as if I were placed
4781: under a ban—as if I had no right to claim their sympathies—as if
4782: never more might I enjoy companionship with them. Yet even thus I
4783: loved them to adoration; and to save them, I resolved to dedicate
4784: myself to my most abhorred task. The prospect of such an occupation
4785: made every other circumstance of existence pass before me like a dream,
4786: and that thought only had to me the reality of life.
4787: 
4788: 
4789: 
4790: 
4791: Chapter 18
4792: 
4793: 
4794: Day after day, week after week, passed away on my return to Geneva; and
4795: I could not collect the courage to recommence my work. I feared the
4796: vengeance of the disappointed fiend, yet I was unable to overcome my
4797: repugnance to the task which was enjoined me. I found that I could not
4798: compose a female without again devoting several months to profound
4799: study and laborious disquisition. I had heard of some discoveries
4800: having been made by an English philosopher, the knowledge of which was
4801: material to my success, and I sometimes thought of obtaining my
4802: father’s consent to visit England for this purpose; but I clung to
4803: every pretence of delay and shrank from taking the first step in an
4804: undertaking whose immediate necessity began to appear less absolute to
4805: me. A change indeed had taken place in me; my health, which had
4806: hitherto declined, was now much restored; and my spirits, when
4807: unchecked by the memory of my unhappy promise, rose proportionably. My
4808: father saw this change with pleasure, and he turned his thoughts
4809: towards the best method of eradicating the remains of my melancholy,
4810: which every now and then would return by fits, and with a devouring
4811: blackness overcast the approaching sunshine. At these moments I took
4812: refuge in the most perfect solitude. I passed whole days on the lake
4813: alone in a little boat, watching the clouds and listening to the
4814: rippling of the waves, silent and listless. But the fresh air and
4815: bright sun seldom failed to restore me to some degree of composure, and
4816: on my return I met the salutations of my friends with a readier smile
4817: and a more cheerful heart.
4818: 
4819: It was after my return from one of these rambles that my father,
4820: calling me aside, thus addressed me,
4821: 
4822: “I am happy to remark, my dear son, that you have resumed your former
4823: pleasures and seem to be returning to yourself. And yet you are still
4824: unhappy and still avoid our society. For some time I was lost in
4825: conjecture as to the cause of this, but yesterday an idea struck me,
4826: and if it is well founded, I conjure you to avow it. Reserve on such a
4827: point would be not only useless, but draw down treble misery on us all.”
4828: 
4829: I trembled violently at his exordium, and my father continued—
4830: 
4831: “I confess, my son, that I have always looked forward to your
4832: marriage with our dear Elizabeth as the tie of our domestic comfort and the
4833: stay of my declining years. You were attached to each other from your
4834: earliest infancy; you studied together, and appeared, in dispositions and
4835: tastes, entirely suited to one another. But so blind is the experience of
4836: man that what I conceived to be the best assistants to my plan may have
4837: entirely destroyed it. You, perhaps, regard her as your sister, without any
4838: wish that she might become your wife. Nay, you may have met with another
4839: whom you may love; and considering yourself as bound in honour to
4840: Elizabeth, this struggle may occasion the poignant misery which you appear
4841: to feel.”
4842: 
4843: “My dear father, reassure yourself. I love my cousin tenderly and
4844: sincerely. I never saw any woman who excited, as Elizabeth does, my
4845: warmest admiration and affection. My future hopes and prospects are
4846: entirely bound up in the expectation of our union.”
4847: 
4848: “The expression of your sentiments of this subject, my dear Victor,
4849: gives me more pleasure than I have for some time experienced. If you
4850: feel thus, we shall assuredly be happy, however present events may cast
4851: a gloom over us. But it is this gloom which appears to have taken so
4852: strong a hold of your mind that I wish to dissipate. Tell me,
4853: therefore, whether you object to an immediate solemnisation of the
4854: marriage. We have been unfortunate, and recent events have drawn us
4855: from that everyday tranquillity befitting my years and infirmities. You
4856: are younger; yet I do not suppose, possessed as you are of a competent
4857: fortune, that an early marriage would at all interfere with any future
4858: plans of honour and utility that you may have formed. Do not suppose,
4859: however, that I wish to dictate happiness to you or that a delay on
4860: your part would cause me any serious uneasiness. Interpret my words
4861: with candour and answer me, I conjure you, with confidence and
4862: sincerity.”
4863: 
4864: I listened to my father in silence and remained for some time incapable
4865: of offering any reply. I revolved rapidly in my mind a multitude of
4866: thoughts and endeavoured to arrive at some conclusion. Alas! To me
4867: the idea of an immediate union with my Elizabeth was one of horror and
4868: dismay. I was bound by a solemn promise which I had not yet fulfilled
4869: and dared not break, or if I did, what manifold miseries might not
4870: impend over me and my devoted family! Could I enter into a festival
4871: with this deadly weight yet hanging round my neck and bowing me to the
4872: ground? I must perform my engagement and let the monster depart with
4873: his mate before I allowed myself to enjoy the delight of a union from
4874: which I expected peace.
4875: 
4876: I remembered also the necessity imposed upon me of either journeying to
4877: England or entering into a long correspondence with those philosophers
4878: of that country whose knowledge and discoveries were of indispensable
4879: use to me in my present undertaking. The latter method of obtaining
4880: the desired intelligence was dilatory and unsatisfactory; besides, I
4881: had an insurmountable aversion to the idea of engaging myself in my
4882: loathsome task in my father’s house while in habits of familiar
4883: intercourse with those I loved. I knew that a thousand fearful
4884: accidents might occur, the slightest of which would disclose a tale to
4885: thrill all connected with me with horror. I was aware also that I
4886: should often lose all self-command, all capacity of hiding the
4887: harrowing sensations that would possess me during the progress of my
4888: unearthly occupation. I must absent myself from all I loved while thus
4889: employed. Once commenced, it would quickly be achieved, and I might be
4890: restored to my family in peace and happiness. My promise fulfilled,
4891: the monster would depart for ever. Or (so my fond fancy imaged) some
4892: accident might meanwhile occur to destroy him and put an end to my
4893: slavery for ever.
4894: 
4895: These feelings dictated my answer to my father. I expressed a wish to
4896: visit England, but concealing the true reasons of this request, I
4897: clothed my desires under a guise which excited no suspicion, while I
4898: urged my desire with an earnestness that easily induced my father to
4899: comply. After so long a period of an absorbing melancholy that
4900: resembled madness in its intensity and effects, he was glad to find
4901: that I was capable of taking pleasure in the idea of such a journey,
4902: and he hoped that change of scene and varied amusement would, before my
4903: return, have restored me entirely to myself.
4904: 
4905: The duration of my absence was left to my own choice; a few months, or
4906: at most a year, was the period contemplated. One paternal kind
4907: precaution he had taken to ensure my having a companion. Without
4908: previously communicating with me, he had, in concert with Elizabeth,
4909: arranged that Clerval should join me at Strasburgh. This interfered
4910: with the solitude I coveted for the prosecution of my task; yet at the
4911: commencement of my journey the presence of my friend could in no way be
4912: an impediment, and truly I rejoiced that thus I should be saved many
4913: hours of lonely, maddening reflection. Nay, Henry might stand between
4914: me and the intrusion of my foe. If I were alone, would he not at times
4915: force his abhorred presence on me to remind me of my task or to
4916: contemplate its progress?
4917: 
4918: To England, therefore, I was bound, and it was understood that my union
4919: with Elizabeth should take place immediately on my return. My father’s
4920: age rendered him extremely averse to delay. For myself, there was one
4921: reward I promised myself from my detested toils—one consolation for my
4922: unparalleled sufferings; it was the prospect of that day when,
4923: enfranchised from my miserable slavery, I might claim Elizabeth and
4924: forget the past in my union with her.
4925: 
4926: I now made arrangements for my journey, but one feeling haunted me
4927: which filled me with fear and agitation. During my absence I should
4928: leave my friends unconscious of the existence of their enemy and
4929: unprotected from his attacks, exasperated as he might be by my
4930: departure. But he had promised to follow me wherever I might go, and
4931: would he not accompany me to England? This imagination was dreadful in
4932: itself, but soothing inasmuch as it supposed the safety of my friends.
4933: I was agonised with the idea of the possibility that the reverse of
4934: this might happen. But through the whole period during which I was the
4935: slave of my creature I allowed myself to be governed by the impulses of
4936: the moment; and my present sensations strongly intimated that the fiend
4937: would follow me and exempt my family from the danger of his
4938: machinations.
4939: 
4940: It was in the latter end of September that I again quitted my native
4941: country. My journey had been my own suggestion, and Elizabeth
4942: therefore acquiesced, but she was filled with disquiet at the idea of
4943: my suffering, away from her, the inroads of misery and grief. It had
4944: been her care which provided me a companion in Clerval—and yet a man
4945: is blind to a thousand minute circumstances which call forth a woman’s
4946: sedulous attention. She longed to bid me hasten my return; a thousand
4947: conflicting emotions rendered her mute as she bade me a tearful, silent
4948: farewell.
4949: 
4950: I threw myself into the carriage that was to convey me away, hardly
4951: knowing whither I was going, and careless of what was passing around.
4952: I remembered only, and it was with a bitter anguish that I reflected on
4953: it, to order that my chemical instruments should be packed to go with
4954: me. Filled with dreary imaginations, I passed through many beautiful
4955: and majestic scenes, but my eyes were fixed and unobserving. I could
4956: only think of the bourne of my travels and the work which was to occupy
4957: me whilst they endured.
4958: 
4959: After some days spent in listless indolence, during which I traversed
4960: many leagues, I arrived at Strasburgh, where I waited two days for
4961: Clerval. He came. Alas, how great was the contrast between us! He
4962: was alive to every new scene, joyful when he saw the beauties of the
4963: setting sun, and more happy when he beheld it rise and recommence a new
4964: day. He pointed out to me the shifting colours of the landscape and
4965: the appearances of the sky. “This is what it is to live,” he cried;
4966: “now I enjoy existence! But you, my dear Frankenstein, wherefore are
4967: you desponding and sorrowful!” In truth, I was occupied by gloomy
4968: thoughts and neither saw the descent of the evening star nor the golden
4969: sunrise reflected in the Rhine. And you, my friend, would be far more
4970: amused with the journal of Clerval, who observed the scenery with an
4971: eye of feeling and delight, than in listening to my reflections. I, a
4972: miserable wretch, haunted by a curse that shut up every avenue to
4973: enjoyment.
4974: 
4975: We had agreed to descend the Rhine in a boat from Strasburgh to
4976: Rotterdam, whence we might take shipping for London. During this
4977: voyage we passed many willowy islands and saw several beautiful towns.
4978: We stayed a day at Mannheim, and on the fifth from our departure from
4979: Strasburgh, arrived at Mainz. The course of the Rhine below Mainz
4980: becomes much more picturesque. The river descends rapidly and winds
4981: between hills, not high, but steep, and of beautiful forms. We saw
4982: many ruined castles standing on the edges of precipices, surrounded by
4983: black woods, high and inaccessible. This part of the Rhine, indeed,
4984: presents a singularly variegated landscape. In one spot you view
4985: rugged hills, ruined castles overlooking tremendous precipices, with
4986: the dark Rhine rushing beneath; and on the sudden turn of a promontory,
4987: flourishing vineyards with green sloping banks and a meandering river
4988: and populous towns occupy the scene.
4989: 
4990: We travelled at the time of the vintage and heard the song of the labourers
4991: as we glided down the stream. Even I, depressed in mind, and my spirits
4992: continually agitated by gloomy feelings, even I was pleased. I lay at the
4993: bottom of the boat, and as I gazed on the cloudless blue sky, I seemed to
4994: drink in a tranquillity to which I had long been a stranger. And if these
4995: were my sensations, who can describe those of Henry? He felt as if he had
4996: been transported to Fairy-land and enjoyed a happiness seldom tasted by
4997: man. “I have seen,” he said, “the most beautiful scenes
4998: of my own country; I have visited the lakes of Lucerne and Uri, where the
4999: snowy mountains descend almost perpendicularly to the water, casting black
5000: and impenetrable shades, which would cause a gloomy and mournful appearance
5001: were it not for the most verdant islands that relieve the eye by their gay
5002: appearance; I have seen this lake agitated by a tempest, when the wind tore
5003: up whirlwinds of water and gave you an idea of what the water-spout must be
5004: on the great ocean; and the waves dash with fury the base of the mountain,
5005: where the priest and his mistress were overwhelmed by an avalanche and
5006: where their dying voices are still said to be heard amid the pauses of the
5007: nightly wind; I have seen the mountains of La Valais, and the Pays de Vaud;
5008: but this country, Victor, pleases me more than all those wonders. The
5009: mountains of Switzerland are more majestic and strange, but there is a
5010: charm in the banks of this divine river that I never before saw equalled.
5011: Look at that castle which overhangs yon precipice; and that also on the
5012: island, almost concealed amongst the foliage of those lovely trees; and now
5013: that group of labourers coming from among their vines; and that village
5014: half hid in the recess of the mountain. Oh, surely the spirit that inhabits
5015: and guards this place has a soul more in harmony with man than those who
5016: pile the glacier or retire to the inaccessible peaks of the mountains of
5017: our own country.”
5018: 
5019: Clerval! Beloved friend! Even now it delights me to record your words and
5020: to dwell on the praise of which you are so eminently deserving. He was a
5021: being formed in the “very poetry of nature.” His wild and
5022: enthusiastic imagination was chastened by the sensibility of his heart. His
5023: soul overflowed with ardent affections, and his friendship was of that
5024: devoted and wondrous nature that the worldly-minded teach us to look for only
5025: in the imagination. But even human sympathies were not sufficient to
5026: satisfy his eager mind. The scenery of external nature, which others regard
5027: only with admiration, he loved with ardour:—
5028: 
5029:     ——The sounding cataract
5030:     Haunted him like a passion: the tall rock,
5031:     The mountain, and the deep and gloomy wood,
5032:     Their colours and their forms, were then to him
5033:     An appetite; a feeling, and a love,
5034:     That had no need of a remoter charm,
5035:     By thought supplied, or any interest
5036:     Unborrow’d from the eye.
5037: 
5038:           [Wordsworth’s “Tintern Abbey”.]
5039: 
5040: And where does he now exist? Is this gentle and lovely being lost
5041: for ever? Has this mind, so replete with ideas, imaginations fanciful
5042: and magnificent, which formed a world, whose existence depended on the
5043: life of its creator;—has this mind perished? Does it now only exist
5044: in my memory? No, it is not thus; your form so divinely wrought, and
5045: beaming with beauty, has decayed, but your spirit still visits and
5046: consoles your unhappy friend.
5047: 
5048: Pardon this gush of sorrow; these ineffectual words are but a slight
5049: tribute to the unexampled worth of Henry, but they soothe my heart,
5050: overflowing with the anguish which his remembrance creates. I will
5051: proceed with my tale.
5052: 
5053: Beyond Cologne we descended to the plains of Holland; and we resolved to
5054: post the remainder of our way, for the wind was contrary and the stream of
5055: the river was too gentle to aid us.
5056: 
5057: Our journey here lost the interest arising from beautiful scenery, but we
5058: arrived in a few days at Rotterdam, whence we proceeded by sea to England.
5059: It was on a clear morning, in the latter days of December, that I first saw
5060: the white cliffs of Britain. The banks of the Thames presented a new scene;
5061: they were flat but fertile, and almost every town was marked by the
5062: remembrance of some story. We saw Tilbury Fort and remembered the Spanish
5063: Armada, Gravesend, Woolwich, and Greenwich—places which I had heard
5064: of even in my country.
5065: 
5066: At length we saw the numerous steeples of London, St. Paul’s towering
5067: above all, and the Tower famed in English history.
5068: 
5069: 
5070: 
5071: 
5072: Chapter 19
5073: 
5074: 
5075: London was our present point of rest; we determined to remain several
5076: months in this wonderful and celebrated city. Clerval desired the
5077: intercourse of the men of genius and talent who flourished at this
5078: time, but this was with me a secondary object; I was principally
5079: occupied with the means of obtaining the information necessary for the
5080: completion of my promise and quickly availed myself of the letters of
5081: introduction that I had brought with me, addressed to the most
5082: distinguished natural philosophers.
5083: 
5084: If this journey had taken place during my days of study and happiness,
5085: it would have afforded me inexpressible pleasure. But a blight had
5086: come over my existence, and I only visited these people for the sake of
5087: the information they might give me on the subject in which my interest
5088: was so terribly profound. Company was irksome to me; when alone, I
5089: could fill my mind with the sights of heaven and earth; the voice of
5090: Henry soothed me, and I could thus cheat myself into a transitory
5091: peace. But busy, uninteresting, joyous faces brought back despair to
5092: my heart. I saw an insurmountable barrier placed between me and my
5093: fellow men; this barrier was sealed with the blood of William and
5094: Justine, and to reflect on the events connected with those names filled
5095: my soul with anguish.
5096: 
5097: But in Clerval I saw the image of my former self; he was inquisitive
5098: and anxious to gain experience and instruction. The difference of
5099: manners which he observed was to him an inexhaustible source of
5100: instruction and amusement. He was also pursuing an object he had long
5101: had in view. His design was to visit India, in the belief that he had
5102: in his knowledge of its various languages, and in the views he had
5103: taken of its society, the means of materially assisting the progress of
5104: European colonization and trade. In Britain only could he further the
5105: execution of his plan. He was for ever busy, and the only check to his
5106: enjoyments was my sorrowful and dejected mind. I tried to conceal this
5107: as much as possible, that I might not debar him from the pleasures
5108: natural to one who was entering on a new scene of life, undisturbed by
5109: any care or bitter recollection. I often refused to accompany him,
5110: alleging another engagement, that I might remain alone. I now also
5111: began to collect the materials necessary for my new creation, and this
5112: was to me like the torture of single drops of water continually falling
5113: on the head. Every thought that was devoted to it was an extreme
5114: anguish, and every word that I spoke in allusion to it caused my lips
5115: to quiver, and my heart to palpitate.
5116: 
5117: After passing some months in London, we received a letter from a person in
5118: Scotland who had formerly been our visitor at Geneva. He mentioned the
5119: beauties of his native country and asked us if those were not sufficient
5120: allurements to induce us to prolong our journey as far north as Perth,
5121: where he resided. Clerval eagerly desired to accept this invitation, and I,
5122: although I abhorred society, wished to view again mountains and streams and
5123: all the wondrous works with which Nature adorns her chosen dwelling-places.
5124: 
5125: We had arrived in England at the beginning of October, and it was now
5126: February. We accordingly determined to commence our journey towards the
5127: north at the expiration of another month. In this expedition we did not
5128: intend to follow the great road to Edinburgh, but to visit Windsor, Oxford,
5129: Matlock, and the Cumberland lakes, resolving to arrive at the completion of
5130: this tour about the end of July. I packed up my chemical instruments and
5131: the materials I had collected, resolving to finish my labours in some
5132: obscure nook in the northern highlands of Scotland.
5133: 
5134: We quitted London on the 27th of March and remained a few days at
5135: Windsor, rambling in its beautiful forest. This was a new scene to us
5136: mountaineers; the majestic oaks, the quantity of game, and the herds of
5137: stately deer were all novelties to us.
5138: 
5139: From thence we proceeded to Oxford. As we entered this city, our minds
5140: were filled with the remembrance of the events that had been transacted
5141: there more than a century and a half before. It was here that Charles
5142: I. had collected his forces. This city had remained faithful to him,
5143: after the whole nation had forsaken his cause to join the standard of
5144: Parliament and liberty. The memory of that unfortunate king and his
5145: companions, the amiable Falkland, the insolent Goring, his queen, and
5146: son, gave a peculiar interest to every part of the city which they
5147: might be supposed to have inhabited. The spirit of elder days found a
5148: dwelling here, and we delighted to trace its footsteps. If these
5149: feelings had not found an imaginary gratification, the appearance of
5150: the city had yet in itself sufficient beauty to obtain our admiration.
5151: The colleges are ancient and picturesque; the streets are almost
5152: magnificent; and the lovely Isis, which flows beside it through meadows
5153: of exquisite verdure, is spread forth into a placid expanse of waters,
5154: which reflects its majestic assemblage of towers, and spires, and
5155: domes, embosomed among aged trees.
5156: 
5157: I enjoyed this scene, and yet my enjoyment was embittered both by the
5158: memory of the past and the anticipation of the future. I was formed
5159: for peaceful happiness. During my youthful days discontent never
5160: visited my mind, and if I was ever overcome by _ennui_, the sight of what
5161: is beautiful in nature or the study of what is excellent and sublime in
5162: the productions of man could always interest my heart and communicate
5163: elasticity to my spirits. But I am a blasted tree; the bolt has
5164: entered my soul; and I felt then that I should survive to exhibit what
5165: I shall soon cease to be—a miserable spectacle of wrecked humanity,
5166: pitiable to others and intolerable to myself.
5167: 
5168: We passed a considerable period at Oxford, rambling among its environs
5169: and endeavouring to identify every spot which might relate to the most
5170: animating epoch of English history. Our little voyages of discovery
5171: were often prolonged by the successive objects that presented
5172: themselves. We visited the tomb of the illustrious Hampden and the
5173: field on which that patriot fell. For a moment my soul was elevated
5174: from its debasing and miserable fears to contemplate the divine ideas
5175: of liberty and self-sacrifice of which these sights were the monuments
5176: and the remembrancers. For an instant I dared to shake off my chains
5177: and look around me with a free and lofty spirit, but the iron had eaten
5178: into my flesh, and I sank again, trembling and hopeless, into my
5179: miserable self.
5180: 
5181: We left Oxford with regret and proceeded to Matlock, which was our next
5182: place of rest. The country in the neighbourhood of this village
5183: resembled, to a greater degree, the scenery of Switzerland; but
5184: everything is on a lower scale, and the green hills want the crown of
5185: distant white Alps which always attend on the piny mountains of my
5186: native country. We visited the wondrous cave and the little cabinets
5187: of natural history, where the curiosities are disposed in the same
5188: manner as in the collections at Servox and Chamounix. The latter name
5189: made me tremble when pronounced by Henry, and I hastened to quit
5190: Matlock, with which that terrible scene was thus associated.
5191: 
5192: From Derby, still journeying northwards, we passed two months in
5193: Cumberland and Westmorland. I could now almost fancy myself among the
5194: Swiss mountains. The little patches of snow which yet lingered on the
5195: northern sides of the mountains, the lakes, and the dashing of the
5196: rocky streams were all familiar and dear sights to me. Here also we
5197: made some acquaintances, who almost contrived to cheat me into
5198: happiness. The delight of Clerval was proportionably greater than
5199: mine; his mind expanded in the company of men of talent, and he found
5200: in his own nature greater capacities and resources than he could have
5201: imagined himself to have possessed while he associated with his
5202: inferiors. “I could pass my life here,” said he to me; “and among
5203: these mountains I should scarcely regret Switzerland and the Rhine.”
5204: 
5205: But he found that a traveller’s life is one that includes much pain
5206: amidst its enjoyments. His feelings are for ever on the stretch; and
5207: when he begins to sink into repose, he finds himself obliged to quit
5208: that on which he rests in pleasure for something new, which again
5209: engages his attention, and which also he forsakes for other novelties.
5210: 
5211: We had scarcely visited the various lakes of Cumberland and Westmorland
5212: and conceived an affection for some of the inhabitants when the period
5213: of our appointment with our Scotch friend approached, and we left them
5214: to travel on. For my own part I was not sorry. I had now neglected my
5215: promise for some time, and I feared the effects of the dæmon’s
5216: disappointment. He might remain in Switzerland and wreak his vengeance
5217: on my relatives. This idea pursued me and tormented me at every moment
5218: from which I might otherwise have snatched repose and peace. I waited
5219: for my letters with feverish impatience; if they were delayed I was
5220: miserable and overcome by a thousand fears; and when they arrived and I
5221: saw the superscription of Elizabeth or my father, I hardly dared to
5222: read and ascertain my fate. Sometimes I thought that the fiend
5223: followed me and might expedite my remissness by murdering my companion.
5224: When these thoughts possessed me, I would not quit Henry for a moment,
5225: but followed him as his shadow, to protect him from the fancied rage of
5226: his destroyer. I felt as if I had committed some great crime, the
5227: consciousness of which haunted me. I was guiltless, but I had indeed
5228: drawn down a horrible curse upon my head, as mortal as that of crime.
5229: 
5230: I visited Edinburgh with languid eyes and mind; and yet that city might
5231: have interested the most unfortunate being. Clerval did not like it so well
5232: as Oxford, for the antiquity of the latter city was more pleasing to him.
5233: But the beauty and regularity of the new town of Edinburgh, its romantic
5234: castle and its environs, the most delightful in the world, Arthur’s
5235: Seat, St. Bernard’s Well, and the Pentland Hills, compensated him for
5236: the change and filled him with cheerfulness and admiration. But I was
5237: impatient to arrive at the termination of my journey.
5238: 
5239: We left Edinburgh in a week, passing through Coupar, St. Andrew’s, and
5240: along the banks of the Tay, to Perth, where our friend expected us.
5241: But I was in no mood to laugh and talk with strangers or enter into
5242: their feelings or plans with the good humour expected from a guest; and
5243: accordingly I told Clerval that I wished to make the tour of Scotland
5244: alone. “Do you,” said I, “enjoy yourself, and let this be our
5245: rendezvous. I may be absent a month or two; but do not interfere with
5246: my motions, I entreat you; leave me to peace and solitude for a short
5247: time; and when I return, I hope it will be with a lighter heart, more
5248: congenial to your own temper.”
5249: 
5250: Henry wished to dissuade me, but seeing me bent on this plan, ceased to
5251: remonstrate. He entreated me to write often. “I had rather be with
5252: you,” he said, “in your solitary rambles, than with these Scotch
5253: people, whom I do not know; hasten, then, my dear friend, to return,
5254: that I may again feel myself somewhat at home, which I cannot do in
5255: your absence.”
5256: 
5257: Having parted from my friend, I determined to visit some remote spot of
5258: Scotland and finish my work in solitude. I did not doubt but that the
5259: monster followed me and would discover himself to me when I should have
5260: finished, that he might receive his companion.
5261: 
5262: With this resolution I traversed the northern highlands and fixed on one of
5263: the remotest of the Orkneys as the scene of my labours. It was a place
5264: fitted for such a work, being hardly more than a rock whose high sides were
5265: continually beaten upon by the waves. The soil was barren, scarcely
5266: affording pasture for a few miserable cows, and oatmeal for its
5267: inhabitants, which consisted of five persons, whose gaunt and scraggy limbs
5268: gave tokens of their miserable fare. Vegetables and bread, when they
5269: indulged in such luxuries, and even fresh water, was to be procured from
5270: the mainland, which was about five miles distant.
5271: 
5272: On the whole island there were but three miserable huts, and one of
5273: these was vacant when I arrived. This I hired. It contained but two
5274: rooms, and these exhibited all the squalidness of the most miserable
5275: penury. The thatch had fallen in, the walls were unplastered, and the
5276: door was off its hinges. I ordered it to be repaired, bought some
5277: furniture, and took possession, an incident which would doubtless have
5278: occasioned some surprise had not all the senses of the cottagers been
5279: benumbed by want and squalid poverty. As it was, I lived ungazed at
5280: and unmolested, hardly thanked for the pittance of food and clothes
5281: which I gave, so much does suffering blunt even the coarsest sensations
5282: of men.
5283: 
5284: In this retreat I devoted the morning to labour; but in the evening,
5285: when the weather permitted, I walked on the stony beach of the sea to
5286: listen to the waves as they roared and dashed at my feet. It was a
5287: monotonous yet ever-changing scene. I thought of Switzerland; it was
5288: far different from this desolate and appalling landscape. Its hills
5289: are covered with vines, and its cottages are scattered thickly in the
5290: plains. Its fair lakes reflect a blue and gentle sky, and when
5291: troubled by the winds, their tumult is but as the play of a lively
5292: infant when compared to the roarings of the giant ocean.
5293: 
5294: In this manner I distributed my occupations when I first arrived, but
5295: as I proceeded in my labour, it became every day more horrible and
5296: irksome to me. Sometimes I could not prevail on myself to enter my
5297: laboratory for several days, and at other times I toiled day and night
5298: in order to complete my work. It was, indeed, a filthy process in
5299: which I was engaged. During my first experiment, a kind of
5300: enthusiastic frenzy had blinded me to the horror of my employment; my
5301: mind was intently fixed on the consummation of my labour, and my eyes
5302: were shut to the horror of my proceedings. But now I went to it in
5303: cold blood, and my heart often sickened at the work of my hands.
5304: 
5305: Thus situated, employed in the most detestable occupation, immersed in
5306: a solitude where nothing could for an instant call my attention from
5307: the actual scene in which I was engaged, my spirits became unequal; I
5308: grew restless and nervous. Every moment I feared to meet my
5309: persecutor. Sometimes I sat with my eyes fixed on the ground, fearing
5310: to raise them lest they should encounter the object which I so much
5311: dreaded to behold. I feared to wander from the sight of my fellow
5312: creatures lest when alone he should come to claim his companion.
5313: 
5314: In the mean time I worked on, and my labour was already considerably
5315: advanced. I looked towards its completion with a tremulous and eager
5316: hope, which I dared not trust myself to question but which was
5317: intermixed with obscure forebodings of evil that made my heart sicken
5318: in my bosom.
5319: 
5320: 
5321: 
5322: 
5323: Chapter 20
5324: 
5325: 
5326: I sat one evening in my laboratory; the sun had set, and the moon was just
5327: rising from the sea; I had not sufficient light for my employment, and I
5328: remained idle, in a pause of consideration of whether I should leave my
5329: labour for the night or hasten its conclusion by an unremitting attention
5330: to it. As I sat, a train of reflection occurred to me which led me to
5331: consider the effects of what I was now doing. Three years before, I was
5332: engaged in the same manner and had created a fiend whose unparalleled
5333: barbarity had desolated my heart and filled it for ever with the bitterest
5334: remorse. I was now about to form another being of whose dispositions I was
5335: alike ignorant; she might become ten thousand times more malignant than her
5336: mate and delight, for its own sake, in murder and wretchedness. He had
5337: sworn to quit the neighbourhood of man and hide himself in deserts, but she
5338: had not; and she, who in all probability was to become a thinking and
5339: reasoning animal, might refuse to comply with a compact made before her
5340: creation. They might even hate each other; the creature who already lived
5341: loathed his own deformity, and might he not conceive a greater abhorrence
5342: for it when it came before his eyes in the female form? She also might turn
5343: with disgust from him to the superior beauty of man; she might quit him,
5344: and he be again alone, exasperated by the fresh provocation of being
5345: deserted by one of his own species.
5346: 
5347: Even if they were to leave Europe and inhabit the deserts of the new world,
5348: yet one of the first results of those sympathies for which the dæmon
5349: thirsted would be children, and a race of devils would be propagated upon
5350: the earth who might make the very existence of the species of man a
5351: condition precarious and full of terror. Had I right, for my own benefit,
5352: to inflict this curse upon everlasting generations? I had before been moved
5353: by the sophisms of the being I had created; I had been struck senseless by
5354: his fiendish threats; but now, for the first time, the wickedness of my
5355: promise burst upon me; I shuddered to think that future ages might curse me
5356: as their pest, whose selfishness had not hesitated to buy its own peace at
5357: the price, perhaps, of the existence of the whole human race.
5358: 
5359: I trembled and my heart failed within me, when, on looking up, I saw by
5360: the light of the moon the dæmon at the casement. A ghastly grin
5361: wrinkled his lips as he gazed on me, where I sat fulfilling the task
5362: which he had allotted to me. Yes, he had followed me in my travels; he
5363: had loitered in forests, hid himself in caves, or taken refuge in wide
5364: and desert heaths; and he now came to mark my progress and claim the
5365: fulfilment of my promise.
5366: 
5367: As I looked on him, his countenance expressed the utmost extent of
5368: malice and treachery. I thought with a sensation of madness on my
5369: promise of creating another like to him, and trembling with passion,
5370: tore to pieces the thing on which I was engaged. The wretch saw me
5371: destroy the creature on whose future existence he depended for
5372: happiness, and with a howl of devilish despair and revenge, withdrew.
5373: 
5374: I left the room, and locking the door, made a solemn vow in my own
5375: heart never to resume my labours; and then, with trembling steps, I
5376: sought my own apartment. I was alone; none were near me to dissipate
5377: the gloom and relieve me from the sickening oppression of the most
5378: terrible reveries.
5379: 
5380: Several hours passed, and I remained near my window gazing on the sea;
5381: it was almost motionless, for the winds were hushed, and all nature
5382: reposed under the eye of the quiet moon. A few fishing vessels alone
5383: specked the water, and now and then the gentle breeze wafted the sound
5384: of voices as the fishermen called to one another. I felt the silence,
5385: although I was hardly conscious of its extreme profundity, until my ear
5386: was suddenly arrested by the paddling of oars near the shore, and a
5387: person landed close to my house.
5388: 
5389: In a few minutes after, I heard the creaking of my door, as if some one
5390: endeavoured to open it softly. I trembled from head to foot; I felt a
5391: presentiment of who it was and wished to rouse one of the peasants who
5392: dwelt in a cottage not far from mine; but I was overcome by the sensation
5393: of helplessness, so often felt in frightful dreams, when you in vain
5394: endeavour to fly from an impending danger, and was rooted to the spot.
5395: 
5396: Presently I heard the sound of footsteps along the passage; the door
5397: opened, and the wretch whom I dreaded appeared. Shutting the door, he
5398: approached me and said in a smothered voice,
5399: 
5400: “You have destroyed the work which you began; what is it that you
5401: intend? Do you dare to break your promise? I have endured toil and misery;
5402: I left Switzerland with you; I crept along the shores of the Rhine, among
5403: its willow islands and over the summits of its hills. I have dwelt many
5404: months in the heaths of England and among the deserts of Scotland. I have
5405: endured incalculable fatigue, and cold, and hunger; do you dare destroy my
5406: hopes?”
5407: 
5408: “Begone! I do break my promise; never will I create another like
5409: yourself, equal in deformity and wickedness.”
5410: 
5411: “Slave, I before reasoned with you, but you have proved yourself
5412: unworthy of my condescension. Remember that I have power; you believe
5413: yourself miserable, but I can make you so wretched that the light of
5414: day will be hateful to you. You are my creator, but I am your master;
5415: obey!”
5416: 
5417: “The hour of my irresolution is past, and the period of your power is
5418: arrived. Your threats cannot move me to do an act of wickedness; but
5419: they confirm me in a determination of not creating you a companion in
5420: vice. Shall I, in cool blood, set loose upon the earth a dæmon whose
5421: delight is in death and wretchedness? Begone! I am firm, and your
5422: words will only exasperate my rage.”
5423: 
5424: The monster saw my determination in my face and gnashed his teeth in the
5425: impotence of anger. “Shall each man,” cried he, “find a
5426: wife for his bosom, and each beast have his mate, and I be alone? I had
5427: feelings of affection, and they were requited by detestation and scorn.
5428: Man! You may hate, but beware! Your hours will pass in dread and misery,
5429: and soon the bolt will fall which must ravish from you your happiness for
5430: ever. Are you to be happy while I grovel in the intensity of my
5431: wretchedness? You can blast my other passions, but revenge
5432: remains—revenge, henceforth dearer than light or food! I may die, but
5433: first you, my tyrant and tormentor, shall curse the sun that gazes on your
5434: misery. Beware, for I am fearless and therefore powerful. I will watch with
5435: the wiliness of a snake, that I may sting with its venom. Man, you shall
5436: repent of the injuries you inflict.”
5437: 
5438: “Devil, cease; and do not poison the air with these sounds of malice.
5439: I have declared my resolution to you, and I am no coward to bend
5440: beneath words. Leave me; I am inexorable.”
5441: 
5442: “It is well. I go; but remember, I shall be with you on your
5443: wedding-night.”
5444: 
5445: I started forward and exclaimed, “Villain! Before you sign my
5446: death-warrant, be sure that you are yourself safe.”
5447: 
5448: I would have seized him, but he eluded me and quitted the house with
5449: precipitation. In a few moments I saw him in his boat, which shot
5450: across the waters with an arrowy swiftness and was soon lost amidst the
5451: waves.
5452: 
5453: All was again silent, but his words rang in my ears. I burned with rage to
5454: pursue the murderer of my peace and precipitate him into the ocean. I
5455: walked up and down my room hastily and perturbed, while my imagination
5456: conjured up a thousand images to torment and sting me. Why had I not
5457: followed him and closed with him in mortal strife? But I had suffered him
5458: to depart, and he had directed his course towards the mainland. I shuddered
5459: to think who might be the next victim sacrificed to his insatiate revenge.
5460: And then I thought again of his words—“_I will be with you on
5461: your wedding-night._” That, then, was the period fixed for the
5462: fulfilment of my destiny. In that hour I should die and at once satisfy and
5463: extinguish his malice. The prospect did not move me to fear; yet when I
5464: thought of my beloved Elizabeth, of her tears and endless sorrow, when she
5465: should find her lover so barbarously snatched from her, tears, the first I
5466: had shed for many months, streamed from my eyes, and I resolved not to fall
5467: before my enemy without a bitter struggle.
5468: 
5469: The night passed away, and the sun rose from the ocean; my feelings became
5470: calmer, if it may be called calmness when the violence of rage sinks into
5471: the depths of despair. I left the house, the horrid scene of the last
5472: night’s contention, and walked on the beach of the sea, which I
5473: almost regarded as an insuperable barrier between me and my fellow
5474: creatures; nay, a wish that such should prove the fact stole across me. I
5475: desired that I might pass my life on that barren rock, wearily, it is true,
5476: but uninterrupted by any sudden shock of misery. If I returned, it was to
5477: be sacrificed or to see those whom I most loved die under the grasp of a
5478: dæmon whom I had myself created.
5479: 
5480: I walked about the isle like a restless spectre, separated from all it
5481: loved and miserable in the separation. When it became noon, and the
5482: sun rose higher, I lay down on the grass and was overpowered by a deep
5483: sleep. I had been awake the whole of the preceding night, my nerves
5484: were agitated, and my eyes inflamed by watching and misery. The sleep
5485: into which I now sank refreshed me; and when I awoke, I again felt as
5486: if I belonged to a race of human beings like myself, and I began to
5487: reflect upon what had passed with greater composure; yet still the
5488: words of the fiend rang in my ears like a death-knell; they appeared
5489: like a dream, yet distinct and oppressive as a reality.
5490: 
5491: The sun had far descended, and I still sat on the shore, satisfying my
