5492: appetite, which had become ravenous, with an oaten cake, when I saw a
5493: fishing-boat land close to me, and one of the men brought me a packet;
5494: it contained letters from Geneva, and one from Clerval entreating me to
5495: join him. He said that he was wearing away his time fruitlessly where
5496: he was, that letters from the friends he had formed in London desired
5497: his return to complete the negotiation they had entered into for his
5498: Indian enterprise. He could not any longer delay his departure; but as
5499: his journey to London might be followed, even sooner than he now
5500: conjectured, by his longer voyage, he entreated me to bestow as much of
5501: my society on him as I could spare. He besought me, therefore, to
5502: leave my solitary isle and to meet him at Perth, that we might proceed
5503: southwards together. This letter in a degree recalled me to life, and
5504: I determined to quit my island at the expiration of two days.
5505: 
5506: Yet, before I departed, there was a task to perform, on which I shuddered
5507: to reflect; I must pack up my chemical instruments, and for that purpose I
5508: must enter the room which had been the scene of my odious work, and I must
5509: handle those utensils the sight of which was sickening to me. The next
5510: morning, at daybreak, I summoned sufficient courage and unlocked the door
5511: of my laboratory. The remains of the half-finished creature, whom I had
5512: destroyed, lay scattered on the floor, and I almost felt as if I had
5513: mangled the living flesh of a human being. I paused to collect myself and
5514: then entered the chamber. With trembling hand I conveyed the instruments
5515: out of the room, but I reflected that I ought not to leave the relics of my
5516: work to excite the horror and suspicion of the peasants; and I accordingly
5517: put them into a basket, with a great quantity of stones, and laying them
5518: up, determined to throw them into the sea that very night; and in the
5519: meantime I sat upon the beach, employed in cleaning and arranging my
5520: chemical apparatus.
5521: 
5522: Nothing could be more complete than the alteration that had taken place
5523: in my feelings since the night of the appearance of the dæmon. I had
5524: before regarded my promise with a gloomy despair as a thing that, with
5525: whatever consequences, must be fulfilled; but I now felt as if a film
5526: had been taken from before my eyes and that I for the first time saw
5527: clearly. The idea of renewing my labours did not for one instant occur
5528: to me; the threat I had heard weighed on my thoughts, but I did not
5529: reflect that a voluntary act of mine could avert it. I had resolved in
5530: my own mind that to create another like the fiend I had first made
5531: would be an act of the basest and most atrocious selfishness, and I
5532: banished from my mind every thought that could lead to a different
5533: conclusion.
5534: 
5535: Between two and three in the morning the moon rose; and I then, putting my
5536: basket aboard a little skiff, sailed out about four miles from the shore.
5537: The scene was perfectly solitary; a few boats were returning towards land,
5538: but I sailed away from them. I felt as if I was about the commission of a
5539: dreadful crime and avoided with shuddering anxiety any encounter with my
5540: fellow creatures. At one time the moon, which had before been clear, was
5541: suddenly overspread by a thick cloud, and I took advantage of the moment of
5542: darkness and cast my basket into the sea; I listened to the gurgling sound
5543: as it sank and then sailed away from the spot. The sky became clouded, but
5544: the air was pure, although chilled by the northeast breeze that was then
5545: rising. But it refreshed me and filled me with such agreeable sensations
5546: that I resolved to prolong my stay on the water, and fixing the rudder in a
5547: direct position, stretched myself at the bottom of the boat. Clouds hid the
5548: moon, everything was obscure, and I heard only the sound of the boat as its
5549: keel cut through the waves; the murmur lulled me, and in a short time I
5550: slept soundly.
5551: 
5552: I do not know how long I remained in this situation, but when I awoke I
5553: found that the sun had already mounted considerably. The wind was high, and
5554: the waves continually threatened the safety of my little skiff. I found
5555: that the wind was northeast and must have driven me far from the coast from
5556: which I had embarked. I endeavoured to change my course but quickly found
5557: that if I again made the attempt the boat would be instantly filled with
5558: water. Thus situated, my only resource was to drive before the wind. I
5559: confess that I felt a few sensations of terror. I had no compass with me
5560: and was so slenderly acquainted with the geography of this part of the
5561: world that the sun was of little benefit to me. I might be driven into the
5562: wide Atlantic and feel all the tortures of starvation or be swallowed up in
5563: the immeasurable waters that roared and buffeted around me. I had already
5564: been out many hours and felt the torment of a burning thirst, a prelude to
5565: my other sufferings. I looked on the heavens, which were covered by clouds
5566: that flew before the wind, only to be replaced by others; I looked upon the
5567: sea; it was to be my grave. “Fiend,” I exclaimed, “your
5568: task is already fulfilled!” I thought of Elizabeth, of my father, and
5569: of Clerval—all left behind, on whom the monster might satisfy his
5570: sanguinary and merciless passions. This idea plunged me into a reverie so
5571: despairing and frightful that even now, when the scene is on the point of
5572: closing before me for ever, I shudder to reflect on it.
5573: 
5574: Some hours passed thus; but by degrees, as the sun declined towards the
5575: horizon, the wind died away into a gentle breeze and the sea became
5576: free from breakers. But these gave place to a heavy swell; I felt sick
5577: and hardly able to hold the rudder, when suddenly I saw a line of high
5578: land towards the south.
5579: 
5580: Almost spent, as I was, by fatigue and the dreadful suspense I endured
5581: for several hours, this sudden certainty of life rushed like a flood of
5582: warm joy to my heart, and tears gushed from my eyes.
5583: 
5584: How mutable are our feelings, and how strange is that clinging love we have
5585: of life even in the excess of misery! I constructed another sail with a
5586: part of my dress and eagerly steered my course towards the land. It had a
5587: wild and rocky appearance, but as I approached nearer I easily perceived
5588: the traces of cultivation. I saw vessels near the shore and found myself
5589: suddenly transported back to the neighbourhood of civilised man. I
5590: carefully traced the windings of the land and hailed a steeple which I at
5591: length saw issuing from behind a small promontory. As I was in a state of
5592: extreme debility, I resolved to sail directly towards the town, as a place
5593: where I could most easily procure nourishment. Fortunately I had money with
5594: me. As I turned the promontory I perceived a small neat town and a good
5595: harbour, which I entered, my heart bounding with joy at my unexpected
5596: escape.
5597: 
5598: As I was occupied in fixing the boat and arranging the sails, several
5599: people crowded towards the spot. They seemed much surprised at my
5600: appearance, but instead of offering me any assistance, whispered
5601: together with gestures that at any other time might have produced in me
5602: a slight sensation of alarm. As it was, I merely remarked that they
5603: spoke English, and I therefore addressed them in that language. “My
5604: good friends,” said I, “will you be so kind as to tell me the name of
5605: this town and inform me where I am?”
5606: 
5607: “You will know that soon enough,” replied a man with a hoarse voice.
5608: “Maybe you are come to a place that will not prove much to your taste,
5609: but you will not be consulted as to your quarters, I promise you.”
5610: 
5611: I was exceedingly surprised on receiving so rude an answer from a
5612: stranger, and I was also disconcerted on perceiving the frowning and
5613: angry countenances of his companions. “Why do you answer me so
5614: roughly?” I replied. “Surely it is not the custom of Englishmen to
5615: receive strangers so inhospitably.”
5616: 
5617: “I do not know,” said the man, “what the custom of the
5618: English may be, but it is the custom of the Irish to hate villains.”
5619: 
5620: While this strange dialogue continued, I perceived the crowd rapidly
5621: increase. Their faces expressed a mixture of curiosity and anger, which
5622: annoyed and in some degree alarmed me. I inquired the way to the inn, but
5623: no one replied. I then moved forward, and a murmuring sound arose from the
5624: crowd as they followed and surrounded me, when an ill-looking man
5625: approaching tapped me on the shoulder and said, “Come, sir, you must
5626: follow me to Mr. Kirwin’s to give an account of yourself.”
5627: 
5628: “Who is Mr. Kirwin? Why am I to give an account of myself? Is not
5629: this a free country?”
5630: 
5631: “Ay, sir, free enough for honest folks. Mr. Kirwin is a magistrate,
5632: and you are to give an account of the death of a gentleman who was
5633: found murdered here last night.”
5634: 
5635: This answer startled me, but I presently recovered myself. I was innocent;
5636: that could easily be proved; accordingly I followed my conductor in silence
5637: and was led to one of the best houses in the town. I was ready to sink from
5638: fatigue and hunger, but being surrounded by a crowd, I thought it politic
5639: to rouse all my strength, that no physical debility might be construed into
5640: apprehension or conscious guilt. Little did I then expect the calamity that
5641: was in a few moments to overwhelm me and extinguish in horror and despair
5642: all fear of ignominy or death.
5643: 
5644: I must pause here, for it requires all my fortitude to recall the memory of
5645: the frightful events which I am about to relate, in proper detail, to my
5646: recollection.
5647: 
5648: 
5649: 
5650: 
5651: Chapter 21
5652: 
5653: 
5654: I was soon introduced into the presence of the magistrate, an old
5655: benevolent man with calm and mild manners. He looked upon me, however,
5656: with some degree of severity, and then, turning towards my conductors,
5657: he asked who appeared as witnesses on this occasion.
5658: 
5659: About half a dozen men came forward; and, one being selected by the
5660: magistrate, he deposed that he had been out fishing the night before with
5661: his son and brother-in-law, Daniel Nugent, when, about ten o’clock,
5662: they observed a strong northerly blast rising, and they accordingly put in
5663: for port. It was a very dark night, as the moon had not yet risen; they did
5664: not land at the harbour, but, as they had been accustomed, at a creek about
5665: two miles below. He walked on first, carrying a part of the fishing tackle,
5666: and his companions followed him at some distance. As he was proceeding
5667: along the sands, he struck his foot against something and fell at his
5668: length on the ground. His companions came up to assist him, and by the
5669: light of their lantern they found that he had fallen on the body of a man,
5670: who was to all appearance dead. Their first supposition was that it was the
5671: corpse of some person who had been drowned and was thrown on shore by the
5672: waves, but on examination they found that the clothes were not wet and even
5673: that the body was not then cold. They instantly carried it to the cottage
5674: of an old woman near the spot and endeavoured, but in vain, to restore it
5675: to life. It appeared to be a handsome young man, about five and twenty
5676: years of age. He had apparently been strangled, for there was no sign of
5677: any violence except the black mark of fingers on his neck.
5678: 
5679: The first part of this deposition did not in the least interest me, but
5680: when the mark of the fingers was mentioned I remembered the murder of
5681: my brother and felt myself extremely agitated; my limbs trembled, and a
5682: mist came over my eyes, which obliged me to lean on a chair for
5683: support. The magistrate observed me with a keen eye and of course drew
5684: an unfavourable augury from my manner.
5685: 
5686: The son confirmed his father’s account, but when Daniel Nugent was
5687: called he swore positively that just before the fall of his companion, he
5688: saw a boat, with a single man in it, at a short distance from the shore;
5689: and as far as he could judge by the light of a few stars, it was the same
5690: boat in which I had just landed.
5691: 
5692: A woman deposed that she lived near the beach and was standing at the door
5693: of her cottage, waiting for the return of the fishermen, about an hour
5694: before she heard of the discovery of the body, when she saw a boat with
5695: only one man in it push off from that part of the shore where the corpse
5696: was afterwards found.
5697: 
5698: Another woman confirmed the account of the fishermen having brought the
5699: body into her house; it was not cold. They put it into a bed and
5700: rubbed it, and Daniel went to the town for an apothecary, but life was
5701: quite gone.
5702: 
5703: Several other men were examined concerning my landing, and they agreed
5704: that, with the strong north wind that had arisen during the night, it
5705: was very probable that I had beaten about for many hours and had been
5706: obliged to return nearly to the same spot from which I had departed.
5707: Besides, they observed that it appeared that I had brought the body
5708: from another place, and it was likely that as I did not appear to know
5709: the shore, I might have put into the harbour ignorant of the distance
5710: of the town of —— from the place where I had deposited the corpse.
5711: 
5712: Mr. Kirwin, on hearing this evidence, desired that I should be taken into
5713: the room where the body lay for interment, that it might be observed what
5714: effect the sight of it would produce upon me. This idea was probably
5715: suggested by the extreme agitation I had exhibited when the mode of the
5716: murder had been described. I was accordingly conducted, by the magistrate
5717: and several other persons, to the inn. I could not help being struck by the
5718: strange coincidences that had taken place during this eventful night; but,
5719: knowing that I had been conversing with several persons in the island I had
5720: inhabited about the time that the body had been found, I was perfectly
5721: tranquil as to the consequences of the affair.
5722: 
5723: I entered the room where the corpse lay and was led up to the coffin. How
5724: can I describe my sensations on beholding it? I feel yet parched with
5725: horror, nor can I reflect on that terrible moment without shuddering and
5726: agony. The examination, the presence of the magistrate and witnesses,
5727: passed like a dream from my memory when I saw the lifeless form of Henry
5728: Clerval stretched before me. I gasped for breath, and throwing myself on
5729: the body, I exclaimed, “Have my murderous machinations deprived you
5730: also, my dearest Henry, of life? Two I have already destroyed; other
5731: victims await their destiny; but you, Clerval, my friend, my
5732: benefactor—”
5733: 
5734: The human frame could no longer support the agonies that I endured, and
5735: I was carried out of the room in strong convulsions.
5736: 
5737: A fever succeeded to this. I lay for two months on the point of death; my
5738: ravings, as I afterwards heard, were frightful; I called myself the
5739: murderer of William, of Justine, and of Clerval. Sometimes I entreated my
5740: attendants to assist me in the destruction of the fiend by whom I was
5741: tormented; and at others I felt the fingers of the monster already grasping
5742: my neck, and screamed aloud with agony and terror. Fortunately, as I spoke
5743: my native language, Mr. Kirwin alone understood me; but my gestures and
5744: bitter cries were sufficient to affright the other witnesses.
5745: 
5746: Why did I not die? More miserable than man ever was before, why did I not
5747: sink into forgetfulness and rest? Death snatches away many blooming
5748: children, the only hopes of their doting parents; how many brides and
5749: youthful lovers have been one day in the bloom of health and hope, and the
5750: next a prey for worms and the decay of the tomb! Of what materials was I
5751: made that I could thus resist so many shocks, which, like the turning of
5752: the wheel, continually renewed the torture?
5753: 
5754: But I was doomed to live and in two months found myself as awaking from
5755: a dream, in a prison, stretched on a wretched bed, surrounded by
5756: gaolers, turnkeys, bolts, and all the miserable apparatus of a dungeon.
5757: It was morning, I remember, when I thus awoke to understanding; I had
5758: forgotten the particulars of what had happened and only felt as if some
5759: great misfortune had suddenly overwhelmed me; but when I looked around
5760: and saw the barred windows and the squalidness of the room in which I
5761: was, all flashed across my memory and I groaned bitterly.
5762: 
5763: This sound disturbed an old woman who was sleeping in a chair beside
5764: me. She was a hired nurse, the wife of one of the turnkeys, and her
5765: countenance expressed all those bad qualities which often characterise
5766: that class. The lines of her face were hard and rude, like that of
5767: persons accustomed to see without sympathising in sights of misery. Her
5768: tone expressed her entire indifference; she addressed me in English,
5769: and the voice struck me as one that I had heard during my sufferings.
5770: 
5771: “Are you better now, sir?” said she.
5772: 
5773: I replied in the same language, with a feeble voice, “I believe I am;
5774: but if it be all true, if indeed I did not dream, I am sorry that I am
5775: still alive to feel this misery and horror.”
5776: 
5777: “For that matter,” replied the old woman, “if you mean about the
5778: gentleman you murdered, I believe that it were better for you if you
5779: were dead, for I fancy it will go hard with you! However, that’s none
5780: of my business; I am sent to nurse you and get you well; I do my duty
5781: with a safe conscience; it were well if everybody did the same.”
5782: 
5783: I turned with loathing from the woman who could utter so unfeeling a
5784: speech to a person just saved, on the very edge of death; but I felt
5785: languid and unable to reflect on all that had passed. The whole series
5786: of my life appeared to me as a dream; I sometimes doubted if indeed it
5787: were all true, for it never presented itself to my mind with the force
5788: of reality.
5789: 
5790: As the images that floated before me became more distinct, I grew
5791: feverish; a darkness pressed around me; no one was near me who soothed
5792: me with the gentle voice of love; no dear hand supported me. The
5793: physician came and prescribed medicines, and the old woman prepared
5794: them for me; but utter carelessness was visible in the first, and the
5795: expression of brutality was strongly marked in the visage of the
5796: second. Who could be interested in the fate of a murderer but the
5797: hangman who would gain his fee?
5798: 
5799: These were my first reflections, but I soon learned that Mr. Kirwin had
5800: shown me extreme kindness. He had caused the best room in the prison
5801: to be prepared for me (wretched indeed was the best); and it was he who
5802: had provided a physician and a nurse. It is true, he seldom came to
5803: see me, for although he ardently desired to relieve the sufferings of
5804: every human creature, he did not wish to be present at the agonies and
5805: miserable ravings of a murderer. He came, therefore, sometimes to see
5806: that I was not neglected, but his visits were short and with long
5807: intervals.
5808: 
5809: One day, while I was gradually recovering, I was seated in a chair, my eyes
5810: half open and my cheeks livid like those in death. I was overcome by gloom
5811: and misery and often reflected I had better seek death than desire to
5812: remain in a world which to me was replete with wretchedness. At one time I
5813: considered whether I should not declare myself guilty and suffer the
5814: penalty of the law, less innocent than poor Justine had been. Such were my
5815: thoughts when the door of my apartment was opened and Mr. Kirwin entered.
5816: His countenance expressed sympathy and compassion; he drew a chair close to
5817: mine and addressed me in French,
5818: 
5819: “I fear that this place is very shocking to you; can I do anything to
5820: make you more comfortable?”
5821: 
5822: “I thank you, but all that you mention is nothing to me; on the whole
5823: earth there is no comfort which I am capable of receiving.”
5824: 
5825: “I know that the sympathy of a stranger can be but of little relief to
5826: one borne down as you are by so strange a misfortune. But you will, I
5827: hope, soon quit this melancholy abode, for doubtless evidence can
5828: easily be brought to free you from the criminal charge.”
5829: 
5830: “That is my least concern; I am, by a course of strange events, become
5831: the most miserable of mortals. Persecuted and tortured as I am and
5832: have been, can death be any evil to me?”
5833: 
5834: “Nothing indeed could be more unfortunate and agonising than the
5835: strange chances that have lately occurred. You were thrown, by some
5836: surprising accident, on this shore, renowned for its hospitality,
5837: seized immediately, and charged with murder. The first sight that was
5838: presented to your eyes was the body of your friend, murdered in so
5839: unaccountable a manner and placed, as it were, by some fiend across
5840: your path.”
5841: 
5842: As Mr. Kirwin said this, notwithstanding the agitation I endured on
5843: this retrospect of my sufferings, I also felt considerable surprise at
5844: the knowledge he seemed to possess concerning me. I suppose some
5845: astonishment was exhibited in my countenance, for Mr. Kirwin hastened
5846: to say,
5847: 
5848: “Immediately upon your being taken ill, all the papers that were on
5849: your person were brought me, and I examined them that I might discover some
5850: trace by which I could send to your relations an account of your misfortune
5851: and illness. I found several letters, and, among others, one which I
5852: discovered from its commencement to be from your father. I instantly wrote
5853: to Geneva; nearly two months have elapsed since the departure of my letter.
5854: But you are ill; even now you tremble; you are unfit for agitation of any
5855: kind.”
5856: 
5857: “This suspense is a thousand times worse than the most horrible event;
5858: tell me what new scene of death has been acted, and whose murder I am
5859: now to lament?”
5860: 
5861: “Your family is perfectly well,” said Mr. Kirwin with
5862: gentleness; “and someone, a friend, is come to visit you.”
5863: 
5864: I know not by what chain of thought the idea presented itself, but it
5865: instantly darted into my mind that the murderer had come to mock at my
5866: misery and taunt me with the death of Clerval, as a new incitement for
5867: me to comply with his hellish desires. I put my hand before my eyes,
5868: and cried out in agony,
5869: 
5870: “Oh! Take him away! I cannot see him; for God’s sake, do not
5871: let him enter!”
5872: 
5873: Mr. Kirwin regarded me with a troubled countenance. He could not help
5874: regarding my exclamation as a presumption of my guilt and said in
5875: rather a severe tone,
5876: 
5877: “I should have thought, young man, that the presence of your father
5878: would have been welcome instead of inspiring such violent repugnance.”
5879: 
5880: “My father!” cried I, while every feature and every muscle was relaxed
5881: from anguish to pleasure. “Is my father indeed come? How kind, how
5882: very kind! But where is he, why does he not hasten to me?”
5883: 
5884: My change of manner surprised and pleased the magistrate; perhaps he
5885: thought that my former exclamation was a momentary return of delirium,
5886: and now he instantly resumed his former benevolence. He rose and
5887: quitted the room with my nurse, and in a moment my father entered it.
5888: 
5889: Nothing, at this moment, could have given me greater pleasure than the
5890: arrival of my father. I stretched out my hand to him and cried,
5891: 
5892: “Are you then safe—and Elizabeth—and Ernest?”
5893: 
5894: My father calmed me with assurances of their welfare and endeavoured, by
5895: dwelling on these subjects so interesting to my heart, to raise my
5896: desponding spirits; but he soon felt that a prison cannot be the abode of
5897: cheerfulness. “What a place is this that you inhabit, my son!”
5898: said he, looking mournfully at the barred windows and wretched appearance
5899: of the room. “You travelled to seek happiness, but a fatality seems
5900: to pursue you. And poor Clerval—”
5901: 
5902: The name of my unfortunate and murdered friend was an agitation too
5903: great to be endured in my weak state; I shed tears.
5904: 
5905: “Alas! Yes, my father,” replied I; “some destiny of the
5906: most horrible kind hangs over me, and I must live to fulfil it, or surely I
5907: should have died on the coffin of Henry.”
5908: 
5909: We were not allowed to converse for any length of time, for the
5910: precarious state of my health rendered every precaution necessary that
5911: could ensure tranquillity. Mr. Kirwin came in and insisted that my
5912: strength should not be exhausted by too much exertion. But the
5913: appearance of my father was to me like that of my good angel, and I
5914: gradually recovered my health.
5915: 
5916: As my sickness quitted me, I was absorbed by a gloomy and black
5917: melancholy that nothing could dissipate. The image of Clerval was
5918: for ever before me, ghastly and murdered. More than once the agitation
5919: into which these reflections threw me made my friends dread a dangerous
5920: relapse. Alas! Why did they preserve so miserable and detested a
5921: life? It was surely that I might fulfil my destiny, which is now
5922: drawing to a close. Soon, oh, very soon, will death extinguish these
5923: throbbings and relieve me from the mighty weight of anguish that bears
5924: me to the dust; and, in executing the award of justice, I shall also
5925: sink to rest. Then the appearance of death was distant, although the
5926: wish was ever present to my thoughts; and I often sat for hours
5927: motionless and speechless, wishing for some mighty revolution that
5928: might bury me and my destroyer in its ruins.
5929: 
5930: The season of the assizes approached. I had already been three months
5931: in prison, and although I was still weak and in continual danger of a
5932: relapse, I was obliged to travel nearly a hundred miles to the country
5933: town where the court was held. Mr. Kirwin charged himself with every
5934: care of collecting witnesses and arranging my defence. I was spared
5935: the disgrace of appearing publicly as a criminal, as the case was not
5936: brought before the court that decides on life and death. The grand
5937: jury rejected the bill, on its being proved that I was on the Orkney
5938: Islands at the hour the body of my friend was found; and a fortnight
5939: after my removal I was liberated from prison.
5940: 
5941: My father was enraptured on finding me freed from the vexations of a
5942: criminal charge, that I was again allowed to breathe the fresh
5943: atmosphere and permitted to return to my native country. I did not
5944: participate in these feelings, for to me the walls of a dungeon or a
5945: palace were alike hateful. The cup of life was poisoned for ever, and
5946: although the sun shone upon me, as upon the happy and gay of heart, I
5947: saw around me nothing but a dense and frightful darkness, penetrated by
5948: no light but the glimmer of two eyes that glared upon me. Sometimes
5949: they were the expressive eyes of Henry, languishing in death, the dark
5950: orbs nearly covered by the lids and the long black lashes that fringed
5951: them; sometimes it was the watery, clouded eyes of the monster, as I
5952: first saw them in my chamber at Ingolstadt.
5953: 
5954: My father tried to awaken in me the feelings of affection. He talked
5955: of Geneva, which I should soon visit, of Elizabeth and Ernest; but
5956: these words only drew deep groans from me. Sometimes, indeed, I felt a
5957: wish for happiness and thought with melancholy delight of my beloved
5958: cousin or longed, with a devouring _maladie du pays_, to see once more
5959: the blue lake and rapid Rhone, that had been so dear to me in early
5960: childhood; but my general state of feeling was a torpor in which a
5961: prison was as welcome a residence as the divinest scene in nature; and
5962: these fits were seldom interrupted but by paroxysms of anguish and
5963: despair. At these moments I often endeavoured to put an end to the
5964: existence I loathed, and it required unceasing attendance and vigilance
5965: to restrain me from committing some dreadful act of violence.
5966: 
5967: Yet one duty remained to me, the recollection of which finally
5968: triumphed over my selfish despair. It was necessary that I should
5969: return without delay to Geneva, there to watch over the lives of those
5970: I so fondly loved and to lie in wait for the murderer, that if any
5971: chance led me to the place of his concealment, or if he dared again to
5972: blast me by his presence, I might, with unfailing aim, put an end to
5973: the existence of the monstrous image which I had endued with the
5974: mockery of a soul still more monstrous. My father still desired to
5975: delay our departure, fearful that I could not sustain the fatigues of a
5976: journey, for I was a shattered wreck—the shadow of a human being. My
5977: strength was gone. I was a mere skeleton, and fever night and day
5978: preyed upon my wasted frame.
5979: 
5980: Still, as I urged our leaving Ireland with such inquietude and impatience,
5981: my father thought it best to yield. We took our passage on board a vessel
5982: bound for Havre-de-Grace and sailed with a fair wind from the Irish shores.
5983: It was midnight. I lay on the deck looking at the stars and listening to
5984: the dashing of the waves. I hailed the darkness that shut Ireland from my
5985: sight, and my pulse beat with a feverish joy when I reflected that I should
5986: soon see Geneva. The past appeared to me in the light of a frightful dream;
5987: yet the vessel in which I was, the wind that blew me from the detested
5988: shore of Ireland, and the sea which surrounded me, told me too forcibly
5989: that I was deceived by no vision and that Clerval, my friend and dearest
5990: companion, had fallen a victim to me and the monster of my creation. I
5991: repassed, in my memory, my whole life; my quiet happiness while residing
5992: with my family in Geneva, the death of my mother, and my departure for
5993: Ingolstadt. I remembered, shuddering, the mad enthusiasm that hurried me on
5994: to the creation of my hideous enemy, and I called to mind the night in
5995: which he first lived. I was unable to pursue the train of thought; a
5996: thousand feelings pressed upon me, and I wept bitterly.
5997: 
5998: Ever since my recovery from the fever, I had been in the custom of taking
5999: every night a small quantity of laudanum, for it was by means of this drug
6000: only that I was enabled to gain the rest necessary for the preservation of
6001: life. Oppressed by the recollection of my various misfortunes, I now
6002: swallowed double my usual quantity and soon slept profoundly. But sleep did
6003: not afford me respite from thought and misery; my dreams presented a
6004: thousand objects that scared me. Towards morning I was possessed by a kind
6005: of nightmare; I felt the fiend’s grasp in my neck and could not free
6006: myself from it; groans and cries rang in my ears. My father, who was
6007: watching over me, perceiving my restlessness, awoke me; the dashing waves
6008: were around, the cloudy sky above, the fiend was not here: a sense of
6009: security, a feeling that a truce was established between the present hour
6010: and the irresistible, disastrous future imparted to me a kind of calm
6011: forgetfulness, of which the human mind is by its structure peculiarly
6012: susceptible.
6013: 
6014: 
6015: 
6016: 
6017: Chapter 22
6018: 
6019: 
6020: The voyage came to an end. We landed, and proceeded to Paris. I soon
6021: found that I had overtaxed my strength and that I must repose before I
6022: could continue my journey. My father’s care and attentions were
6023: indefatigable, but he did not know the origin of my sufferings and
6024: sought erroneous methods to remedy the incurable ill. He wished me to
6025: seek amusement in society. I abhorred the face of man. Oh, not
6026: abhorred! They were my brethren, my fellow beings, and I felt
6027: attracted even to the most repulsive among them, as to creatures of an
6028: angelic nature and celestial mechanism. But I felt that I had no right
6029: to share their intercourse. I had unchained an enemy among them whose
6030: joy it was to shed their blood and to revel in their groans. How they
6031: would, each and all, abhor me and hunt me from the world, did they know
6032: my unhallowed acts and the crimes which had their source in me!
6033: 
6034: My father yielded at length to my desire to avoid society and strove by
6035: various arguments to banish my despair. Sometimes he thought that I
6036: felt deeply the degradation of being obliged to answer a charge of
6037: murder, and he endeavoured to prove to me the futility of pride.
6038: 
6039: “Alas! My father,” said I, “how little do you know me. 
6040: Human beings, their feelings and passions, would indeed be degraded if such
6041: a wretch as I felt pride. Justine, poor unhappy Justine, was as innocent
6042: as I, and she suffered the same charge; she died for it; and I am the cause
6043: of this—I murdered her. William, Justine, and Henry—they all
6044: died by my hands.”
6045: 
6046: My father had often, during my imprisonment, heard me make the same
6047: assertion; when I thus accused myself, he sometimes seemed to desire an
6048: explanation, and at others he appeared to consider it as the offspring of
6049: delirium, and that, during my illness, some idea of this kind had presented
6050: itself to my imagination, the remembrance of which I preserved in my
6051: convalescence. I avoided explanation and maintained a continual silence
6052: concerning the wretch I had created. I had a persuasion that I should be
6053: supposed mad, and this in itself would for ever have chained my tongue. But,
6054: besides, I could not bring myself to disclose a secret which would fill my
6055: hearer with consternation and make fear and unnatural horror the inmates of
6056: his breast. I checked, therefore, my impatient thirst for sympathy and was
6057: silent when I would have given the world to have confided the fatal secret.
6058: Yet, still, words like those I have recorded would burst uncontrollably
6059: from me. I could offer no explanation of them, but their truth in part
6060: relieved the burden of my mysterious woe.
6061: 
6062: Upon this occasion my father said, with an expression of unbounded wonder,
6063: “My dearest Victor, what infatuation is this? My dear son, I entreat
6064: you never to make such an assertion again.”
6065: 
6066: “I am not mad,” I cried energetically; “the sun and the heavens, who
6067: have viewed my operations, can bear witness of my truth. I am the
6068: assassin of those most innocent victims; they died by my machinations.
6069: A thousand times would I have shed my own blood, drop by drop, to have
6070: saved their lives; but I could not, my father, indeed I could not
6071: sacrifice the whole human race.”
6072: 
6073: The conclusion of this speech convinced my father that my ideas were
6074: deranged, and he instantly changed the subject of our conversation and
6075: endeavoured to alter the course of my thoughts. He wished as much as
6076: possible to obliterate the memory of the scenes that had taken place in
6077: Ireland and never alluded to them or suffered me to speak of my
6078: misfortunes.
6079: 
6080: As time passed away I became more calm; misery had her dwelling in my
6081: heart, but I no longer talked in the same incoherent manner of my own
6082: crimes; sufficient for me was the consciousness of them. By the utmost
6083: self-violence I curbed the imperious voice of wretchedness, which
6084: sometimes desired to declare itself to the whole world, and my manners
6085: were calmer and more composed than they had ever been since my journey
6086: to the sea of ice.
6087: 
6088: A few days before we left Paris on our way to Switzerland, I received the
6089: following letter from Elizabeth:
6090: 
6091: “My dear Friend,
6092: 
6093: “It gave me the greatest pleasure to receive a letter from my uncle
6094: dated at Paris; you are no longer at a formidable distance, and I may
6095: hope to see you in less than a fortnight. My poor cousin, how much you
6096: must have suffered! I expect to see you looking even more ill than
6097: when you quitted Geneva. This winter has been passed most miserably,
6098: tortured as I have been by anxious suspense; yet I hope to see peace in
6099: your countenance and to find that your heart is not totally void of
6100: comfort and tranquillity.
6101: 
6102: “Yet I fear that the same feelings now exist that made you so miserable
6103: a year ago, even perhaps augmented by time. I would not disturb you at
6104: this period, when so many misfortunes weigh upon you, but a
6105: conversation that I had with my uncle previous to his departure renders
6106: some explanation necessary before we meet.
6107: 
6108: Explanation! You may possibly say, What can Elizabeth have to explain? If
6109: you really say this, my questions are answered and all my doubts satisfied.
6110: But you are distant from me, and it is possible that you may dread and yet
6111: be pleased with this explanation; and in a probability of this being the
6112: case, I dare not any longer postpone writing what, during your absence, I
6113: have often wished to express to you but have never had the courage to begin.
6114: 
6115: “You well know, Victor, that our union had been the favourite plan of
6116: your parents ever since our infancy. We were told this when young, and
6117: taught to look forward to it as an event that would certainly take
6118: place. We were affectionate playfellows during childhood, and, I
6119: believe, dear and valued friends to one another as we grew older. But
6120: as brother and sister often entertain a lively affection towards each
6121: other without desiring a more intimate union, may not such also be our
6122: case? Tell me, dearest Victor. Answer me, I conjure you by our mutual
6123: happiness, with simple truth—Do you not love another?
6124: 
6125: “You have travelled; you have spent several years of your life at
6126: Ingolstadt; and I confess to you, my friend, that when I saw you last
6127: autumn so unhappy, flying to solitude from the society of every
6128: creature, I could not help supposing that you might regret our
6129: connection and believe yourself bound in honour to fulfil the wishes of
6130: your parents, although they opposed themselves to your inclinations.
6131: But this is false reasoning. I confess to you, my friend, that I love
6132: you and that in my airy dreams of futurity you have been my constant
6133: friend and companion. But it is your happiness I desire as well as my
6134: own when I declare to you that our marriage would render me eternally
6135: miserable unless it were the dictate of your own free choice. Even now
6136: I weep to think that, borne down as you are by the cruellest
6137: misfortunes, you may stifle, by the word _honour_, all hope of that
6138: love and happiness which would alone restore you to yourself. I, who
6139: have so disinterested an affection for you, may increase your miseries
6140: tenfold by being an obstacle to your wishes. Ah! Victor, be assured
6141: that your cousin and playmate has too sincere a love for you not to be
6142: made miserable by this supposition. Be happy, my friend; and if you
6143: obey me in this one request, remain satisfied that nothing on earth
6144: will have the power to interrupt my tranquillity.
6145: 
6146: “Do not let this letter disturb you; do not answer tomorrow, or the
6147: next day, or even until you come, if it will give you pain. My uncle
6148: will send me news of your health, and if I see but one smile on your
6149: lips when we meet, occasioned by this or any other exertion of mine, I
6150: shall need no other happiness.
6151: 
6152: “Elizabeth Lavenza.
6153: 
6154: 
6155: 
6156: “Geneva, May 18th, 17—”
6157: 
6158: 
6159: 
6160: This letter revived in my memory what I had before forgotten, the threat of
6161: the fiend—“_I will be with you on your
6162: wedding-night!_” Such was my sentence, and on that night would the
6163: dæmon employ every art to destroy me and tear me from the glimpse of
6164: happiness which promised partly to console my sufferings. On that night he
6165: had determined to consummate his crimes by my death. Well, be it so; a
6166: deadly struggle would then assuredly take place, in which if he were
6167: victorious I should be at peace and his power over me be at an end. If he
6168: were vanquished, I should be a free man. Alas! What freedom? Such as the
6169: peasant enjoys when his family have been massacred before his eyes, his
6170: cottage burnt, his lands laid waste, and he is turned adrift, homeless,
6171: penniless, and alone, but free. Such would be my liberty except that in my
6172: Elizabeth I possessed a treasure, alas, balanced by those horrors of
6173: remorse and guilt which would pursue me until death.
6174: 
6175: Sweet and beloved Elizabeth! I read and reread her letter, and some
6176: softened feelings stole into my heart and dared to whisper paradisiacal
6177: dreams of love and joy; but the apple was already eaten, and the
6178: angel’s arm bared to drive me from all hope. Yet I would die to make
6179: her happy. If the monster executed his threat, death was inevitable; yet,
6180: again, I considered whether my marriage would hasten my fate. My
6181: destruction might indeed arrive a few months sooner, but if my torturer
6182: should suspect that I postponed it, influenced by his menaces, he would
6183: surely find other and perhaps more dreadful means of revenge. He had vowed
6184: _to be with me on my wedding-night_, yet he did not consider that
6185: threat as binding him to peace in the meantime, for as if to show me that
6186: he was not yet satiated with blood, he had murdered Clerval immediately
6187: after the enunciation of his threats. I resolved, therefore, that if my
6188: immediate union with my cousin would conduce either to hers or my
6189: father’s happiness, my adversary’s designs against my life
6190: should not retard it a single hour.
6191: 
6192: In this state of mind I wrote to Elizabeth. My letter was calm and
6193: affectionate. “I fear, my beloved girl,” I said, “little happiness
6194: remains for us on earth; yet all that I may one day enjoy is centred in
6195: you. Chase away your idle fears; to you alone do I consecrate my life
6196: and my endeavours for contentment. I have one secret, Elizabeth, a
6197: dreadful one; when revealed to you, it will chill your frame with
6198: horror, and then, far from being surprised at my misery, you will only
6199: wonder that I survive what I have endured. I will confide this tale of
6200: misery and terror to you the day after our marriage shall take place,
6201: for, my sweet cousin, there must be perfect confidence between us. But
6202: until then, I conjure you, do not mention or allude to it. This I most
6203: earnestly entreat, and I know you will comply.”
6204: 
6205: In about a week after the arrival of Elizabeth’s letter we returned
6206: to Geneva. The sweet girl welcomed me with warm affection, yet tears were
6207: in her eyes as she beheld my emaciated frame and feverish cheeks. I saw a
6208: change in her also. She was thinner and had lost much of that heavenly
6209: vivacity that had before charmed me; but her gentleness and soft looks of
6210: compassion made her a more fit companion for one blasted and miserable as I
6211: was.
6212: 
6213: The tranquillity which I now enjoyed did not endure. Memory brought madness
6214: with it, and when I thought of what had passed, a real insanity possessed
6215: me; sometimes I was furious and burnt with rage, sometimes low and
6216: despondent. I neither spoke nor looked at anyone, but sat motionless,
6217: bewildered by the multitude of miseries that overcame me.
6218: 
6219: Elizabeth alone had the power to draw me from these fits; her gentle voice
6220: would soothe me when transported by passion and inspire me with human
6221: feelings when sunk in torpor. She wept with me and for me. When reason
6222: returned, she would remonstrate and endeavour to inspire me with
6223: resignation. Ah! It is well for the unfortunate to be resigned, but for the
6224: guilty there is no peace. The agonies of remorse poison the luxury there is
6225: otherwise sometimes found in indulging the excess of grief.
6226: 
6227: Soon after my arrival my father spoke of my immediate marriage with
6228: Elizabeth. I remained silent.
6229: 
6230: “Have you, then, some other attachment?”
6231: 
6232: “None on earth. I love Elizabeth and look forward to our union with
6233: delight. Let the day therefore be fixed; and on it I will consecrate
6234: myself, in life or death, to the happiness of my cousin.”
6235: 
6236: “My dear Victor, do not speak thus. Heavy misfortunes have befallen
6237: us, but let us only cling closer to what remains and transfer our love
6238: for those whom we have lost to those who yet live. Our circle will be
6239: small but bound close by the ties of affection and mutual misfortune.
6240: And when time shall have softened your despair, new and dear objects of
6241: care will be born to replace those of whom we have been so cruelly
6242: deprived.”
6243: 
6244: Such were the lessons of my father. But to me the remembrance of the
6245: threat returned; nor can you wonder that, omnipotent as the fiend had
6246: yet been in his deeds of blood, I should almost regard him as
6247: invincible, and that when he had pronounced the words “_I shall be with
6248: you on your wedding-night_,” I should regard the threatened fate as
6249: unavoidable. But death was no evil to me if the loss of Elizabeth were
6250: balanced with it, and I therefore, with a contented and even cheerful
6251: countenance, agreed with my father that if my cousin would consent, the
6252: ceremony should take place in ten days, and thus put, as I imagined,
6253: the seal to my fate.
6254: 
6255: Great God! If for one instant I had thought what might be the hellish
6256: intention of my fiendish adversary, I would rather have banished myself
6257: for ever from my native country and wandered a friendless outcast over
6258: the earth than have consented to this miserable marriage. But, as if
6259: possessed of magic powers, the monster had blinded me to his real
6260: intentions; and when I thought that I had prepared only my own death, I
6261: hastened that of a far dearer victim.
6262: 
6263: As the period fixed for our marriage drew nearer, whether from cowardice or
6264: a prophetic feeling, I felt my heart sink within me. But I concealed my
6265: feelings by an appearance of hilarity that brought smiles and joy to the
6266: countenance of my father, but hardly deceived the ever-watchful and nicer
6267: eye of Elizabeth. She looked forward to our union with placid contentment,
6268: not unmingled with a little fear, which past misfortunes had impressed,
6269: that what now appeared certain and tangible happiness might soon dissipate
6270: into an airy dream and leave no trace but deep and everlasting regret.
6271: 
6272: Preparations were made for the event, congratulatory visits were received,
6273: and all wore a smiling appearance. I shut up, as well as I could, in my own
6274: heart the anxiety that preyed there and entered with seeming earnestness
6275: into the plans of my father, although they might only serve as the
6276: decorations of my tragedy. Through my father’s exertions a part of
6277: the inheritance of Elizabeth had been restored to her by the Austrian
6278: government. A small possession on the shores of Como belonged to her. It
6279: was agreed that, immediately after our union, we should proceed to Villa
6280: Lavenza and spend our first days of happiness beside the beautiful lake
6281: near which it stood.
6282: 
6283: In the meantime I took every precaution to defend my person in case the
6284: fiend should openly attack me. I carried pistols and a dagger
6285: constantly about me and was ever on the watch to prevent artifice, and
6286: by these means gained a greater degree of tranquillity. Indeed, as the
6287: period approached, the threat appeared more as a delusion, not to be
6288: regarded as worthy to disturb my peace, while the happiness I hoped for
6289: in my marriage wore a greater appearance of certainty as the day fixed
6290: for its solemnisation drew nearer and I heard it continually spoken of
6291: as an occurrence which no accident could possibly prevent.
6292: 
6293: Elizabeth seemed happy; my tranquil demeanour contributed greatly to
6294: calm her mind. But on the day that was to fulfil my wishes and my
6295: destiny, she was melancholy, and a presentiment of evil pervaded her;
6296: and perhaps also she thought of the dreadful secret which I had
6297: promised to reveal to her on the following day. My father was in the
6298: meantime overjoyed, and, in the bustle of preparation, only recognised in
6299: the melancholy of his niece the diffidence of a bride.
6300: 
6301: After the ceremony was performed a large party assembled at my
6302: father’s, but it was agreed that Elizabeth and I should commence our
6303: journey by water, sleeping that night at Evian and continuing our
6304: voyage on the following day. The day was fair, the wind favourable;
6305: all smiled on our nuptial embarkation.
6306: 
6307: Those were the last moments of my life during which I enjoyed the
6308: feeling of happiness. We passed rapidly along; the sun was hot, but we
6309: were sheltered from its rays by a kind of canopy while we enjoyed the
6310: beauty of the scene, sometimes on one side of the lake, where we saw
6311: Mont Salêve, the pleasant banks of Montalègre, and at a distance,
6312: surmounting all, the beautiful Mont Blanc, and the assemblage of snowy
6313: mountains that in vain endeavour to emulate her; sometimes coasting the
6314: opposite banks, we saw the mighty Jura opposing its dark side to the
6315: ambition that would quit its native country, and an almost
6316: insurmountable barrier to the invader who should wish to enslave it.
6317: 
6318: I took the hand of Elizabeth. “You are sorrowful, my love. Ah! If
6319: you knew what I have suffered and what I may yet endure, you would
6320: endeavour to let me taste the quiet and freedom from despair that this
6321: one day at least permits me to enjoy.”
6322: 
6323: “Be happy, my dear Victor,” replied Elizabeth; “there is, I hope,
6324: nothing to distress you; and be assured that if a lively joy is not
6325: painted in my face, my heart is contented. Something whispers to me
6326: not to depend too much on the prospect that is opened before us, but I
6327: will not listen to such a sinister voice. Observe how fast we move
6328: along and how the clouds, which sometimes obscure and sometimes rise
6329: above the dome of Mont Blanc, render this scene of beauty still more
6330: interesting. Look also at the innumerable fish that are swimming in
6331: the clear waters, where we can distinguish every pebble that lies at
6332: the bottom. What a divine day! How happy and serene all nature
6333: appears!”
6334: 
6335: Thus Elizabeth endeavoured to divert her thoughts and mine from all
6336: reflection upon melancholy subjects. But her temper was fluctuating;
6337: joy for a few instants shone in her eyes, but it continually gave place
6338: to distraction and reverie.
6339: 
6340: The sun sank lower in the heavens; we passed the river Drance and
6341: observed its path through the chasms of the higher and the glens of the
6342: lower hills. The Alps here come closer to the lake, and we approached
6343: the amphitheatre of mountains which forms its eastern boundary. The
6344: spire of Evian shone under the woods that surrounded it and the range
6345: of mountain above mountain by which it was overhung.
6346: 
6347: The wind, which had hitherto carried us along with amazing rapidity,
6348: sank at sunset to a light breeze; the soft air just ruffled the water
6349: and caused a pleasant motion among the trees as we approached the
6350: shore, from which it wafted the most delightful scent of flowers and
6351: hay. The sun sank beneath the horizon as we landed, and as I touched
6352: the shore I felt those cares and fears revive which soon were to clasp
6353: me and cling to me for ever.
6354: 
6355: 
6356: 
6357: 
6358: Chapter 23
6359: 
6360: 
6361: It was eight o’clock when we landed; we walked for a short time on the
6362: shore, enjoying the transitory light, and then retired to the inn and
6363: contemplated the lovely scene of waters, woods, and mountains, obscured
6364: in darkness, yet still displaying their black outlines.
6365: 
6366: The wind, which had fallen in the south, now rose with great violence
6367: in the west. The moon had reached her summit in the heavens and was
6368: beginning to descend; the clouds swept across it swifter than the
6369: flight of the vulture and dimmed her rays, while the lake reflected the
6370: scene of the busy heavens, rendered still busier by the restless waves
6371: that were beginning to rise. Suddenly a heavy storm of rain descended.
6372: 
6373: I had been calm during the day, but so soon as night obscured the
6374: shapes of objects, a thousand fears arose in my mind. I was anxious
6375: and watchful, while my right hand grasped a pistol which was hidden in
6376: my bosom; every sound terrified me, but I resolved that I would sell my
6377: life dearly and not shrink from the conflict until my own life or that
6378: of my adversary was extinguished.
6379: 
6380: Elizabeth observed my agitation for some time in timid and fearful silence,
6381: but there was something in my glance which communicated terror to her, and
6382: trembling, she asked, “What is it that agitates you, my dear Victor?
6383: What is it you fear?”
6384: 
6385: “Oh! Peace, peace, my love,” replied I; “this night, and
6386: all will be safe; but this night is dreadful, very dreadful.”
6387: 
6388: I passed an hour in this state of mind, when suddenly I reflected how
6389: fearful the combat which I momentarily expected would be to my wife,
6390: and I earnestly entreated her to retire, resolving not to join her
6391: until I had obtained some knowledge as to the situation of my enemy.
6392: 
6393: She left me, and I continued some time walking up and down the passages
6394: of the house and inspecting every corner that might afford a retreat to
6395: my adversary. But I discovered no trace of him and was beginning to
6396: conjecture that some fortunate chance had intervened to prevent the
6397: execution of his menaces when suddenly I heard a shrill and dreadful
6398: scream. It came from the room into which Elizabeth had retired. As I
6399: heard it, the whole truth rushed into my mind, my arms dropped, the
6400: motion of every muscle and fibre was suspended; I could feel the blood
6401: trickling in my veins and tingling in the extremities of my limbs. This
6402: state lasted but for an instant; the scream was repeated, and I rushed
6403: into the room.
6404: 
6405: Great God! Why did I not then expire! Why am I here to relate the
6406: destruction of the best hope and the purest creature on earth? She was
6407: there, lifeless and inanimate, thrown across the bed, her head hanging down
6408: and her pale and distorted features half covered by her hair. Everywhere I
6409: turn I see the same figure—her bloodless arms and relaxed form flung
6410: by the murderer on its bridal bier. Could I behold this and live? Alas!
6411: Life is obstinate and clings closest where it is most hated. For a moment
6412: only did I lose recollection; I fell senseless on the ground.
6413: 
6414: When I recovered I found myself surrounded by the people of the inn; their
6415: countenances expressed a breathless terror, but the horror of others
6416: appeared only as a mockery, a shadow of the feelings that oppressed me. I
6417: escaped from them to the room where lay the body of Elizabeth, my love, my
6418: wife, so lately living, so dear, so worthy. She had been moved from the
6419: posture in which I had first beheld her, and now, as she lay, her head upon
6420: her arm and a handkerchief thrown across her face and neck, I might have
6421: supposed her asleep. I rushed towards her and embraced her with ardour, but
6422: the deadly languor and coldness of the limbs told me that what I now held
6423: in my arms had ceased to be the Elizabeth whom I had loved and cherished.
6424: The murderous mark of the fiend’s grasp was on her neck, and the
6425: breath had ceased to issue from her lips.
6426: 
6427: While I still hung over her in the agony of despair, I happened to look up.
6428: The windows of the room had before been darkened, and I felt a kind of
6429: panic on seeing the pale yellow light of the moon illuminate the chamber.
6430: The shutters had been thrown back, and with a sensation of horror not to be
6431: described, I saw at the open window a figure the most hideous and abhorred.
6432: A grin was on the face of the monster; he seemed to jeer, as with his
6433: fiendish finger he pointed towards the corpse of my wife. I rushed towards
6434: the window, and drawing a pistol from my bosom, fired; but he eluded me,
6435: leaped from his station, and running with the swiftness of lightning,
6436: plunged into the lake.
6437: 
6438: The report of the pistol brought a crowd into the room. I pointed to
6439: the spot where he had disappeared, and we followed the track with
6440: boats; nets were cast, but in vain. After passing several hours, we
6441: returned hopeless, most of my companions believing it to have been a
6442: form conjured up by my fancy. After having landed, they proceeded to
6443: search the country, parties going in different directions among the
6444: woods and vines.
6445: 
6446: I attempted to accompany them and proceeded a short distance from the
6447: house, but my head whirled round, my steps were like those of a drunken
6448: man, I fell at last in a state of utter exhaustion; a film covered my
6449: eyes, and my skin was parched with the heat of fever. In this state I
6450: was carried back and placed on a bed, hardly conscious of what had
6451: happened; my eyes wandered round the room as if to seek something that
6452: I had lost.
6453: 
6454: After an interval I arose, and as if by instinct, crawled into the room
6455: where the corpse of my beloved lay. There were women weeping around; I
6456: hung over it and joined my sad tears to theirs; all this time no
6457: distinct idea presented itself to my mind, but my thoughts rambled to
6458: various subjects, reflecting confusedly on my misfortunes and their
6459: cause. I was bewildered, in a cloud of wonder and horror. The death
6460: of William, the execution of Justine, the murder of Clerval, and lastly
6461: of my wife; even at that moment I knew not that my only remaining
6462: friends were safe from the malignity of the fiend; my father even now
6463: might be writhing under his grasp, and Ernest might be dead at his
6464: feet. This idea made me shudder and recalled me to action. I started
6465: up and resolved to return to Geneva with all possible speed.
6466: 
6467: There were no horses to be procured, and I must return by the lake; but the
6468: wind was unfavourable, and the rain fell in torrents. However, it was
6469: hardly morning, and I might reasonably hope to arrive by night. I hired men
6470: to row and took an oar myself, for I had always experienced relief from
6471: mental torment in bodily exercise. But the overflowing misery I now felt,
6472: and the excess of agitation that I endured rendered me incapable of any
6473: exertion. I threw down the oar, and leaning my head upon my hands, gave way
6474: to every gloomy idea that arose. If I looked up, I saw scenes which were
6475: familiar to me in my happier time and which I had contemplated but the day
6476: before in the company of her who was now but a shadow and a recollection.
6477: Tears streamed from my eyes. The rain had ceased for a moment, and I saw
6478: the fish play in the waters as they had done a few hours before; they had
6479: then been observed by Elizabeth. Nothing is so painful to the human mind as
6480: a great and sudden change. The sun might shine or the clouds might lower,
6481: but nothing could appear to me as it had done the day before. A fiend had
6482: snatched from me every hope of future happiness; no creature had ever been
6483: so miserable as I was; so frightful an event is single in the history of
6484: man.
6485: 
6486: But why should I dwell upon the incidents that followed this last
6487: overwhelming event? Mine has been a tale of horrors; I have reached their
6488: _acme_, and what I must now relate can but be tedious to you. Know
6489: that, one by one, my friends were snatched away; I was left desolate. My
6490: own strength is exhausted, and I must tell, in a few words, what remains of
6491: my hideous narration.
6492: 
6493: I arrived at Geneva. My father and Ernest yet lived, but the former sunk
6494: under the tidings that I bore. I see him now, excellent and venerable old
6495: man! His eyes wandered in vacancy, for they had lost their charm and their
6496: delight—his Elizabeth, his more than daughter, whom he doted on with
6497: all that affection which a man feels, who in the decline of life, having
6498: few affections, clings more earnestly to those that remain. Cursed, cursed
6499: be the fiend that brought misery on his grey hairs and doomed him to waste
6500: in wretchedness! He could not live under the horrors that were accumulated
6501: around him; the springs of existence suddenly gave way; he was unable to
6502: rise from his bed, and in a few days he died in my arms.
6503: 
6504: What then became of me? I know not; I lost sensation, and chains and
6505: darkness were the only objects that pressed upon me. Sometimes,
6506: indeed, I dreamt that I wandered in flowery meadows and pleasant vales
6507: with the friends of my youth, but I awoke and found myself in a
6508: dungeon. Melancholy followed, but by degrees I gained a clear
6509: conception of my miseries and situation and was then released from my
6510: prison. For they had called me mad, and during many months, as I
6511: understood, a solitary cell had been my habitation.
6512: 
6513: Liberty, however, had been a useless gift to me, had I not, as I
6514: awakened to reason, at the same time awakened to revenge. As the
6515: memory of past misfortunes pressed upon me, I began to reflect on their
6516: cause—the monster whom I had created, the miserable dæmon whom I had
6517: sent abroad into the world for my destruction. I was possessed by a
6518: maddening rage when I thought of him, and desired and ardently prayed
6519: that I might have him within my grasp to wreak a great and signal
6520: revenge on his cursed head.
6521: 
6522: Nor did my hate long confine itself to useless wishes; I began to
6523: reflect on the best means of securing him; and for this purpose, about
6524: a month after my release, I repaired to a criminal judge in the town
6525: and told him that I had an accusation to make, that I knew the
6526: destroyer of my family, and that I required him to exert his whole
6527: authority for the apprehension of the murderer.
6528: 
6529: The magistrate listened to me with attention and kindness. “Be
6530: assured, sir,” said he, “no pains or exertions on my part shall
6531: be spared to discover the villain.”
6532: 
6533: “I thank you,” replied I; “listen, therefore, to the
6534: deposition that I have to make. It is indeed a tale so strange that I
6535: should fear you would not credit it were there not something in truth
6536: which, however wonderful, forces conviction. The story is too connected to
6537: be mistaken for a dream, and I have no motive for falsehood.” My
6538: manner as I thus addressed him was impressive but calm; I had formed in my
6539: own heart a resolution to pursue my destroyer to death, and this purpose
6540: quieted my agony and for an interval reconciled me to life. I now related
6541: my history briefly but with firmness and precision, marking the dates with
6542: accuracy and never deviating into invective or exclamation.
6543: 
6544: The magistrate appeared at first perfectly incredulous, but as I continued
6545: he became more attentive and interested; I saw him sometimes shudder with
6546: horror; at others a lively surprise, unmingled with disbelief, was painted
6547: on his countenance.
6548: 
6549: When I had concluded my narration, I said, “This is the being whom I
6550: accuse and for whose seizure and punishment I call upon you to exert your
6551: whole power. It is your duty as a magistrate, and I believe and hope that
6552: your feelings as a man will not revolt from the execution of those
6553: functions on this occasion.”
6554: 
6555: This address caused a considerable change in the physiognomy of my own
6556: auditor. He had heard my story with that half kind of belief that is given
6557: to a tale of spirits and supernatural events; but when he was called upon
6558: to act officially in consequence, the whole tide of his incredulity
6559: returned. He, however, answered mildly, “I would willingly afford you
6560: every aid in your pursuit, but the creature of whom you speak appears to
6561: have powers which would put all my exertions to defiance. Who can follow an
6562: animal which can traverse the sea of ice and inhabit caves and dens where
6563: no man would venture to intrude? Besides, some months have elapsed since
6564: the commission of his crimes, and no one can conjecture to what place he
6565: has wandered or what region he may now inhabit.”
6566: 
6567: “I do not doubt that he hovers near the spot which I inhabit, and if
6568: he has indeed taken refuge in the Alps, he may be hunted like the chamois
6569: and destroyed as a beast of prey. But I perceive your thoughts; you do not
6570: credit my narrative and do not intend to pursue my enemy with the
6571: punishment which is his desert.”
6572: 
6573: As I spoke, rage sparkled in my eyes; the magistrate was intimidated.
6574: “You are mistaken,” said he. “I will exert myself, and if
6575: it is in my power to seize the monster, be assured that he shall suffer
6576: punishment proportionate to his crimes. But I fear, from what you have
6577: yourself described to be his properties, that this will prove
6578: impracticable; and thus, while every proper measure is pursued, you should
6579: make up your mind to disappointment.”
6580: 
6581: “That cannot be; but all that I can say will be of little avail. My
6582: revenge is of no moment to you; yet, while I allow it to be a vice, I
6583: confess that it is the devouring and only passion of my soul. My rage
6584: is unspeakable when I reflect that the murderer, whom I have turned
6585: loose upon society, still exists. You refuse my just demand; I have
6586: but one resource, and I devote myself, either in my life or death, to
6587: his destruction.”
6588: 
6589: I trembled with excess of agitation as I said this; there was a frenzy
6590: in my manner, and something, I doubt not, of that haughty fierceness
6591: which the martyrs of old are said to have possessed. But to a Genevan
6592: magistrate, whose mind was occupied by far other ideas than those of
6593: devotion and heroism, this elevation of mind had much the appearance of
6594: madness. He endeavoured to soothe me as a nurse does a child and
6595: reverted to my tale as the effects of delirium.
6596: 
6597: “Man,” I cried, “how ignorant art thou in thy pride of
6598: wisdom! Cease; you know not what it is you say.”
6599: 
6600: I broke from the house angry and disturbed and retired to meditate on
6601: some other mode of action.
6602: 
6603: 
6604: 
6605: 
6606: Chapter 24
6607: 
6608: 
6609: My present situation was one in which all voluntary thought was
6610: swallowed up and lost. I was hurried away by fury; revenge alone
6611: endowed me with strength and composure; it moulded my feelings and
6612: allowed me to be calculating and calm at periods when otherwise
6613: delirium or death would have been my portion.
6614: 
6615: My first resolution was to quit Geneva for ever; my country, which, when I
6616: was happy and beloved, was dear to me, now, in my adversity, became
6617: hateful. I provided myself with a sum of money, together with a few jewels
6618: which had belonged to my mother, and departed.
6619: 
6620: And now my wanderings began which are to cease but with life. I have
6621: traversed a vast portion of the earth and have endured all the hardships
6622: which travellers in deserts and barbarous countries are wont to meet. How I
6623: have lived I hardly know; many times have I stretched my failing limbs upon
6624: the sandy plain and prayed for death. But revenge kept me alive; I dared
6625: not die and leave my adversary in being.
6626: 
6627: When I quitted Geneva my first labour was to gain some clue by which I
6628: might trace the steps of my fiendish enemy. But my plan was unsettled,
6629: and I wandered many hours round the confines of the town, uncertain
6630: what path I should pursue. As night approached I found myself at the
6631: entrance of the cemetery where William, Elizabeth, and my father
6632: reposed. I entered it and approached the tomb which marked their
6633: graves. Everything was silent except the leaves of the trees, which
6634: were gently agitated by the wind; the night was nearly dark, and the
6635: scene would have been solemn and affecting even to an uninterested
6636: observer. The spirits of the departed seemed to flit around and to
6637: cast a shadow, which was felt but not seen, around the head of the
6638: mourner.
6639: 
6640: The deep grief which this scene had at first excited quickly gave way to
6641: rage and despair. They were dead, and I lived; their murderer also lived,
6642: and to destroy him I must drag out my weary existence. I knelt on the grass
6643: and kissed the earth and with quivering lips exclaimed, “By the
6644: sacred earth on which I kneel, by the shades that wander near me, by the
6645: deep and eternal grief that I feel, I swear; and by thee, O Night, and the
6646: spirits that preside over thee, to pursue the dæmon who caused this misery,
6647: until he or I shall perish in mortal conflict. For this purpose I will
6648: preserve my life; to execute this dear revenge will I again behold the sun
6649: and tread the green herbage of earth, which otherwise should vanish from my
6650: eyes for ever. And I call on you, spirits of the dead, and on you, wandering
6651: ministers of vengeance, to aid and conduct me in my work. Let the cursed
6652: and hellish monster drink deep of agony; let him feel the despair that now
6653: torments me.”
6654: 
6655: I had begun my adjuration with solemnity and an awe which almost assured me
6656: that the shades of my murdered friends heard and approved my devotion, but
6657: the furies possessed me as I concluded, and rage choked my utterance.
6658: 
6659: I was answered through the stillness of night by a loud and fiendish
6660: laugh. It rang on my ears long and heavily; the mountains re-echoed
6661: it, and I felt as if all hell surrounded me with mockery and laughter.
6662: Surely in that moment I should have been possessed by frenzy and have
6663: destroyed my miserable existence but that my vow was heard and that I
6664: was reserved for vengeance. The laughter died away, when a well-known
6665: and abhorred voice, apparently close to my ear, addressed me in an
6666: audible whisper, “I am satisfied, miserable wretch! You have
6667: determined to live, and I am satisfied.”
6668: 
6669: I darted towards the spot from which the sound proceeded, but the devil
6670: eluded my grasp. Suddenly the broad disk of the moon arose and shone
6671: full upon his ghastly and distorted shape as he fled with more than
6672: mortal speed.
6673: 
6674: I pursued him, and for many months this has been my task. Guided by a
6675: slight clue, I followed the windings of the Rhone, but vainly. The
6676: blue Mediterranean appeared, and by a strange chance, I saw the fiend
6677: enter by night and hide himself in a vessel bound for the Black Sea. I
6678: took my passage in the same ship, but he escaped, I know not how.
6679: 
6680: Amidst the wilds of Tartary and Russia, although he still evaded me, I
6681: have ever followed in his track. Sometimes the peasants, scared by
6682: this horrid apparition, informed me of his path; sometimes he himself,
6683: who feared that if I lost all trace of him I should despair and die,
6684: left some mark to guide me. The snows descended on my head, and I saw
6685: the print of his huge step on the white plain. To you first entering
6686: on life, to whom care is new and agony unknown, how can you understand
6687: what I have felt and still feel? Cold, want, and fatigue were the
6688: least pains which I was destined to endure; I was cursed by some devil
6689: and carried about with me my eternal hell; yet still a spirit of good
6690: followed and directed my steps and when I most murmured would suddenly
6691: extricate me from seemingly insurmountable difficulties. Sometimes,
6692: when nature, overcome by hunger, sank under the exhaustion, a repast
6693: was prepared for me in the desert that restored and inspirited me. The
6694: fare was, indeed, coarse, such as the peasants of the country ate, but
6695: I will not doubt that it was set there by the spirits that I had
6696: invoked to aid me. Often, when all was dry, the heavens cloudless, and
6697: I was parched by thirst, a slight cloud would bedim the sky, shed the
6698: few drops that revived me, and vanish.
6699: 
6700: I followed, when I could, the courses of the rivers; but the dæmon
6701: generally avoided these, as it was here that the population of the
6702: country chiefly collected. In other places human beings were seldom
6703: seen, and I generally subsisted on the wild animals that crossed my
6704: path. I had money with me and gained the friendship of the villagers
6705: by distributing it; or I brought with me some food that I had killed,
6706: which, after taking a small part, I always presented to those who had
6707: provided me with fire and utensils for cooking.
6708: 
6709: My life, as it passed thus, was indeed hateful to me, and it was during
6710: sleep alone that I could taste joy. O blessed sleep! Often, when most
6711: miserable, I sank to repose, and my dreams lulled me even to rapture. The
6712: spirits that guarded me had provided these moments, or rather hours, of
6713: happiness that I might retain strength to fulfil my pilgrimage. Deprived of
6714: this respite, I should have sunk under my hardships. During the day I was
6715: sustained and inspirited by the hope of night, for in sleep I saw my
6716: friends, my wife, and my beloved country; again I saw the benevolent
6717: countenance of my father, heard the silver tones of my Elizabeth’s
6718: voice, and beheld Clerval enjoying health and youth. Often, when wearied by
6719: a toilsome march, I persuaded myself that I was dreaming until night should
6720: come and that I should then enjoy reality in the arms of my dearest
6721: friends. What agonising fondness did I feel for them! How did I cling to
6722: their dear forms, as sometimes they haunted even my waking hours, and
6723: persuade myself that they still lived! At such moments vengeance, that
6724: burned within me, died in my heart, and I pursued my path towards the
6725: destruction of the dæmon more as a task enjoined by heaven, as the
6726: mechanical impulse of some power of which I was unconscious, than as the
6727: ardent desire of my soul.
6728: 
6729: What his feelings were whom I pursued I cannot know. Sometimes, indeed, he
6730: left marks in writing on the barks of the trees or cut in stone that guided
6731: me and instigated my fury. “My reign is not yet
6732: over”—these words were legible in one of these
6733: inscriptions—“you live, and my power is complete. Follow me; I
6734: seek the everlasting ices of the north, where you will feel the misery of
6735: cold and frost, to which I am impassive. You will find near this place, if
6736: you follow not too tardily, a dead hare; eat and be refreshed. Come on, my
6737: enemy; we have yet to wrestle for our lives, but many hard and miserable
6738: hours must you endure until that period shall arrive.”
6739: 
6740: Scoffing devil! Again do I vow vengeance; again do I devote thee,
6741: miserable fiend, to torture and death. Never will I give up my search
6742: until he or I perish; and then with what ecstasy shall I join my
6743: Elizabeth and my departed friends, who even now prepare for me the
6744: reward of my tedious toil and horrible pilgrimage!
6745: 
6746: As I still pursued my journey to the northward, the snows thickened and the
