6747: cold increased in a degree almost too severe to support. The peasants were
6748: shut up in their hovels, and only a few of the most hardy ventured forth to
6749: seize the animals whom starvation had forced from their hiding-places to
6750: seek for prey. The rivers were covered with ice, and no fish could be
6751: procured; and thus I was cut off from my chief article of maintenance.
6752: 
6753: The triumph of my enemy increased with the difficulty of my labours. One
6754: inscription that he left was in these words: “Prepare! Your toils
6755: only begin; wrap yourself in furs and provide food, for we shall soon enter
6756: upon a journey where your sufferings will satisfy my everlasting
6757: hatred.”
6758: 
6759: My courage and perseverance were invigorated by these scoffing words; I
6760: resolved not to fail in my purpose, and calling on Heaven to support
6761: me, I continued with unabated fervour to traverse immense deserts,
6762: until the ocean appeared at a distance and formed the utmost boundary
6763: of the horizon. Oh! How unlike it was to the blue seasons of the
6764: south! Covered with ice, it was only to be distinguished from land by
6765: its superior wildness and ruggedness. The Greeks wept for joy when
6766: they beheld the Mediterranean from the hills of Asia, and hailed with
6767: rapture the boundary of their toils. I did not weep, but I knelt down
6768: and with a full heart thanked my guiding spirit for conducting me in
6769: safety to the place where I hoped, notwithstanding my adversary’s gibe,
6770: to meet and grapple with him.
6771: 
6772: Some weeks before this period I had procured a sledge and dogs and thus
6773: traversed the snows with inconceivable speed. I know not whether the
6774: fiend possessed the same advantages, but I found that, as before I had
6775: daily lost ground in the pursuit, I now gained on him, so much so that
6776: when I first saw the ocean he was but one day’s journey in advance, and
6777: I hoped to intercept him before he should reach the beach. With new
6778: courage, therefore, I pressed on, and in two days arrived at a wretched
6779: hamlet on the seashore. I inquired of the inhabitants concerning the
6780: fiend and gained accurate information. A gigantic monster, they said,
6781: had arrived the night before, armed with a gun and many pistols,
6782: putting to flight the inhabitants of a solitary cottage through fear of
6783: his terrific appearance. He had carried off their store of winter
6784: food, and placing it in a sledge, to draw which he had seized on a
6785: numerous drove of trained dogs, he had harnessed them, and the same
6786: night, to the joy of the horror-struck villagers, had pursued his
6787: journey across the sea in a direction that led to no land; and they
6788: conjectured that he must speedily be destroyed by the breaking of the
6789: ice or frozen by the eternal frosts.
6790: 
6791: On hearing this information I suffered a temporary access of despair.
6792: He had escaped me, and I must commence a destructive and almost endless
6793: journey across the mountainous ices of the ocean, amidst cold that few
6794: of the inhabitants could long endure and which I, the native of a
6795: genial and sunny climate, could not hope to survive. Yet at the idea
6796: that the fiend should live and be triumphant, my rage and vengeance
6797: returned, and like a mighty tide, overwhelmed every other feeling.
6798: After a slight repose, during which the spirits of the dead hovered
6799: round and instigated me to toil and revenge, I prepared for my journey.
6800: 
6801: I exchanged my land-sledge for one fashioned for the inequalities of
6802: the Frozen Ocean, and purchasing a plentiful stock of provisions, I
6803: departed from land.
6804: 
6805: I cannot guess how many days have passed since then, but I have endured
6806: misery which nothing but the eternal sentiment of a just retribution
6807: burning within my heart could have enabled me to support. Immense and
6808: rugged mountains of ice often barred up my passage, and I often heard
6809: the thunder of the ground sea, which threatened my destruction. But
6810: again the frost came and made the paths of the sea secure.
6811: 
6812: By the quantity of provision which I had consumed, I should guess that
6813: I had passed three weeks in this journey; and the continual protraction
6814: of hope, returning back upon the heart, often wrung bitter drops of
6815: despondency and grief from my eyes. Despair had indeed almost secured
6816: her prey, and I should soon have sunk beneath this misery. Once, after
6817: the poor animals that conveyed me had with incredible toil gained the
6818: summit of a sloping ice mountain, and one, sinking under his fatigue,
6819: died, I viewed the expanse before me with anguish, when suddenly my eye
6820: caught a dark speck upon the dusky plain. I strained my sight to
6821: discover what it could be and uttered a wild cry of ecstasy when I
6822: distinguished a sledge and the distorted proportions of a well-known
6823: form within. Oh! With what a burning gush did hope revisit my heart!
6824: Warm tears filled my eyes, which I hastily wiped away, that they might
6825: not intercept the view I had of the dæmon; but still my sight was
6826: dimmed by the burning drops, until, giving way to the emotions that
6827: oppressed me, I wept aloud.
6828: 
6829: But this was not the time for delay; I disencumbered the dogs of their
6830: dead companion, gave them a plentiful portion of food, and after an
6831: hour’s rest, which was absolutely necessary, and yet which was bitterly
6832: irksome to me, I continued my route. The sledge was still visible, nor
6833: did I again lose sight of it except at the moments when for a short
6834: time some ice-rock concealed it with its intervening crags. I indeed
6835: perceptibly gained on it, and when, after nearly two days’ journey, I
6836: beheld my enemy at no more than a mile distant, my heart bounded within
6837: me.
6838: 
6839: But now, when I appeared almost within grasp of my foe, my hopes were
6840: suddenly extinguished, and I lost all trace of him more utterly than I had
6841: ever done before. A ground sea was heard; the thunder of its progress, as
6842: the waters rolled and swelled beneath me, became every moment more ominous
6843: and terrific. I pressed on, but in vain. The wind arose; the sea roared;
6844: and, as with the mighty shock of an earthquake, it split and cracked with a
6845: tremendous and overwhelming sound. The work was soon finished; in a few
6846: minutes a tumultuous sea rolled between me and my enemy, and I was left
6847: drifting on a scattered piece of ice that was continually lessening and
6848: thus preparing for me a hideous death.
6849: 
6850: In this manner many appalling hours passed; several of my dogs died, and I
6851: myself was about to sink under the accumulation of distress when I saw your
6852: vessel riding at anchor and holding forth to me hopes of succour and life.
6853: I had no conception that vessels ever came so far north and was astounded
6854: at the sight. I quickly destroyed part of my sledge to construct oars, and
6855: by these means was enabled, with infinite fatigue, to move my ice raft in
6856: the direction of your ship. I had determined, if you were going southwards,
6857: still to trust myself to the mercy of the seas rather than abandon my
6858: purpose. I hoped to induce you to grant me a boat with which I could pursue
6859: my enemy. But your direction was northwards. You took me on board when my
6860: vigour was exhausted, and I should soon have sunk under my multiplied
6861: hardships into a death which I still dread, for my task is unfulfilled.
6862: 
6863: Oh! When will my guiding spirit, in conducting me to the dæmon, allow
6864: me the rest I so much desire; or must I die, and he yet live? If I do,
6865: swear to me, Walton, that he shall not escape, that you will seek him
6866: and satisfy my vengeance in his death. And do I dare to ask of you to
6867: undertake my pilgrimage, to endure the hardships that I have undergone?
6868: No; I am not so selfish. Yet, when I am dead, if he should appear, if
6869: the ministers of vengeance should conduct him to you, swear that he
6870: shall not live—swear that he shall not triumph over my accumulated
6871: woes and survive to add to the list of his dark crimes. He is eloquent
6872: and persuasive, and once his words had even power over my heart; but
6873: trust him not. His soul is as hellish as his form, full of treachery
6874: and fiend-like malice. Hear him not; call on the names of William,
6875: Justine, Clerval, Elizabeth, my father, and of the wretched Victor, and
6876: thrust your sword into his heart. I will hover near and direct the
6877: steel aright.
6878: 
6879: Walton, _in continuation._
6880: 
6881: 
6882: August 26th, 17—.
6883: 
6884: 
6885: You have read this strange and terrific story, Margaret; and do you not
6886: feel your blood congeal with horror, like that which even now curdles
6887: mine? Sometimes, seized with sudden agony, he could not continue his
6888: tale; at others, his voice broken, yet piercing, uttered with
6889: difficulty the words so replete with anguish. His fine and lovely eyes
6890: were now lighted up with indignation, now subdued to downcast sorrow
6891: and quenched in infinite wretchedness. Sometimes he commanded his
6892: countenance and tones and related the most horrible incidents with a
6893: tranquil voice, suppressing every mark of agitation; then, like a
6894: volcano bursting forth, his face would suddenly change to an expression
6895: of the wildest rage as he shrieked out imprecations on his persecutor.
6896: 
6897: His tale is connected and told with an appearance of the simplest truth,
6898: yet I own to you that the letters of Felix and Safie, which he showed me,
6899: and the apparition of the monster seen from our ship, brought to me a
6900: greater conviction of the truth of his narrative than his asseverations,
6901: however earnest and connected. Such a monster has, then, really existence!
6902: I cannot doubt it, yet I am lost in surprise and admiration. Sometimes I
6903: endeavoured to gain from Frankenstein the particulars of his
6904: creature’s formation, but on this point he was impenetrable.
6905: 
6906: “Are you mad, my friend?” said he. “Or whither does your
6907: senseless curiosity lead you? Would you also create for yourself and the
6908: world a demoniacal enemy? Peace, peace! Learn my miseries and do not seek
6909: to increase your own.”
6910: 
6911: Frankenstein discovered that I made notes concerning his history; he asked
6912: to see them and then himself corrected and augmented them in many places,
6913: but principally in giving the life and spirit to the conversations he held
6914: with his enemy. “Since you have preserved my narration,” said
6915: he, “I would not that a mutilated one should go down to
6916: posterity.”
6917: 
6918: Thus has a week passed away, while I have listened to the strangest
6919: tale that ever imagination formed. My thoughts and every feeling of my
6920: soul have been drunk up by the interest for my guest which this tale
6921: and his own elevated and gentle manners have created. I wish to soothe
6922: him, yet can I counsel one so infinitely miserable, so destitute of
6923: every hope of consolation, to live? Oh, no! The only joy that he can
6924: now know will be when he composes his shattered spirit to peace and
6925: death. Yet he enjoys one comfort, the offspring of solitude and
6926: delirium; he believes that when in dreams he holds converse with his
6927: friends and derives from that communion consolation for his miseries or
6928: excitements to his vengeance, that they are not the creations of his
6929: fancy, but the beings themselves who visit him from the regions of a
6930: remote world. This faith gives a solemnity to his reveries that render
6931: them to me almost as imposing and interesting as truth.
6932: 
6933: Our conversations are not always confined to his own history and
6934: misfortunes. On every point of general literature he displays
6935: unbounded knowledge and a quick and piercing apprehension. His
6936: eloquence is forcible and touching; nor can I hear him, when he relates
6937: a pathetic incident or endeavours to move the passions of pity or love,
6938: without tears. What a glorious creature must he have been in the days
6939: of his prosperity, when he is thus noble and godlike in ruin! He seems
6940: to feel his own worth and the greatness of his fall.
6941: 
6942: “When younger,” said he, “I believed myself destined for
6943: some great enterprise. My feelings are profound, but I possessed a coolness
6944: of judgment that fitted me for illustrious achievements. This sentiment of
6945: the worth of my nature supported me when others would have been oppressed,
6946: for I deemed it criminal to throw away in useless grief those talents that
6947: might be useful to my fellow creatures. When I reflected on the work I had
6948: completed, no less a one than the creation of a sensitive and rational
6949: animal, I could not rank myself with the herd of common projectors. But
6950: this thought, which supported me in the commencement of my career, now
6951: serves only to plunge me lower in the dust. All my speculations and hopes
6952: are as nothing, and like the archangel who aspired to omnipotence, I am
6953: chained in an eternal hell. My imagination was vivid, yet my powers of
6954: analysis and application were intense; by the union of these qualities I
6955: conceived the idea and executed the creation of a man. Even now I cannot
6956: recollect without passion my reveries while the work was incomplete. I trod
6957: heaven in my thoughts, now exulting in my powers, now burning with the idea
6958: of their effects. From my infancy I was imbued with high hopes and a lofty
6959: ambition; but how am I sunk! Oh! My friend, if you had known me as I once
6960: was, you would not recognise me in this state of degradation. Despondency
6961: rarely visited my heart; a high destiny seemed to bear me on, until I fell,
6962: never, never again to rise.”
6963: 
6964: Must I then lose this admirable being? I have longed for a friend; I have
6965: sought one who would sympathise with and love me. Behold, on these desert
6966: seas I have found such a one, but I fear I have gained him only to know his
6967: value and lose him. I would reconcile him to life, but he repulses the idea.
6968: 
6969: “I thank you, Walton,” he said, “for your kind intentions towards so
6970: miserable a wretch; but when you speak of new ties and fresh
6971: affections, think you that any can replace those who are gone? Can any
6972: man be to me as Clerval was, or any woman another Elizabeth? Even
6973: where the affections are not strongly moved by any superior excellence,
6974: the companions of our childhood always possess a certain power over our
6975: minds which hardly any later friend can obtain. They know our
6976: infantine dispositions, which, however they may be afterwards modified,
6977: are never eradicated; and they can judge of our actions with more
6978: certain conclusions as to the integrity of our motives. A sister or a
6979: brother can never, unless indeed such symptoms have been shown early,
6980: suspect the other of fraud or false dealing, when another friend,
6981: however strongly he may be attached, may, in spite of himself, be
6982: contemplated with suspicion. But I enjoyed friends, dear not only
6983: through habit and association, but from their own merits; and wherever
6984: I am, the soothing voice of my Elizabeth and the conversation of
6985: Clerval will be ever whispered in my ear. They are dead, and but one
6986: feeling in such a solitude can persuade me to preserve my life. If I
6987: were engaged in any high undertaking or design, fraught with extensive
6988: utility to my fellow creatures, then could I live to fulfil it. But
6989: such is not my destiny; I must pursue and destroy the being to whom I
6990: gave existence; then my lot on earth will be fulfilled and I may die.”
6991: 
6992: My beloved Sister,
6993: 
6994: September 2d.
6995: 
6996: 
6997: I write to you, encompassed by peril and ignorant whether I am ever
6998: doomed to see again dear England and the dearer friends that inhabit
6999: it. I am surrounded by mountains of ice which admit of no escape and
7000: threaten every moment to crush my vessel. The brave fellows whom I
7001: have persuaded to be my companions look towards me for aid, but I have
7002: none to bestow. There is something terribly appalling in our
7003: situation, yet my courage and hopes do not desert me. Yet it is
7004: terrible to reflect that the lives of all these men are endangered
7005: through me. If we are lost, my mad schemes are the cause.
7006: 
7007: And what, Margaret, will be the state of your mind? You will not hear of my
7008: destruction, and you will anxiously await my return. Years will pass, and
7009: you will have visitings of despair and yet be tortured by hope. Oh! My
7010: beloved sister, the sickening failing of your heart-felt expectations is,
7011: in prospect, more terrible to me than my own death. But you have a husband
7012: and lovely children; you may be happy. Heaven bless you and make you so!
7013: 
7014: My unfortunate guest regards me with the tenderest compassion. He
7015: endeavours to fill me with hope and talks as if life were a possession
7016: which he valued. He reminds me how often the same accidents have
7017: happened to other navigators who have attempted this sea, and in spite
7018: of myself, he fills me with cheerful auguries. Even the sailors feel
7019: the power of his eloquence; when he speaks, they no longer despair; he
7020: rouses their energies, and while they hear his voice they believe these
7021: vast mountains of ice are mole-hills which will vanish before the
7022: resolutions of man. These feelings are transitory; each day of
7023: expectation delayed fills them with fear, and I almost dread a mutiny
7024: caused by this despair.
7025: 
7026: September 5th.
7027: 
7028: 
7029: A scene has just passed of such uncommon interest that, although it is
7030: highly probable that these papers may never reach you, yet I cannot
7031: forbear recording it.
7032: 
7033: We are still surrounded by mountains of ice, still in imminent danger
7034: of being crushed in their conflict. The cold is excessive, and many of
7035: my unfortunate comrades have already found a grave amidst this scene of
7036: desolation. Frankenstein has daily declined in health; a feverish fire
7037: still glimmers in his eyes, but he is exhausted, and when suddenly
7038: roused to any exertion, he speedily sinks again into apparent
7039: lifelessness.
7040: 
7041: I mentioned in my last letter the fears I entertained of a mutiny.
7042: This morning, as I sat watching the wan countenance of my friend—his
7043: eyes half closed and his limbs hanging listlessly—I was roused by half
7044: a dozen of the sailors, who demanded admission into the cabin. They
7045: entered, and their leader addressed me. He told me that he and his
7046: companions had been chosen by the other sailors to come in deputation
7047: to me to make me a requisition which, in justice, I could not refuse.
7048: We were immured in ice and should probably never escape, but they
7049: feared that if, as was possible, the ice should dissipate and a free
7050: passage be opened, I should be rash enough to continue my voyage and
7051: lead them into fresh dangers, after they might happily have surmounted
7052: this. They insisted, therefore, that I should engage with a solemn
7053: promise that if the vessel should be freed I would instantly direct my
7054: course southwards.
7055: 
7056: This speech troubled me. I had not despaired, nor had I yet conceived
7057: the idea of returning if set free. Yet could I, in justice, or even in
7058: possibility, refuse this demand? I hesitated before I answered, when
7059: Frankenstein, who had at first been silent, and indeed appeared hardly
7060: to have force enough to attend, now roused himself; his eyes sparkled,
7061: and his cheeks flushed with momentary vigour. Turning towards the men,
7062: he said,
7063: 
7064: “What do you mean? What do you demand of your captain? Are you, then,
7065: so easily turned from your design? Did you not call this a glorious
7066: expedition? “And wherefore was it glorious? Not because the way was
7067: smooth and placid as a southern sea, but because it was full of dangers and
7068: terror, because at every new incident your fortitude was to be called forth
7069: and your courage exhibited, because danger and death surrounded it, and
7070: these you were to brave and overcome. For this was it a glorious, for this
7071: was it an honourable undertaking. You were hereafter to be hailed as the
7072: benefactors of your species, your names adored as belonging to brave men
7073: who encountered death for honour and the benefit of mankind. And now,
7074: behold, with the first imagination of danger, or, if you will, the first
7075: mighty and terrific trial of your courage, you shrink away and are content
7076: to be handed down as men who had not strength enough to endure cold and
7077: peril; and so, poor souls, they were chilly and returned to their warm
7078: firesides. Why, that requires not this preparation; ye need not have come
7079: thus far and dragged your captain to the shame of a defeat merely to prove
7080: yourselves cowards. Oh! Be men, or be more than men. Be steady to your
7081: purposes and firm as a rock. This ice is not made of such stuff as your
7082: hearts may be; it is mutable and cannot withstand you if you say that it
7083: shall not. Do not return to your families with the stigma of disgrace
7084: marked on your brows. Return as heroes who have fought and conquered and
7085: who know not what it is to turn their backs on the foe.”
7086: 
7087: He spoke this with a voice so modulated to the different feelings expressed
7088: in his speech, with an eye so full of lofty design and heroism, that can
7089: you wonder that these men were moved? They looked at one another and were
7090: unable to reply. I spoke; I told them to retire and consider of what had
7091: been said, that I would not lead them farther north if they strenuously
7092: desired the contrary, but that I hoped that, with reflection, their courage
7093: would return.
7094: 
7095: They retired and I turned towards my friend, but he was sunk in languor and
7096: almost deprived of life.
7097: 
7098: How all this will terminate, I know not, but I had rather die than
7099: return shamefully, my purpose unfulfilled. Yet I fear such will be my
7100: fate; the men, unsupported by ideas of glory and honour, can never
7101: willingly continue to endure their present hardships.
7102: 
7103: September 7th.
7104: 
7105: 
7106: The die is cast; I have consented to return if we are not destroyed.
7107: Thus are my hopes blasted by cowardice and indecision; I come back
7108: ignorant and disappointed. It requires more philosophy than I possess
7109: to bear this injustice with patience.
7110: 
7111: September 12th.
7112: 
7113: 
7114: It is past; I am returning to England. I have lost my hopes of utility
7115: and glory; I have lost my friend. But I will endeavour to detail these
7116: bitter circumstances to you, my dear sister; and while I am wafted
7117: towards England and towards you, I will not despond.
7118: 
7119: September 9th, the ice began to move, and roarings like thunder were heard
7120: at a distance as the islands split and cracked in every direction. We were
7121: in the most imminent peril, but as we could only remain passive, my chief
7122: attention was occupied by my unfortunate guest whose illness increased in
7123: such a degree that he was entirely confined to his bed. The ice cracked
7124: behind us and was driven with force towards the north; a breeze sprang from
7125: the west, and on the 11th the passage towards the south became perfectly
7126: free. When the sailors saw this and that their return to their native
7127: country was apparently assured, a shout of tumultuous joy broke from them,
7128: loud and long-continued. Frankenstein, who was dozing, awoke and asked the
7129: cause of the tumult. “They shout,” I said, “because they
7130: will soon return to England.”
7131: 
7132: “Do you, then, really return?”
7133: 
7134: “Alas! Yes; I cannot withstand their demands. I cannot lead them
7135: unwillingly to danger, and I must return.”
7136: 
7137: “Do so, if you will; but I will not. You may give up your purpose, but
7138: mine is assigned to me by Heaven, and I dare not. I am weak, but
7139: surely the spirits who assist my vengeance will endow me with
7140: sufficient strength.” Saying this, he endeavoured to spring from the
7141: bed, but the exertion was too great for him; he fell back and fainted.
7142: 
7143: It was long before he was restored, and I often thought that life was
7144: entirely extinct. At length he opened his eyes; he breathed with
7145: difficulty and was unable to speak. The surgeon gave him a composing
7146: draught and ordered us to leave him undisturbed. In the meantime he
7147: told me that my friend had certainly not many hours to live.
7148: 
7149: His sentence was pronounced, and I could only grieve and be patient. I sat
7150: by his bed, watching him; his eyes were closed, and I thought he slept; but
7151: presently he called to me in a feeble voice, and bidding me come near,
7152: said, “Alas! The strength I relied on is gone; I feel that I shall
7153: soon die, and he, my enemy and persecutor, may still be in being. Think
7154: not, Walton, that in the last moments of my existence I feel that burning
7155: hatred and ardent desire of revenge I once expressed; but I feel myself
7156: justified in desiring the death of my adversary. During these last days I
7157: have been occupied in examining my past conduct; nor do I find it blamable.
7158: In a fit of enthusiastic madness I created a rational creature and was
7159: bound towards him to assure, as far as was in my power, his happiness and
7160: well-being. This was my duty, but there was another still paramount to
7161: that. My duties towards the beings of my own species had greater claims to
7162: my attention because they included a greater proportion of happiness or
7163: misery. Urged by this view, I refused, and I did right in refusing, to
7164: create a companion for the first creature. He showed unparalleled malignity
7165: and selfishness in evil; he destroyed my friends; he devoted to destruction
7166: beings who possessed exquisite sensations, happiness, and wisdom; nor do I
7167: know where this thirst for vengeance may end. Miserable himself that he may
7168: render no other wretched, he ought to die. The task of his destruction was
7169: mine, but I have failed. When actuated by selfish and vicious motives, I
7170: asked you to undertake my unfinished work, and I renew this request now,
7171: when I am only induced by reason and virtue.
7172: 
7173: “Yet I cannot ask you to renounce your country and friends to fulfil
7174: this task; and now that you are returning to England, you will have
7175: little chance of meeting with him. But the consideration of these
7176: points, and the well balancing of what you may esteem your duties, I
7177: leave to you; my judgment and ideas are already disturbed by the near
7178: approach of death. I dare not ask you to do what I think right, for I
7179: may still be misled by passion.
7180: 
7181: “That he should live to be an instrument of mischief disturbs me; in
7182: other respects, this hour, when I momentarily expect my release, is the
7183: only happy one which I have enjoyed for several years. The forms of
7184: the beloved dead flit before me, and I hasten to their arms. Farewell,
7185: Walton! Seek happiness in tranquillity and avoid ambition, even if it
7186: be only the apparently innocent one of distinguishing yourself in
7187: science and discoveries. Yet why do I say this? I have myself been
7188: blasted in these hopes, yet another may succeed.”
7189: 
7190: His voice became fainter as he spoke, and at length, exhausted by his
7191: effort, he sank into silence. About half an hour afterwards he
7192: attempted again to speak but was unable; he pressed my hand feebly, and
7193: his eyes closed for ever, while the irradiation of a gentle smile passed
7194: away from his lips.
7195: 
7196: Margaret, what comment can I make on the untimely extinction of this
7197: glorious spirit? What can I say that will enable you to understand the
7198: depth of my sorrow? All that I should express would be inadequate and
7199: feeble. My tears flow; my mind is overshadowed by a cloud of
7200: disappointment. But I journey towards England, and I may there find
7201: consolation.
7202: 
7203: I am interrupted. What do these sounds portend? It is midnight; the
7204: breeze blows fairly, and the watch on deck scarcely stir. Again there
7205: is a sound as of a human voice, but hoarser; it comes from the cabin
7206: where the remains of Frankenstein still lie. I must arise and examine.
7207: Good night, my sister.
7208: 
7209: Great God! what a scene has just taken place! I am yet dizzy with the
7210: remembrance of it. I hardly know whether I shall have the power to detail
7211: it; yet the tale which I have recorded would be incomplete without this
7212: final and wonderful catastrophe.
7213: 
7214: I entered the cabin where lay the remains of my ill-fated and admirable
7215: friend. Over him hung a form which I cannot find words to
7216: describe—gigantic in stature, yet uncouth and distorted in its
7217: proportions. As he hung over the coffin, his face was concealed by long
7218: locks of ragged hair; but one vast hand was extended, in colour and
7219: apparent texture like that of a mummy. When he heard the sound of my
7220: approach, he ceased to utter exclamations of grief and horror and sprung
7221: towards the window. Never did I behold a vision so horrible as his face, of
7222: such loathsome yet appalling hideousness. I shut my eyes involuntarily and
7223: endeavoured to recollect what were my duties with regard to this destroyer.
7224: I called on him to stay.
7225: 
7226: He paused, looking on me with wonder, and again turning towards the
7227: lifeless form of his creator, he seemed to forget my presence, and
7228: every feature and gesture seemed instigated by the wildest rage of some
7229: uncontrollable passion.
7230: 
7231: “That is also my victim!” he exclaimed. “In his murder my
7232: crimes are consummated; the miserable series of my being is wound to its
7233: close! Oh, Frankenstein! Generous and self-devoted being! What does it
7234: avail that I now ask thee to pardon me? I, who irretrievably destroyed thee
7235: by destroying all thou lovedst. Alas! He is cold, he cannot answer
7236: me.”
7237: 
7238: His voice seemed suffocated, and my first impulses, which had suggested to
7239: me the duty of obeying the dying request of my friend in destroying his
7240: enemy, were now suspended by a mixture of curiosity and compassion. I
7241: approached this tremendous being; I dared not again raise my eyes to his
7242: face, there was something so scaring and unearthly in his ugliness. I
7243: attempted to speak, but the words died away on my lips. The monster
7244: continued to utter wild and incoherent self-reproaches. At length I
7245: gathered resolution to address him in a pause of the tempest of his passion.
7246: 
7247: “Your repentance,” I said, “is now superfluous. If you
7248: had listened to the voice of conscience and heeded the stings of remorse
7249: before you had urged your diabolical vengeance to this extremity,
7250: Frankenstein would yet have lived.”
7251: 
7252: “And do you dream?” said the dæmon. “Do you think that I was then
7253: dead to agony and remorse? He,” he continued, pointing to the corpse,
7254: “he suffered not in the consummation of the deed. Oh! Not the
7255: ten-thousandth portion of the anguish that was mine during the
7256: lingering detail of its execution. A frightful selfishness hurried me
7257: on, while my heart was poisoned with remorse. Think you that the
7258: groans of Clerval were music to my ears? My heart was fashioned to be
7259: susceptible of love and sympathy, and when wrenched by misery to vice
7260: and hatred, it did not endure the violence of the change without
7261: torture such as you cannot even imagine.
7262: 
7263: “After the murder of Clerval I returned to Switzerland, heart-broken
7264: and overcome. I pitied Frankenstein; my pity amounted to horror; I
7265: abhorred myself. But when I discovered that he, the author at once of
7266: my existence and of its unspeakable torments, dared to hope for
7267: happiness, that while he accumulated wretchedness and despair upon me
7268: he sought his own enjoyment in feelings and passions from the
7269: indulgence of which I was for ever barred, then impotent envy and bitter
7270: indignation filled me with an insatiable thirst for vengeance. I
7271: recollected my threat and resolved that it should be accomplished. I
7272: knew that I was preparing for myself a deadly torture, but I was the
7273: slave, not the master, of an impulse which I detested yet could not
7274: disobey. Yet when she died! Nay, then I was not miserable. I had
7275: cast off all feeling, subdued all anguish, to riot in the excess of my
7276: despair. Evil thenceforth became my good. Urged thus far, I had no
7277: choice but to adapt my nature to an element which I had willingly
7278: chosen. The completion of my demoniacal design became an insatiable
7279: passion. And now it is ended; there is my last victim!”
7280: 
7281: I was at first touched by the expressions of his misery; yet, when I called
7282: to mind what Frankenstein had said of his powers of eloquence and
7283: persuasion, and when I again cast my eyes on the lifeless form of my
7284: friend, indignation was rekindled within me. “Wretch!” I said.
7285: “It is well that you come here to whine over the desolation that you
7286: have made. You throw a torch into a pile of buildings, and when they are
7287: consumed, you sit among the ruins and lament the fall. Hypocritical fiend!
7288: If he whom you mourn still lived, still would he be the object, again would
7289: he become the prey, of your accursed vengeance. It is not pity that you
7290: feel; you lament only because the victim of your malignity is withdrawn
7291: from your power.”
7292: 
7293: “Oh, it is not thus—not thus,” interrupted the being.
7294: “Yet such must be the impression conveyed to you by what appears to
7295: be the purport of my actions. Yet I seek not a fellow feeling in my misery.
7296: No sympathy may I ever find. When I first sought it, it was the love of
7297: virtue, the feelings of happiness and affection with which my whole being
7298: overflowed, that I wished to be participated. But now that virtue has
7299: become to me a shadow, and that happiness and affection are turned into
7300: bitter and loathing despair, in what should I seek for sympathy? I am
7301: content to suffer alone while my sufferings shall endure; when I die, I am
7302: well satisfied that abhorrence and opprobrium should load my memory. Once
7303: my fancy was soothed with dreams of virtue, of fame, and of enjoyment. Once
7304: I falsely hoped to meet with beings who, pardoning my outward form, would
7305: love me for the excellent qualities which I was capable of unfolding. I was
7306: nourished with high thoughts of honour and devotion. But now crime has
7307: degraded me beneath the meanest animal. No guilt, no mischief, no
7308: malignity, no misery, can be found comparable to mine. When I run over the
7309: frightful catalogue of my sins, I cannot believe that I am the same
7310: creature whose thoughts were once filled with sublime and transcendent
7311: visions of the beauty and the majesty of goodness. But it is even so; the
7312: fallen angel becomes a malignant devil. Yet even that enemy of God and man
7313: had friends and associates in his desolation; I am alone.
7314: 
7315: “You, who call Frankenstein your friend, seem to have a knowledge of my
7316: crimes and his misfortunes. But in the detail which he gave you of them
7317: he could not sum up the hours and months of misery which I endured
7318: wasting in impotent passions. For while I destroyed his hopes, I did
7319: not satisfy my own desires. They were for ever ardent and craving; still
7320: I desired love and fellowship, and I was still spurned. Was there no
7321: injustice in this? Am I to be thought the only criminal, when all
7322: humankind sinned against me? Why do you not hate Felix, who drove his
7323: friend from his door with contumely? Why do you not execrate the rustic
7324: who sought to destroy the saviour of his child? Nay, these are virtuous
7325: and immaculate beings! I, the miserable and the abandoned, am an
7326: abortion, to be spurned at, and kicked, and trampled on. Even now my
7327: blood boils at the recollection of this injustice.
7328: 
7329: “But it is true that I am a wretch. I have murdered the lovely and
7330: the helpless; I have strangled the innocent as they slept and grasped to
7331: death his throat who never injured me or any other living thing. I have
7332: devoted my creator, the select specimen of all that is worthy of love and
7333: admiration among men, to misery; I have pursued him even to that
7334: irremediable ruin. There he lies, white and cold in death. You hate me, but
7335: your abhorrence cannot equal that with which I regard myself. I look on the
7336: hands which executed the deed; I think on the heart in which the
7337: imagination of it was conceived and long for the moment when these hands
7338: will meet my eyes, when that imagination will haunt my thoughts no more.
7339: 
7340: “Fear not that I shall be the instrument of future mischief. My work
7341: is nearly complete. Neither yours nor any man’s death is needed to
7342: consummate the series of my being and accomplish that which must be done,
7343: but it requires my own. Do not think that I shall be slow to perform this
7344: sacrifice. I shall quit your vessel on the ice raft which brought me
7345: thither and shall seek the most northern extremity of the globe; I shall
7346: collect my funeral pile and consume to ashes this miserable frame, that its
7347: remains may afford no light to any curious and unhallowed wretch who would
7348: create such another as I have been. I shall die. I shall no longer feel the
7349: agonies which now consume me or be the prey of feelings unsatisfied, yet
7350: unquenched. He is dead who called me into being; and when I shall be no
7351: more, the very remembrance of us both will speedily vanish. I shall no
7352: longer see the sun or stars or feel the winds play on my cheeks. Light,
7353: feeling, and sense will pass away; and in this condition must I find my
7354: happiness. Some years ago, when the images which this world affords first
7355: opened upon me, when I felt the cheering warmth of summer and heard the
7356: rustling of the leaves and the warbling of the birds, and these were all to
7357: me, I should have wept to die; now it is my only consolation. Polluted by
7358: crimes and torn by the bitterest remorse, where can I find rest but in
7359: death?
7360: 
7361: “Farewell! I leave you, and in you the last of humankind whom these
7362: eyes will ever behold. Farewell, Frankenstein! If thou wert yet alive
7363: and yet cherished a desire of revenge against me, it would be better
7364: satiated in my life than in my destruction. But it was not so; thou
7365: didst seek my extinction, that I might not cause greater wretchedness;
7366: and if yet, in some mode unknown to me, thou hadst not ceased to think
7367: and feel, thou wouldst not desire against me a vengeance greater than
7368: that which I feel. Blasted as thou wert, my agony was still superior to
7369: thine, for the bitter sting of remorse will not cease to rankle in my
7370: wounds until death shall close them for ever.
7371: 
7372: “But soon,” he cried with sad and solemn enthusiasm, “I
7373: shall die, and what I now feel be no longer felt. Soon these burning
7374: miseries will be extinct. I shall ascend my funeral pile triumphantly and
7375: exult in the agony of the torturing flames. The light of that conflagration
7376: will fade away; my ashes will be swept into the sea by the winds. My spirit
7377: will sleep in peace, or if it thinks, it will not surely think thus.
7378: Farewell.”
7379: 
7380: He sprang from the cabin-window as he said this, upon the ice raft
7381: which lay close to the vessel. He was soon borne away by the waves and
7382: lost in darkness and distance.
7383: 
7384: 
7385: 
7386:
